# Chapter 1: Root Density

Reed had been talking for eleven minutes. He knew this because Marsh's field watch was visible from where he stood, and because nobody had interrupted him yet, which meant either his data was so compelling it had silenced five opinionated scientists, or they had all stopped listening around minute three.

He pushed his glasses up his nose and pointed at the soil cross-section pinned to the foldout table. "The point, the actual point, is that this density is not a statistical outlier. This is seventeen standard deviations. Seventeen. The literature does not have a category for seventeen standard deviations because at that point you are no longer describing an anomaly, you are describing a failure of the model itself, which means the model, our model, the model I have been working with for three years, is either fundamentally wrong or, and this is where it gets, wait, wait, wait, hold on."

He shuffled through three sheets of graph paper, found the one he wanted underneath a soil sample bag, and held it up. The ink had smudged in the humidity. Everything smudged in the humidity. Reed's glasses were smudged. His notes were smudged. The distinction between his left sock and his right sock had smudged into a shared state of damp misery weeks ago.

"This quadrant," he said. "Root density three times anything I have ever measured. Three times anything anyone has ever measured. And the soil temperature is elevated by two degrees, which should not be possible at this depth without a geothermal source, and there is no geothermal source, I checked, I checked twice, and the mineral profile is."

"Roots are dense in the Amazon, Reed." Vale did not look up from the satellite phone. He was standing at the edge of the tarp shade, one finger pressed to his free ear, arguing with someone named Daniela about supply shipments. "That is what makes it the Amazon."

"That is not what makes it the Amazon. Several things make it the Amazon. Precipitation patterns, equatorial positioning, the."

"Right." Vale covered the phone's mouthpiece. "So you've found dense roots in a dense forest. Should I alert the Nobel committee, or would you prefer to wait until the data is less smudged?"

Reed stared at Vale. Vale returned to Daniela. There was a specific quality to Vale's dismissals that Reed had never learned to counter, a way of reducing an argument to absurdity without ever engaging with its substance. It was, objectively, a rhetorical gift. Reed hated it.

"The density is not the point," Reed said, turning to the others. "The density is the symptom. The chemistry is the point. The mineral uptake ratios in these root samples do not correspond to any known tropical hardwood, any known tropical softwood, or, actually, any known plant. I ran the field kit analysis four times."

Hart was crouched beside the water filtration unit on the far side of the camp, her field kit open beside her. She had removed the filter housing and was examining the interior with a penlight. "Your field kit," she said, without looking up. "The one with the reagents that expired in February."

"They did not expire. The expiration is conservative. There is a paper from 2019 that demonstrates reagent viability well beyond."

"There is fungal contamination in the water filter."

"I am aware that the water filter."

"I am not sure you are aware. I am not sure anyone is aware." Hart turned the filter housing toward the light. Something dark and faintly green clung to the interior mesh. "This is not normal biofilm. The growth pattern is filamentous, the color is wrong, and it appeared in the last forty-eight hours, which suggests a contamination rate that I would describe as clinically concerning."

"Hart, I am trying to show you root density data that defies three centuries of botanical."

"Spores, Reed. Unidentified fungal spores in our drinking water. I want you to hear the words 'unidentified fungal spores' and then tell me which problem is more urgent."

Reed opened his mouth. Closed it. Opened it again. "The roots."

"Wrong."

Marsh was sitting on a camp stool several meters from the argument, her field notebook open across her knee. She was sketching something, her pencil moving in short, careful strokes. She had not spoken. When Marsh stayed quiet for extended periods it usually meant she was either bored or about to say something that made everyone else feel stupid.

She was also tilting her head. Slightly. Repeatedly. As if trying to catch a sound.

Quill was sitting on a fallen log near the treeline, his face upturned toward the canopy, his hands resting on his knees, palms up. His sunburned nose was peeling. He had been staring at the upper branches for the better part of twenty minutes, and Reed could not tell if he was thinking about primate locomotion or experiencing some private philosophical event.

"Quill."

Quill lowered his gaze slowly, as if returning from a significant distance. "Hmm?"

"Did you look at the root density data I left on your cot this morning?"

"I did, yes."

"And?"

"I found it very interesting."

Reed waited. That was, apparently, the complete response. "Interesting how?"

Quill's hands began to move, which meant he was about to say something that would take a while. "I suppose what struck me, and I might be coming at this from entirely the wrong angle, I'm not a soil chemist, but what struck me was the coordination. Not the density itself but the, how do I put this, the organized quality of it. The roots aren't just dense, they're, I mean, this might be a silly question, but is there any precedent for root systems that appear to be structurally deliberate? As if they were built rather than grown?"

Reed pushed his glasses up. "That is not a silly question."

"I said it might be."

"It is not. That is exactly what the data suggests, which is what I have been trying to explain for," he glanced at Marsh's watch, "thirteen minutes. The branching pattern is too regular. The spacing is too even. If you showed me these cross-sections without context I would tell you they were engineered."

"Engineered by what?" Vale had finished with Daniela or Daniela had finished with Vale. He pocketed the satellite phone and walked toward the table, arms folded. "Trees don't engineer, Reed. Trees grow. Some grow interestingly. Then a postdoc writes a paper and everyone calms down."

"I am not a postdoc."

"No. You have a postdoc's enthusiasm and a professor's salary. The combination concerns me."

"The branching is too regular to be growth."

"So something underground is organizing tree roots. What is your hypothesis? Termites with an architecture degree? A very ambitious mycorrhizal network?"

"I don't have a hypothesis. I have data."

"Data without a hypothesis is a spreadsheet, Reed. What are you proposing?"

Reed removed his glasses and cleaned them on his shirt, which made them worse. "I am proposing that the root systems in quadrant seven are behaving in a way that is not consistent with any known biological process, and that the soil chemistry suggests an unidentified substrate at depth, and that we should core deeper."

"We should core deeper." Vale repeated the phrase the way a man might repeat a restaurant's daily special when he already knew it would give him indigestion. "How deep?"

"Twelve meters. Fifteen. As deep as we can."

"With what equipment?"

"With the coring rig."

"The coring rig rated for eight meters."

"We could modify it."

"So. Your plan. Is to modify our equipment to investigate anomalous roots that are probably anomalous because your reagents expired in February. And this will take, what, a week? Two weeks? While Hart's fungal water filter slowly poisons the camp."

Hart, still crouched over the filter, raised one hand in acknowledgment without turning around. "Thank you. Someone heard me."

"I heard you," Vale said. "You're the only one talking about something that might actually kill us."

Reed turned to Marsh. This was, he knew, a tactical decision. Marsh did not join arguments she considered premature, but she also did not ignore evidence. If the data was real, Marsh would say so. She would say it once, and it would settle things, and Reed could stop shouting into the collective indifference of his colleagues.

"Marsh."

Marsh stopped sketching. She looked up, and her expression was the one Reed thought of as her field face. Neutral, attentive, slightly amused by something only she had noticed.

"I pulled one of the roots near the secondary transect yesterday," she said. "Not for your study. For mine. I wanted to check whether the primates were stripping bark at the root level."

"And?"

"The root was warm."

Silence. Not a dramatic silence. Just the ordinary absence of words that happens when five people are recalibrating at different speeds. The water filter dripped. Something shrieked in the canopy, distant and uninterested in root temperature.

"Warm how?" Reed said.

"Like it had been sitting in the sun. Except it was thirty centimeters underground."

"That supports the elevated soil temperature data."

"It does." Marsh returned to her sketch. "It also had a smell I didn't recognize. Sweet, but mineral. Like hot stone after rain, but coming from a living root."

Reed looked at Vale. Vale's expression had not changed, but his arms had uncrossed, which in Vale's body language was the equivalent of a standing ovation.

"Could be decomposition gases," Hart offered from the filter station. "Anaerobic decay at depth. Would explain the temperature and the smell."

"Doesn't explain the regularity of the branching," Reed said.

"Doesn't need to. Two problems can have two causes."

"I wonder," Quill said, and then stopped. His hands moved. He started again. "This is probably irrelevant, but has anyone else noticed a sound? A low sound. From the ground."

Reed turned. "What kind of sound?"

"I'm not sure. It might be tinnitus. I've been worried about tinnitus since Bogota. But it has a, I suppose you'd call it a tonal quality? It comes and goes. I notice it most when the camp is quiet, which isn't often."

"I hear it too."

Everyone looked at Marsh. She had stopped sketching again, her pencil resting on the notebook page, her head tilted at that slight angle Reed had noticed earlier.

"It's very faint," she said. "Low register. Constant, but it shifts pitch. Like a harmonic."

"Tinnitus," Reed said. "Two cases of tinnitus in the same camp isn't unusual. Humidity, pressure changes, altitude adjustment."

"We're at two hundred meters above sea level," Hart said. "There is no altitude to adjust to."

"Then humidity."

"Tinnitus is not contagious, Reed."

"I didn't say it was contagious, I said the environmental conditions."

"Could it be a gas pocket?" Hart stood up from the filter, wiping her hands on her trousers. "Pressurized gas escaping through fissures could produce a tonal sound. Methane, hydrogen sulfide. Could also explain the soil temperature. Could also explain why my filter is growing something colonial."

"Could it be biological?" Quill asked.

The question sat there. It was one of Quill's questions, the kind that sounded like idle curiosity and landed somewhere else entirely. Reed wanted to dismiss it, and found that he could not immediately construct a dismissal that did not sound defensive.

"Biological how?" Vale said.

"I don't know. I don't know what I mean, exactly. I suppose I'm asking whether a sound from underground could be produced by something alive. Something that isn't a gas pocket or a geological feature. Something organized."

"That is a large supposition," Vale said. "Based on two people who might have tinnitus."

"Three," Hart said. Everyone looked at her. She shrugged. "I assumed it was the filter vibrating. Now I'm less sure."

Reed's glasses had slid down his nose again. He pushed them up with both hands, which he only did when his brain was running faster than his ability to manage his own face.

He started to say something. Then he stopped, because he was not sure what sentence to build out of the components available. The data was pointing somewhere he could not name.

"I want to core deeper," he said, finally.

"You want to core deeper." Vale again. "Of course you do."

"The data justifies it."

"The data justifies curiosity. Curiosity does not justify two weeks of equipment modification in a camp with compromised water filtration." Vale turned to Hart. "Can you fix the filter?"

"I am fixing the filter. I have been fixing the filter for the last hour. Nobody noticed because root density is apparently more exciting than drinking water that won't give us dysentery."

"I noticed," Vale said.

"You noticed just now."

"I noticed at the strategically appropriate time."

Hart looked at Vale for a long moment, then returned to the filter housing. "I'll have it running by tonight. The contamination is localized. I'll need to replace the secondary membrane, but I brought spares because I am the only person on this team who thinks about what happens when things break."

The argument ran out of air before it ran out of opinions, and the group drifted apart. Vale returned to the tarp, pulled out a notebook, and began writing something that was probably a logistics complaint. Reed stayed at the table, reorganizing his soil samples, muttering to himself about mineral uptake ratios. Quill drifted back toward the treeline, his attention drawn upward again. Hart dismantled the filter. At least the filter did what she told it to.

Marsh closed her field notebook and stood. She walked toward the secondary transect, past the sample flags and the measuring tapes that marked their primate observation grid. Reed watched her go, vaguely, his attention already re-absorbed by the cross-section data.

She walked for perhaps five minutes. The camp sounds faded. What replaced them was insect noise and heat, and underneath both, just at the edge of hearing, the harmonic.

Marsh crouched beside a root cluster near the transect boundary. She had been here yesterday, pulling the root for her bark-stripping study. The cluster was dense, the roots braided and interlocked in a pattern that did look, as Reed had said, deliberate.

Something caught the light wrong.

A stone, embedded in the root lattice. Small, perhaps the size of a large marble. Marsh reached for it. The roots gripped it loosely, like fingers holding something they did not want to release.

She pulled it free.

It was warm. Not from the sun. The heat came from inside the stone itself. And in the green shade under the canopy, it was glowing. Faintly, steadily, from no source she could identify. She had no idea what it was.

Marsh turned it over once. She held it up to the filtered light. She brought it close to her ear and listened, though she could not have said what she expected to hear.

Then she put it in her pocket.

She stood, wiped her hands on her trousers, and walked back toward camp. She did not mention the stone to Reed. She did not mention it to anyone.

Behind her, where the stone had been, the roots remained. Braided, interlocked, reaching down into soil that was two degrees warmer than it should have been. The harmonic pulsed beneath them, low and steady, the same sound Quill and Hart and Marsh had all heard and none of them could explain.

---

# Chapter 2: The Monitored

They had been walking for two hours when Quill began to suspect the forest was paying attention.

He could not have defended this. The forest was doing what forests do: growing, dripping, smelling of wet decay and something faintly metallic he could not place. The canopy filtered the light into a greenish murk that turned his colleagues into silhouettes arguing at various distances ahead of him. Nothing was watching him. He was tired, he had slept badly, and the particular quality of tropical humidity that made the air feel inhabited was a well-documented perceptual artifact. He had read the paper.

He still felt watched.

Ahead, Reed had not stopped talking since they left camp. His voice carried through the undergrowth like a radio signal that refused to degrade. "The root density increases as we move southeast, which is the opposite of what you would expect given the drainage gradient, and the mineral uptake ratios I showed you yesterday, the ones Vale didn't look at."

"I looked at them," Vale said, from somewhere to Reed's left.

"You looked at the first page."

"The first page contained the methodology. The methodology was sufficient."

"Sufficient for what?"

"For determining that I did not need to read the second page."

Reed made a sound that might have been a word swallowed by indignation. He pushed a broad leaf out of his path, and it swung back and hit Marsh, who caught it without breaking stride and held it for Hart behind her.

"The point," Reed continued, "is that the density gradient runs counter to every drainage model, counter to every soil composition survey for this basin, and counter to, actually, counter to basic thermodynamics, because the soil temperature is climbing as we move deeper, not dropping, and there is no volcanic activity within four hundred kilometers, I checked this morning, I checked it twice, and what I want to know is."

"Whether anyone has funded a geological survey of this quadrant in the last twenty years," Vale said. "Which is what I have been asking."

"That is not what I want to know."

"It should be. If the data is real, someone should have found it already. If no one found it, either the data is wrong or someone chose not to look. Both options are political."

"Not everything is political, Vale."

"Name one thing."

Reed opened his mouth. He was still thinking about it three steps later when Hart spoke from the back of the line.

"The copper levels in this soil should have killed everything in a fifty-meter radius."

Nobody responded. Hart was used to this. She adjusted the strap on her field kit and continued. "I tested three samples since we left camp. Copper concentrations roughly eight times the upper threshold for tropical hardwoods. Plus something I can't identify. An organic compound the kit doesn't recognize. And the soil is warm."

"We know the soil is warm," Reed said.

"You know the soil is warm from your data. I know the soil is warm because I am standing on it and the soles of my boots feel like I am walking on a heating duct. These are different kinds of knowing, Reed. Mine involves feet."

Marsh had stopped walking. She was crouched near the base of a massive trunk, her palm flat against the ground, head tilted. She stayed there for perhaps ten seconds, then stood and continued without a word. She had been doing this every few hundred meters since they entered the deeper growth. Each time, she stayed a little longer.

"What is she listening for?" Vale asked.

"I don't know," Reed said.

"Has anyone asked her?"

"She'll tell us when she wants to."

"That is not how field science works."

"It is how Marsh works."

Quill trailed behind them, his hands occasionally brushing the hanging roots that curtained the path. The roots were warm. Not from the sun. From inside, as though something deep in the ground were generating heat that migrated upward through the wood. The warmth had a quality to it, a steadiness, that did not belong to decomposition or chemical reaction. It was the warmth of something metabolizing. Something alive.

He did not say this. He was not sure enough, and the sensation was too strange to survive the translation into spoken words. If he said "the roots feel alive," Vale would ask him to define alive. Reed would want to measure it. Hart would test it for pathogens. And they would all be right, and the thing he meant would disappear.

The faint metallic smell was stronger here. Copper and something green, something herbal, carried on air that should have smelled only of rot and moisture. It reminded Quill of a laboratory he had visited in Edinburgh years ago, a room full of chemical preparations that had been left to interact without supervision. The smell of things doing something you hadn't asked them to do.

"Has anyone considered," Quill said, and immediately wished he hadn't started the sentence, because now he would have to finish it. "I mean, I know this might not be, but has anyone considered that the sound Marsh is hearing might be biological?"

The group stopped walking. Not all at once. Reed stopped first, which caused Vale to nearly walk into him, which caused Vale to stop with the particular rigidity of a man who has been physically inconvenienced by someone else's curiosity. Hart stopped because everyone else had. Marsh, who was already ahead, turned around.

"Biological how?" Reed said.

"I don't know. I suppose what I'm asking is whether the sound, and the warm soil, and the copper concentrations, and the root density, whether all of it could be, not geological. Not chemical. But produced by something. Organized."

"Organized," Vale repeated.

"Living."

The word sat in the humid air. A macaw called from somewhere above them, oblivious and unhelpful.

Reed pushed his glasses up with both hands. "That would require a biological system of extraordinary scale. Something kilometers across, at minimum. Operating underground, generating detectable heat, producing subsonic harmonics, and somehow remaining undetected by every survey, satellite, and indigenous community in the Amazon basin."

"Yes," Quill said. "I know it sounds."

"It sounds insane."

"Yes."

"It's not impossible," Marsh said.

Everyone looked at her. She was standing very still, her field notebook pressed against her thigh, her face wearing the expression Quill thought of as her listening face. Not listening to them. Listening to something underneath them.

"Mycelial networks can span thousands of hectares," she said. "The honey fungus in Oregon is three and a half square miles. Something analogous, something we haven't categorized, operating at depth, in these conditions." She paused. "I'm not saying it's likely. I'm saying Quill's question isn't stupid."

"I didn't say it was stupid," Reed said.

"You said it sounded insane."

"Insane and stupid are completely different assessments."

"Right," Vale said. "So. We're now entertaining the hypothesis that the Amazon rainforest is sitting on top of an undetected biological superorganism. Based on warm dirt and a sound two people may or may not be hearing."

"Three people," Hart said. "The sound is real. I hear it right now."

The silence after this lasted long enough for Quill to become aware of his own breathing, the ticking of insects, the distant complaint of something large moving through the canopy. And beneath all of it, barely there, a low sustained tone. Not a hum, exactly. A vibration that entered through the feet and settled somewhere behind the sternum. The sound of something large and slow and alive, if alive was the right word, which it probably wasn't, but no better word presented itself.

Reed started walking again. "I want to reach the secondary anomaly site before we lose the light. We can discuss the superorganism hypothesis over dinner."

"It's not a hypothesis," Quill said, but quietly, and to no one in particular. His hands were moving. He put them in his pockets to make them stop.

They walked.

The forest was getting denser. The roots underfoot were braided, interlocked, running in patterns that did not look like growth. They looked like infrastructure. Nobody said this. Quill suspected they were all thinking it.

The copper smell intensified. The air tasted of it now, green and mineral, coating the back of the throat.

And somewhere below them, something was aware they were coming.

---

Seven meters underground, in a chamber lined with living root and lit by the pale glow of the stuff that grew in the walls, a frog the size of a human thumb sat in shallow water and received a report.

The chamber was warm, damp, and busy with the kind of attention that has no visible center. Three smaller frogs, their skin flickering between grey and muted brown in quick, unsettled pulses, faced the larger one across a basin of mineral water that vibrated with sounds too low for human ears. The air smelled of wet copper and crushed herbs, sharp enough to sting.

The report was not delivered in words. It was delivered in everything at once. The three monitors clicked and croaked in overlapping patterns while their skins cycled through colors that modified each phrase the way a raised eyebrow modifies a sentence. A chemical tang entered the water, carrying information that had no equivalent in any human language. The whole thing arrived at once.

What it meant, approximately, was: five of them. Moving southeast. Past the third marker. Carrying instruments.

Noll, who was old, and grey-green, and held one forelimb slightly inward against his body in a way that nobody mentioned, absorbed the report the way a stone absorbs rain. His colors did not shift. His body did not move. He sat in the water and was still, and his stillness said more than a human speech could.

When the monitors finished, a low vibration passed through the basin. A question, asked with the body. The water carried it outward to the three smaller frogs, who responded with a flicker of dull orange across their throats. Confirmed. Still moving. Faster than expected.

Noll's skin shifted, finally, to a steady muted blue. The color of formality. The color that meant the next thing said would be said for the record, for the group, for the structure that held them all in place.

A sound emerged from him: clicks, harmonics, a tone that seemed to come from the water and the walls and his body simultaneously. It was measured, unhurried, administratively precise. Translated very roughly, stripped of the colors and the chemical undertones and the vibration that gave it context, it would have sounded something like: the situation will be presented to the Pools. All coordinators of the outer zones will attend. The timing is not favorable. The preparations for what is coming cannot accommodate disruption.

One of the monitors, the smallest, produced a rapid series of clicks. A question, or an objection, or both. Its skin went briefly pale.

A second frog answered before Noll could. This one sat at the far edge of the basin, half-submerged, smaller and darker than the others. Dark green that was almost black. Pale spots across her back that flared white as she spoke, broadcasting an irritation she could not hide and did not try to.

Talla's response was short, sharp, and untranslatable in its full effect, but the core of it was simple: redirect them. Now. The chemical compounds for disorientation were available. The paths could be rearranged. The outer growth could be accelerated to close the route they were using. Five large warm-blooded animals stumbling through the perimeter during the preparations was not a problem that required a meeting at the Pools. It required a decision, made here, made now.

Noll's blue deepened. The forelimb he held against his body shifted, almost imperceptibly. His response came as a single sustained harmonic, low and certain, felt in the water more than heard through the air. It carried the weight of someone who had been asked to hurry many times by many frogs and had never once complied.

The matter would go to the Pools.

Talla's spots flared and then, with visible effort, dimmed. She sank lower in the water. The smell of crushed herbs sharpened briefly around her, a chemical signal she could not fully suppress: frustration, or disgust, or the particular scent that meant someone had confused caution with leadership.

The monitors waited. Noll waited. The walls glowed. The water hummed with the residue of what had been said, carrying it downward through root and stone to wherever such things went when the conversation was over.

Five large warm-blooded animals. Still moving southeast. Carrying instruments.

---

They made camp at dusk in a clearing that was not quite a clearing. The trees had pulled back from a rough circle of open ground as though something underneath had discouraged their roots. The soil was exposed, reddish-brown, and warm to the touch.

Quill helped Hart set up the water filtration unit while Reed hammered sample stakes into the ground and Marsh strung the tarp between two trunks. Vale stood at the edge of the clearing with his arms folded, surveying the forest with the expression of a man assessing a room for exits.

"The root structures end here," Reed said, on his knees with a headlamp. "Abruptly. Not tapered. They just stop, as if something drew a line."

"Or as if something is underneath that they can't grow through," Hart said.

"Or can't grow into. Which would mean something is down there that the roots are avoiding, which would mean the roots are responding to a stimulus at depth, which would mean."

"Reed." Vale had not moved. "Set up your tent."

"I am collecting data."

"You are collecting data in the dark, on your knees, in soil that Hart says should be toxic. Set up your tent. Collect data in the morning. With sunlight. Like a professional."

Reed looked at the soil under his headlamp. He looked at Vale. He set up his tent.

The five of them ate in relative quiet: rehydrated meals, a thermos of coffee Hart had prepared that was the only thing she trusted from the water supply, and energy bars that had been in Marsh's pack since Bogota and tasted like compressed sawdust and optimism. The conversation was tired. Reed talked about root termination patterns. Vale asked pointed questions about the university's communication schedule. Hart inventoried their medical supplies aloud, which no one had asked her to do but which she considered a public service.

Marsh ate, and sketched in her notebook, and said nothing. Her hand went to her trouser pocket twice. She touched something there and then moved her hand away.

Quill sat on the warm ground and tried to identify what was different about this clearing. Not the temperature, though the ground was noticeably warmer here, warm enough to feel through the seat of his trousers. Not the smell, though the copper-and-herb scent was stronger, pervasive, almost pleasant in a way that unsettled him. Something else. Something about the quality of the silence.

He was trying to formulate this thought, turning it over in his mind the way he turned over most thoughts, approaching and retreating and approaching again from a different angle, when the ground began to sing.

It was not a metaphor. The sound rose from beneath them, a low, sustained vibration that passed through the soil and into their bodies. Not faint. Not ambiguous. A clear tone, layered, shifting in pitch, resonating in the chest and the teeth and the bones of the skull. The sound of something enormous, breathing through the earth.

Reed put his hand flat on the ground. "It's warm," he said, which was true but not remotely sufficient.

"What the hell is that?" Vale was on his feet, arms no longer folded.

Hart reached for her field kit. Her hands were steady. Her face was not.

Marsh closed her eyes. She sat very still, her palms pressed to the soil, her head tilted at that listening angle. She did not look frightened. She looked like someone who had been waiting to hear a particular piece of music and had finally found the right frequency.

Quill stood in the center of the clearing, the vibration moving through his legs and his spine and the back of his head. The ground was not ground. It was the surface of something.

Below them, something was alive, and it knew they were there.

The harmonic did not fade. The insects had gone quiet. The canopy was still. Nobody spoke for a long time.

---

# Chapter 3: First Contact

Reed found it first, which he would mention later, and often.

The formation sat at the base of a strangler fig where the ground had cracked open along a seam that ran roughly north-south for about four meters. It looked geological. Most things in the Amazon looked geological if you squinted. But Reed was not squinting. He was on his knees with his face six inches from the rock, his glasses fogged, his headlamp illuminating a surface that should not have been smooth.

"This is not erosion," he said. "This is not water-carved, this is not wind-worn, this is not any process I can identify that would produce a surface this uniform at this depth in this substrate. The mineral composition alone is, wait, wait, wait." He ran his fingers along the seam. "Feel this."

Nobody moved to feel it.

"The edges are beveled. Beveled. Not fractured. Something shaped this."

"Something shaped a rock," Vale said. He was standing four meters back with his arms folded, which was his default position for receiving information he considered premature. "In the Amazon. Where geological processes have been shaping rocks for approximately four billion years."

"Not like this."

"Reed, everything you have shown me for the past three days has been 'not like this.' The roots are not like this. The soil is not like this. The mineral composition is not like this. At some point, the simplest explanation is that your model of 'this' is wrong."

"My model is not wrong. My model is incomplete. There is a difference."

"The difference is funding."

Hart was crouched near the base of the seam, running a strip of litmus paper along the rock face. She sniffed the paper, frowned, and sniffed the air itself. "There's something in the air here. Organic. Not decomposition. Not floral. Something I haven't smelled before." She paused. "Actually, that's not true. It's the copper smell. Stronger. Much stronger. And something underneath it. Sweet, almost. Like overripe fruit stored in a mineral cave."

"Fermentation," Marsh said.

Everyone looked at her. She was standing slightly apart from the group, her field notebook in her left hand, her pencil behind her ear. She had not written anything down since they arrived at the formation. She was looking at the ground.

"What?" Hart said.

"It smells like fermentation. Not rot. Fermentation. Something is being broken down on purpose."

"By what?"

"I don't know." Marsh looked up. "I know what fermentation smells like. That's what this is. Something underground is fermenting."

"Lovely," Vale said. "Underground fermentation. Add it to the list."

Reed was already moving along the seam, tracing it with his fingers. His headlamp caught something and he stopped. "There's an opening."

This produced a silence of the variety that occurs when five people simultaneously recognize that a situation has changed category. Reed was pointing his headlamp into a gap in the rock, perhaps a meter wide at the base, narrowing upward into the root lattice. Beyond the light's reach, the gap continued downward.

"That's a passage," Reed said.

"That's a hole," Hart said.

"It's a passage. Look at the walls. Smooth. Beveled. The same surface treatment as the seam. Something made this."

"Something made a hole in the ground," Vale said. "Perhaps we should call National Geographic."

"We should go down," Reed said.

This initiated a conversation that Marsh would later describe, in her field notebook, as "the fastest argument in the history of scientific fieldwork." It lasted perhaps forty-five seconds. Marsh timed it because she was curious and because nobody else would think to.

Vale said yes immediately, which surprised everyone including Vale. He phrased it as a command rather than an opinion. "We go down. We document everything. If this is what Reed thinks it is, someone else will find it eventually, and I want our names on the data before that happens."

Hart said: "No. Absolutely not. We have no idea what the air quality is below the surface. We have no gas detection equipment rated for confined spaces. We do not know the structural integrity of that passage. If someone falls and breaks a leg down there, the nearest hospital is a two-day boat ride and a helicopter. Nobody goes anywhere until I can confirm we won't asphyxiate."

Reed said nothing because Reed was already three steps into the passage, his headlamp bobbing downward.

"Reed!" Hart said.

"The air is fine," Reed called back, his voice already acquiring the flat quality of enclosed spaces. "Better than fine. It's cool. There's a breeze. A breeze means ventilation, which means another opening."

"A breeze means a pressure differential, which means nothing about toxicity."

Quill was standing at the edge of the formation with his hands at his sides. He looked at the passage, then at Marsh, then at his own feet. His mouth opened and closed. He did not speak.

Marsh put her pencil back behind her ear and closed her notebook. "We're scientists," she said. "There's a hole in the ground that shouldn't exist, and it smells like an underground brewery. The question answers itself."

She walked into the passage.

Vale followed. Hart said something in French that Marsh did not catch and followed. Quill stood for another few seconds, his hands opening and closing at his sides, and then he followed too, which left the forest empty and indifferent above them.

The passage went down at perhaps thirty degrees, steep enough that Marsh had to brace her hand against the wall. The stone was cool and smooth under her palm. Too smooth. Not polished, exactly, but worn, the way a banister gets worn by a century of hands. Except these grooves were small. Narrow. Spaced close together, running in parallel tracks along the lower wall at about ankle height.

Something had walked this path. Something small, walking the same route, over and over, for a very long time.

The air changed as they descended. The temperature dropped by what felt like ten degrees in perhaps twenty meters. Underneath the cool: wet stone, mineral water, the fermentation smell Marsh had identified, and something green and sharp she could not name. Her skin prickled.

"The air is cooler here than the surface," Reed said from ahead, as if this were not obvious to everyone currently experiencing it.

"Thank you, Reed," Vale said.

"Which means temperature regulation. Active temperature regulation. Something is managing the airflow in this system."

Hart was running her hand along the wall. "There's moisture on these surfaces. Condensation, maybe. Or something excreted." She brought her fingers to her nose. "Copper. Faint, but it's there."

"Don't taste it," Marsh said.

"I was not going to taste it."

"You were moving your fingers toward your mouth."

"I was bringing them to my nose. There is a six-centimeter difference that I would think a field biologist could distinguish."

The glow began gradually. Marsh almost missed it. A faint blue-green glow in the walls, so dim at first that it might have been an afterimage from Reed's headlamp. But it persisted. And it spread. Within another ten meters of descent, the walls on both sides were producing their own light, a soft fungal glow embedded in the root structures that lined the passage. The light shifted with the air currents, brightening and dimming in slow pulses.

Reed turned off his headlamp. The blue-green light was enough to see by, barely. It turned their faces into something from a painting nobody would buy.

"That is bioluminescence," Reed said. "In the walls. Generated by a fungal network integrated into the root system. The energy requirements for sustained luminescence at this level would be, actually, I don't know what they would be, because this shouldn't be possible at this scale without."

"Reed," Vale said.

"Right. Yes. Moving."

Marsh stopped. Ahead, she could hear Reed's voice continuing its monologue and the others' footsteps on stone. But she was looking at the floor of the passage.

A channel ran along the left wall, perhaps ten centimeters wide, carrying a thin stream of water that moved with purpose, not trickling but flowing as though it had somewhere to be. In the water, small objects moved. Regular shapes. Not stones. Things that had been placed in the current to be carried somewhere. They were too small to identify in the dim light, but they were uniform and deliberate and they were floating downstream toward whatever was at the bottom of this passage.

Marsh crouched and watched them pass. Six, seven, eight identical shapes moving in a line. Deliveries.

She stood and kept walking.

The sound reached them before anything else did. A low, constant murmur, like distant conversation heard through walls. Water sounds underneath: flowing, dripping, the hollow echo of liquid moving through stone chambers. And something else. Something tonal. Not the harmonic from the surface. This was higher, more complex, and it came from multiple sources, as if several things were communicating at once in frequencies that sat just at the edge of what Marsh could hear.

The passage opened.

Marsh stepped out of the corridor and stopped, and behind her Vale stopped, and behind him Hart stopped, and for a few seconds nobody said anything because the space they had entered did not match any expectation they had carried into it.

The corridor opened into a junction where several passages met, and the ceiling rose to perhaps four meters, and the walls were lined with roots that had been shaped into something between architecture and gardening. Water channels ran along every surface, converging at a shallow basin in the center of the floor. The blue-green light was brighter here, bright enough that details emerged: the texture of the root walls, the moisture beading on stone surfaces, the worn paths on the floor where something had walked the same routes so many times the stone had grooved.

And in the center of the chamber, arranged in a loose semicircle facing the passage the biologists had just emerged from, seven frogs sat in shallow water and waited.

The largest was perhaps eight centimeters long. Muted green-grey. Very still. One forelimb held slightly inward against its body. Flanking it on either side, two slightly smaller frogs whose skin shifted between shades of brown and grey in slow, controlled patterns. Behind them, four more, their colors less controlled, flickering between curiosity and something that might have been alarm.

The large frog's skin shifted to a steady, muted blue.

"You are the research team from the surface," it said, in English. "We have been discussing you."

The voice was small. Of course it was small. The frog was eight centimeters long. But the words were precise, each consonant placed with care, each vowel given its full value. The accent was not quite anything Marsh could identify. It sounded like someone who had learned English from books written a long time ago and had never had the opportunity to practice with a native speaker. Victorian. Administrative. The voice of a colonial filing clerk from 1890, if the clerk had been a frog.

Five things happened at once.

Reed stepped forward and his glasses caught the blue-green light and his voice came out at a pitch Marsh had never heard from him, somewhere between elation and cardiac arrest. "How. Wait. You speak English? How long have you. The root density, is that yours? The mineral uptake, the branching patterns, we measured seventeen standard deviations, seventeen, and the bioluminescence, is this a cultivated network or a symbiotic, and how are you, how is this, wait, wait, wait."

Vale's arms uncrossed. He stepped to Reed's left and his voice dropped into the register he used when he was talking to someone he suspected of lying. "Who authorized this contact? Who decided what we would be shown? I want to know who is running this."

Hart had not moved from the passage entrance. Her hands were on the straps of her field kit. "Nobody touch anything," she said. "Nobody breathe through your mouth. We don't know what's in this air. We don't know what's on these surfaces. If this is a biologically active environment with species we have never encountered, the contamination risk goes in both directions."

Quill stood very still. His hands were at his sides. His mouth was closed. His eyes moved. He did not speak.

Marsh watched all of this, and filed it, and looked at the frogs. Not at the large one who had spoken. At the others. The four behind the delegation were shifting colors rapidly, patterns she could not read, their small bodies angled toward each other in postures that looked like commentary. One of them, the smallest, flickered a bright orange across its throat and the frog beside it responded with a color Marsh could not name, something between green and gold, and they both turned to look at the biologists with an attention that felt less like scientific curiosity and more like the way a crowd watches a street performer before deciding whether to stay or leave.

The large frog waited through all of this with the patience of something that had learned, over a very long time, exactly how much silence it took for other beings to finish panicking.

"My name is Noll," it said. "I coordinate, loosely, for this settlement. These are my colleagues." A small gesture with the undamaged forelimb toward the flanking frogs. "Your questions are understandable. Many of them have answers. Some do not. We have been monitoring your work since you arrived in this region. Your data, if I may say, has been quite thorough, though your mineral uptake model requires a correction I am sure Dr. Reed will find interesting."

Reed made a sound.

"We had not planned to make contact at this time," Noll continued. "You have been more persistent than we anticipated. The current circumstances make delay impractical. We felt it better to meet than to continue redirecting your path."

"Redirecting," Vale said. The word landed like a dropped tool.

"Your navigation through the forest has been subtly guided for three days. Chemical gradients. Adjusted root structures. Minor atmospheric changes. I regret the discourtesy."

Vale's jaw moved but no sound came out, which was, Marsh thought, probably a first.

"You've been herding us," Hart said.

"Guiding," Noll said. "Herding implies livestock. We intended no disrespect."

"You intended no disrespect while chemically manipulating our movement through a forest without our knowledge or consent."

Noll's blue coloring deepened slightly. "Yes. I can see how that would be objectionable from your perspective."

"From any perspective."

"Perspectives vary more than you might expect."

Behind the delegation, the chamber was filling. Marsh saw it happening in the way she noticed most things, through accumulated detail rather than sudden recognition. More frogs were arriving. Not from the passage the biologists had used but from other corridors, other openings in the root walls that Marsh had not initially seen. Small bodies moving through shallow water, climbing damp stone, settling on root ledges at various heights around the chamber. Some were the muted greens and browns of the delegation. Others showed colors she had not expected on frogs: deep rust, pale gold, a violet so dark it was almost black. Their skin shifted as they settled, colors rippling across their bodies in patterns that looked like conversation.

There were perhaps twenty of them now. Then thirty. A crowd, forming the way crowds form anywhere: a few curious arrivals, then a few more drawn by the presence of the first few, then a general drift toward the spectacle. Two of them, perched on a root shelf to Marsh's right, were murmuring to each other in sounds she could not parse, clicks and low harmonics that traveled through the water channels and resonated faintly in her teeth. One of them made a short, sharp sound and the other responded with a flicker of pale green, and they both leaned forward to get a better look at Hart's field kit.

They were, Marsh realized, rubbernecking.

"How many of you are there?" Reed asked. His voice had steadied slightly. His glasses were still fogged but he did not seem to have noticed.

"In this settlement, approximately twelve hundred," Noll said. "Globally, the number is somewhat larger. I would be happy to discuss demographics at a more suitable time."

"Twelve hundred." Reed's voice climbed back up. "Twelve hundred individuals of an undiscovered intelligent species living in a structured settlement beneath the Amazon rainforest. With bioluminescent infrastructure. And language. And you speak English. You speak English."

"We speak several of your languages. English is among the more practical, if architecturally limited. Four bricks." He paused. "Forgive me. That is an internal reference."

One of the flanking frogs shifted colors briefly. Noll's undamaged forelimb moved, a gesture so small Marsh almost missed it. The flanking frog settled.

"Architecturally limited," Reed repeated.

"Your language encodes meaning in one channel. Sound. We find this, I believe the word is, quaint."

On the root shelf to the right, the two watching frogs exchanged a rapid series of clicks. One of them shifted to a bright yellow-orange and then back to brown, very quickly. The frog equivalent, Marsh suspected, of nudging your friend and saying, "Did you hear that?"

"You've been monitoring us," Vale said. He had recovered from the shock faster than anyone, which Marsh noted because it confirmed something she had always suspected about Vale: his suspicion was structural, not emotional, and when the world rearranged itself he simply applied the same suspicion to the new arrangement. "You knew we were coming. You chose to meet us here. You arranged this. Which means you have an objective. What do you want?"

Noll regarded Vale for a moment. His colors remained that steady, controlled blue. "You assume that because we prepared for your arrival, we must have an agenda beyond the arrival itself. This is a reasonable assumption. It is also, in this case, incorrect. We are meeting you because you found us. The agenda, such as it is, is yours."

"Nobody meets anyone without an agenda."

"That has not been my experience."

"Then your experience is incomplete."

"I have been coordinating for this settlement for eleven years. I have lived twenty-eight. In that time I have attended perhaps four thousand meetings. Some had agendas. Some did not. Some had agendas that became apparent only afterward, which I find is the most honest kind."

The number landed on Marsh a beat later than it should have. Twenty-eight years. He said it the way a person might say a number that was obviously, self-evidently, not a long time. Because for him it was not. Twenty-eight years was old. Twenty-eight years was most of a life.

She filed that away and kept watching.

Reed was vibrating. Not literally, though it was close. "The bioluminescent network in the walls. Is it cultivated? Is it symbiotic? What is the energy source? Because the sustained output I'm observing here is not consistent with any known fungal metabolism and the spectral properties suggest a substrate interaction that would require, conservatively, a Nobel Prize in three separate categories to explain."

Noll blinked, which on a frog was a slower, more deliberate action than on a human, and gave the impression of a government official carefully not responding to a question he found endearing.

"Dr. Reed. You are standing in a settlement that your species has not detected for the entirety of its existence. I would gently suggest that the questions you have may benefit from context, and that context may benefit from patience."

"I have context. I have three years of anomalous data."

"You have three years of questions. We have two hundred million years of answers. Perhaps we might find a pace that accommodates both."

Reed's glasses had fogged completely. He took them off, cleaned them on his shirt, and put them back on. They were immediately worse.

Hart had moved two steps into the chamber. She was looking not at the frogs but at the air. Marsh could see her nostrils working, cataloguing smells. "The air in here," Hart said. "The copper compound. The organic volatiles. Are those produced by you? By your bodies?"

"Some, yes," Noll said. "Others are produced by the settlement itself. The walls, as you may have observed, are alive."

"I had observed that. Are they dangerous?"

"To you? Almost certainly not. To us? Occasionally, if maintenance is neglected. We are, I should note, as concerned about contamination from your presence as you are about contamination from ours. You are very large. You are very warm. You are breathing out an extraordinary volume of carbon dioxide and foreign microorganisms. Several of our physicians are, at this moment, extremely unhappy with me."

A frog near the back of the growing crowd, barely visible in the dim light, made a sound that Marsh could only describe as a harrumph. Several nearby frogs shifted colors in what was clearly amusement.

"May I suggest," Noll said, "that we continue this conversation inside the settlement proper. The corridors here are functional, not social. We have spaces that are better suited to extended discussion. The water is warm. The light is better. And my colleagues behind me have been waiting quite patiently, though I suspect their patience is reaching its natural limit."

He gestured with his undamaged forelimb toward the corridors beyond the chamber. In the blue-green light, Marsh could see them branching outward, some rising, some descending, all carrying that constant sound of flowing water. From deeper inside, the murmur of what could only be called activity. Life, happening at a scale she had not expected.

"Dissolve gently," Noll added, turning toward the nearest corridor. He said it to the two flanking frogs, lightly, the way a person might say "shall we" when leaving a restaurant. The flanking frogs shifted colors in acknowledgment and began to move.

Marsh heard it. Filed it. Did not understand it. It sounded like a pleasantry from a language she had not been introduced to.

Reed was already moving forward. Vale followed, his arms folded again, his face set in the expression of a man who intended to find the power structure or die trying. Hart adjusted her field kit and followed, scanning the walls as she walked. Quill had not spoken. He looked at Marsh and she looked at him and something passed between them that was not a word. An acknowledgment that whatever they had been doing before this moment had ended, and whatever came next had not yet acquired a name.

They entered the settlement.

The corridors branched. The water channels widened. The blue-green light spread and deepened and the sound of life grew louder, the murmur of a community going about its day that had now paused to watch five large mammals walk through its streets. An older frog, dark brown with pale yellow spots, sat in a water channel and watched them pass with the frank, unhurried interest of a retiree watching a parade.

The crowd was behind them now. And ahead of them. And on both sides.

Marsh walked, and watched, and listened to the water, and noticed that the corridor they had entered through was no longer visible behind them. Not because anything had closed. The turns had simply accumulated. She could not, if asked, have described the route back to the surface.

She put her hand in her pocket. The stone was warm.

---

# Chapter 4: Temporary Coordinators of Distributed Harmonies

Noll had arranged them in a line, which Vale found telling.

The frog had not said "follow me in single file." He had said "the corridors are narrow in places, and your comfort will be greater if you proceed in sequence." The result was the same. They were in a line, Noll in front, and the line moved at Noll's pace, which was the pace of an eight-centimeter frog navigating root corridors designed for eight-centimeter frogs. Vale, who was six foot one and had already hit his head on a ceiling root twice, found his comfort was not notably greater.

Behind him, the tour had acquired passengers. Two frogs trailed at the rear of the group, small and grey-brown, officially there to observe. They were not observing. They were having a conversation in quick clicks and color changes that Vale could hear but not parse, and from their posture and the way they kept glancing at Hart's field kit and then at each other, they were gossiping. He recognized the body language. He had spent twenty years in faculty departments.

The other passenger was worse.

"And this," Noll said, pausing at the entrance to a wide, low chamber, "is one of our Speaking Chambers. You will observe that it is partially flooded. This is by design. The mineral water facilitates communication and promotes a hormonal environment conducive to measured deliberation."

"The water is also warm," said a voice from behind Vale's left knee, "which is why everyone falls asleep during the long sessions."

Oona Reedmoss had appeared at some point between the residential corridor and here. Nobody had invited her. Nobody had objected to her arrival, either, which Vale noted. Noll's colors did not shift. His damaged forelimb did not move. He simply absorbed her presence the way a river absorbs a thrown stone, closing around it without acknowledging the splash.

Oona was round-bodied, olive-green, with an orange patch at her throat that was currently very bright. She had the energy of a neighbor who had seen the tour group pass her window and come outside to contribute.

"Please," Noll said, to the biologists and to nobody in particular. "Observe."

Vale observed.

The Speaking Chamber was an amphitheater carved from living root, descending in concentric rings to a central basin of dark water. Perhaps forty frogs sat on the rings, half-submerged, arranged in no pattern Vale could identify as hierarchical. Their colors moved. Not flickering, not random. Shifting in waves that passed from one side of the chamber to the other, like wind through grass, except the grass was alive and opinionated. A sustained harmonic came through the water and the stone and the soles of Vale's boots, settling in his chest. It was not a sound he was hearing. It was a sound he was standing in.

"What are they deciding?" he asked.

"They are coordinating resource allocation for the western perimeter," Noll said. "The specifics would require context I am not certain we have time to provide."

"Who decides the outcome?"

"The coordination emerges."

"From what?"

Noll paused. The pause was perfectly measured. "From the process."

"Right." Vale watched a ripple of amber pass through the assembled frogs, answered by a wave of deep green from the opposite side. "So nobody has power. Nobody makes decisions. The moss just arranges itself into policy."

"There is no moss in this chamber," Noll said. "But I take your meaning. You believe that consensus is a performance behind which a smaller group exercises actual control."

"I believe that in every system I have ever studied."

"Then your studies have been thorough. Whether they have been complete is a separate question."

Vale filed that. He filed the pause before it, too. The pause was the more interesting data.

Reed had broken from the line. He was crouched at the edge of the basin, his glasses catching the blue-green light, one hand hovering over the water, almost touching it, pulling back, hovering again. "The vibration in the water. Is that how they vote? Through vibration?"

"It is how they communicate. Voting is a human metaphor that does not precisely apply."

"The water hummed," Marsh said quietly, from behind her notebook. She was sketching the concentric rings. "They deliberated and the water hummed."

Oona had positioned herself on a root ledge near Vale's ankle. "The Third Pool district tried to get a drainage maintenance vote through this chamber last season. The coordination emerged that it was not a priority. The Third Pool district still has drainage problems. The coordination did not emerge for them." Her orange throat patch flared. "But as Noll says, the process is the process."

Noll did not respond to this. His blue coloring held steady.

"We should continue," he said.

They continued.

The corridors branched, narrowed, opened into spaces that Vale's brain wanted to categorize and could not. Not rooms exactly. Not hallways. Grown structures that followed a logic he did not have the vocabulary for, shaped by a species that squatted and clung and floated. Water ran through every surface, along channels carved by centuries of flow. The light was the dim blue-green of the fungal networks, pulsing faintly with air currents, and Vale had stopped noticing it was strange about ten minutes ago. That was more disturbing than the strangeness itself.

Noll stopped at a low opening. "This is a residential section. You may find the noise startling."

The noise was already audible. Croaking, clicking, the ambient percussion of many small bodies in enclosed damp spaces. And, cutting through it, what was clearly an argument. Two frogs, somewhere in the warren, were going at each other in sharp bursts of sound that needed no translation. The rhythm was universal. Someone was wrong about something and someone else was making sure they knew it.

Noll walked past the argument as though it were furniture.

"Sleeping chamber allocation," Oona said, keeping pace with the humans. "It happens every season. Everyone wants the chambers near the warm channels. Nobody wants the upper levels, because they drip. It is the same argument every year. My sister had the warm chamber for three seasons running and people still bring it up."

"Do they have a formal process for allocating chambers?" Vale asked.

"They have a coordination."

"And does the coordination work?"

"The coordination," Oona said, "produces an outcome that nobody is satisfied with, which is then adjusted informally until people get roughly what they got last year. This is called harmony."

Hart had fallen behind. Vale looked back and found her standing at the edge of what appeared to be a trench running along the corridor floor, filled with a dark, glistening material that was growing. The growth was alive. It was doing something to the water that passed through it. It was, as far as Vale could determine, the sanitation.

"That's your sanitation," Hart said.

Noll turned. His blue coloring did not waver. "That is our relationship with biology."

"That is a trench of living organic material processing waste in an open corridor next to where people sleep."

"It is an effective system."

"It's right there. In the hallway."

"We do not have hallways in your sense. We have continuous living space. The processing systems are integrated into the living space. Separation would require introducing barriers to the biological flow, which would be, in our terms, counterproductive."

Hart stared at the trench. The trench pulsed faintly. "In my terms, it's a disease vector."

"The trenches in the Third Pool district are worse," Oona said. "Much worse. Nobody wants to coordinate the maintenance schedule. The last coordinator resigned after two seasons and nobody replaced her. If you think this is bad, you should see the eastern warrens after the wet months."

Noll's forelimb twitched. It was the smallest motion Vale had seen from him, and it was the first he had seen that Noll did not appear to have chosen.

They passed through the warrens. Brief rooms, moss-padded sleeping surfaces, the sound of domestic life through root-shaped walls. In one chamber, three frogs were eating something from a communal stone dish, their colors shifting in lazy, social patterns. They looked up as the humans passed. One of them turned a vivid, fascinated yellow. The other two pointedly did not look.

Marsh was writing in her notebook. Not fast. Precise entries, one per space, her pencil moving in the careful strokes of someone cataloguing specimens. Temperature. Humidity. The angle of the water channels. The wear patterns on the root-shaped seating. She was, Vale thought, the only one of them doing anything useful.

Quill was not taking notes. He was not asking questions. He was watching the frogs. Not the ones Noll presented. Not the ones who reacted to the humans. The ones in the background, the ones going about their day, the ones who were talking to each other in the rapid, layered communication the humans could hear and not understand. Quill was watching the way a frog at a water channel deferred to an older frog approaching from the other direction. The way two frogs on a root ledge shifted their colors in synchrony without appearing to notice they were doing it. The domestic choreography of a civilization that had been doing this for longer than mammals had existed.

He had not spoken since the tour began. Vale kept track of that.

Behind the group, the two monitoring frogs had stopped gossiping about Hart's field kit and started gossiping about Quill. Vale could not understand the sounds, but he recognized the glances, the way one tilted toward the other while looking at the silent human. He had seen the same thing at a hundred conferences. Two people in the back row, watching the quiet one, trying to work out what he was thinking.

"Dr. Vale." Noll had stopped at a junction where three corridors met. Water flowed from all three into a common channel, warmer here, smelling of minerals and something faintly herbal. "You have questions about governance."

"I have a question about the title you gave us. Temporary Coordinator of Distributed Harmonies. For the Marsh Federations."

"That is my formal designation, yes."

"Who gave it to you?"

"The designation rotates. I was selected through a consensus process that I can describe if you are interested."

"I'm interested in how temporary a temporary coordinator is."

Noll's blue held. Steady, controlled. The color of a man who has heard this question before. "The coordination cycle is five years. I am in my third cycle. Continuation is a matter of general feeling, not personal ambition."

"Eleven years. Three cycles. You've been coordinating for eleven years."

"The general feeling has persisted."

"Right. So the general feeling is that nobody else should do your job. That's power, Noll. That's a mandate wearing a modest hat."

"That is one interpretation."

"Name another."

"Service. Obligation. The willingness to do work that nobody else wishes to do. These are also interpretations. Whether they are more flattering to me or more accurate is a question I am not the appropriate person to answer."

The two monitoring frogs behind them exchanged a quick burst of clicks. One of them shifted briefly to a color Vale had not seen before, something between amber and pink. The other responded with what looked, to Vale's eye, like suppressed amusement.

"Can I touch this?" Reed said, from somewhere ahead of the group.

He had found Krrik's laboratory.

Or rather, he had found the entrance to Krrik's laboratory, which was marked by the smell of ozone and fermentation and by the presence of Krrik, who was standing in the doorway with the expression of a frog who had not been expecting visitors and was not pleased to have received them. He was smaller than Noll, mottled brown-green, dusted with something pale and powdery that might have been spores, and one of his hind legs was noticeably shorter than the other. His eyes were focused on something inside the laboratory that was apparently more interesting than five humans.

"No," Krrik said.

"Just the surface. The texture of this growth medium, the structural density is unlike anything I have ever."

"I said no."

"I won't remove anything. I just want to feel the."

"You are warm, you are damp, you are shedding microorganisms at a rate I find distressing, and the moss you are reaching toward contains more processing power than every computer your species has ever built. It is thinking right now. It is solving a calibration sequence I have been working on for three seasons. If you touch it, you will introduce biological contaminants and thermal disruption into a system that is, at this moment, smarter than both of us. So no. You may not touch it."

Reed pulled his hand back. His glasses were fogged. "More processing power than every computer we've ever built."

"That is what I said."

"And it's being tended by." Reed looked at the moss bed, then at the watering vessel sitting beside it on a stone ledge, then at Krrik. "You water it."

"It requires hydration. The hydration schedule is precise. I am the one who maintains it. If you are about to make a joke about gardening, I have heard it. The last human who was here made the same joke. It was not funny then either."

This landed on the group in stages. Reed first, because Reed processed fast. "The last human who was."

"We should continue," Noll said.

"Wait," Vale said. "The last human? There have been other humans here?"

"That is a topic we will address at a more suitable point in the tour."

"Noll."

"Dr. Vale, there are many things to see. The tour is structured for a reason. Context accumulates."

"Context is being managed. That's different from accumulating."

Noll regarded him. The blue held. The damaged forelimb was perfectly still. "You are correct that I am managing the sequence in which you receive information. I would hope this is understood as courtesy rather than concealment. The distinction is, I recognize, a fine one."

Vale stared at the frog. The frog stared at Vale. The silence had a texture to it, the quality of two people who both understood exactly what was happening and neither of whom was willing to blink.

"Fine," Vale said. "Continue the tour."

They moved on.

In the corridor outside Krrik's lab, the biologists fell apart. Not dramatically. Not with shouting. With the particular quiet disintegration of a group that has absorbed too much in too little time and needs to process it in smaller clusters.

Vale and Hart dropped to the back. "The sanitation," Hart said.

"I know."

"It's an open biological processing system in a residential corridor. The contamination potential is extraordinary. And he called it a relationship with biology."

"He calls everything a relationship with something."

"And the moss bed. Computing. In moss. Tended with a watering can."

"While the sewer runs through the kitchen."

"It's not a sewer. It's worse than a sewer. A sewer at least acknowledges the concept of separation between waste and living space."

Ahead, Reed had cornered Marsh and was talking at a speed that suggested his brain had overtaken his mouth and both had given up on his body. "The computation density in that moss bed, Marsh, the neural architecture, the substrate alone would require a revision of every model we have for biological information processing, and the watering, the watering is the thing, because it means the system is alive, it's not a machine, it's a living system that thinks and also needs to drink, and that means."

"Reed," Marsh said.

"And that means."

"Reed. Write it down. You'll lose half of it by dinner."

Reed patted his pockets, found no notebook, and looked stricken.

Marsh tore a page from hers and handed it to him.

Quill walked alone, between the two clusters, watching a frog in a water channel carefully arrange a series of small objects in a line. The objects were uniform, pale, about the size of rice grains. They were being placed with deliberate care into the flow of water and carried downstream. Deliveries. The frog arranging them had the focused, unhurried attention of someone doing a task that was important and tedious and had been done the same way for a very long time.

Quill watched this. He did not take notes. His hands were at his sides.

"Does the distributed consensus actually work?" he said.

Vale turned. Quill was not looking at him. He was still watching the frog with the deliveries.

"No," Vale said.

"You're certain about that."

"I'm certain that no system in history has distributed power without concentrating it somewhere. The question is not whether there is hierarchy. The question is whether the hierarchy admits it exists."

"And if it genuinely doesn't? If the consensus is real?"

"Then it's the first time in the history of organized life. And I would want to know what's holding it together, because something always is."

Quill turned from the water channel. "You don't think it's possible that a system could be genuinely equal."

"I think a system can be genuinely committed to the idea of equality while being structurally unequal. Which is most systems. Including this one."

Noll, who had been waiting ahead of them with the patience of a man who has spent eleven years waiting for people to finish talking, turned his head slightly. The motion was small. Vale caught it. Noll had been listening. Noll's colors had not changed, which meant Noll was choosing not to respond, which was its own response.

"Shall we proceed?" Noll said. "There is one more thing I believe will interest you."

He led them to a wider space, a junction where the ceiling opened and the blue-green light was brighter. Roots descended from above in shapes that looked architectural, deliberate, grown into pillars and arches over what might have been centuries. The water here was deeper, warmer, and the smell of minerals was strong enough to taste.

"This settlement is called Vreth-in-the-Root," Noll said. "It is one of several dozen across the globe. We are connected by displacement, though the specifics of that need not concern you today."

"Several dozen," Reed said.

"The population across all settlements is estimated at four to six million. We have never been numerous. We prefer this."

"Four to six million." Reed's voice had gone flat. The flatness of a number too large to process. "How long have you been here?"

"In this settlement? Approximately fourteen thousand years, in your counting."

"And as a civilization?"

"Roughly two hundred million years."

The corridor was very quiet.

"Two hundred million years," Hart said.

"Give or take. We predate flowering plants. We found them a pleasant development."

"And humans?" Vale said. "How long have you been aware of us?"

Noll's blue coloring did not change. His body did not shift. He delivered the next sentence like a weather forecast. "Approximately three hundred thousand years."

The five reactions happened in the space of about four seconds.

Vale: "Why the hell didn't you make contact?"

Reed: "How did you monitor us? What instruments? Over what range?"

Hart: "Has there been cross-species pathogen exposure? Three hundred thousand years of proximity without containment protocols is an epidemiological."

Marsh: "What did you think of us?"

Quill said nothing. His hands opened and closed at his sides.

Noll let them finish. He let the silence hold for a beat. Then he addressed Vale, and only Vale.

"Discovery is a form of entanglement," he said. "We preferred to remain disentangled. Until, of course, you made that impossible."

He said it like he said everything. Measured. Courteous. Final. Then he turned and continued down the corridor, and the tour was apparently over, or at least this part of it was over, and behind him five biologists stood in the blue-green light of a civilization that had been watching them for longer than their species had existed.

Oona's throat patch dimmed. She looked at the humans, then at Noll's retreating form, then back at the humans.

"He does that," she said. "Says the enormous thing and walks away. Very dramatic. I tell him it's rude. He tells me it's pacing." She hopped down from her root ledge. "The fermentation cellars are this way, if anyone wants something to drink. You look like you need it."

Nobody moved for a moment. Then Marsh closed her notebook and followed Oona, and the others followed Marsh, and the two monitoring frogs trailed behind them, clicking softly to each other about something the humans would never understand.

---

# Chapter 5: The Pairings

The Pools were louder than Quill had expected anything underground to be.

He stood at the edge of the largest pool, warm mineral water lapping near his boots, and tried to separate the noise into components. Croaking. Clicking. A sustained harmonic that passed through the water and into the stone and into his feet, settling somewhere behind his sternum. Above it all, the layered buzz of dozens of conversations happening at once in a language that used sound and color and vibration and smell simultaneously, so that the air itself felt busy. A frog the color of wet bark waded past his ankle and said something to another frog on a root ledge that made the second frog turn a vivid, scandalized orange. Two more sat at the shallow end of the mediation pool, eating something from a communal stone dish and arguing about it between bites. A young frog, impossibly small, bright green, perched on the lip of a water channel and watched the humans with the frank, unblinking interest of a child at a zoo.

The child was not wrong. The five of them were the exhibit.

Quill moved. He had learned, over the past two days — since Oona had marched them through the fermentation cellars and Noll had arranged quarters for them in a damp residential corridor — that the best way to exist in the settlement was to keep walking. Standing still attracted attention. Walking made you part of the current. Frogs moved around him in the water and along the root paths with the practiced indifference of commuters sharing a platform, stepping around his boots like pedestrians around a bollard, which was, he supposed, approximately what he was.

The Pools were the settlement's living room. He understood that now. Different pools served different purposes. The large, still, warm one to his right was for mediation, its water thick with minerals that Quill could smell, metallic and herbal. The running channel on the far side was for socializing, where frogs perched on wet stone and talked to each other in overlapping bursts. A deeper pool near the back wall held a few solitary frogs floating motionless, eyes half-closed, doing something that might have been meditation or might have been napping. The light was the usual blue-green of the fungal networks, brighter here than in the corridors, reflecting off the water and the wet stone and the shifting colors of a hundred small bodies.

Vale had cornered Noll near the mediation pool.

Across the chamber, Vale had cornered Noll. Vale was standing with his arms folded, leaning slightly forward, his posture the familiar geometry of interrogation. Noll sat at the pool's edge, his steady blue coloring unperturbed, his damaged forelimb tucked against his body. They were talking, but only fragments carried over the ambient noise.

"...the coordination process, as I have explained, is open to all who wish to..."

"Right. Open to all. So anyone could challenge your position."

"That is correct."

"Has anyone?"

A pause. Not long. Long enough.

"The question, Dr. Vale, is not whether challenge is possible but whether the general feeling of the community has produced..."

"The general feeling. You keep saying that."

"It is the mechanism."

"It's a word, Noll. General feeling is a word for something that has a structure and an enforcement mechanism, and I want to know what the enforcement mechanism is."

Hart drifted near them. She had been circling the mediation pool, examining the water with the focus of someone cataloguing potential diseases. She paused close enough to hear, and without looking at either of them, said: "The enforcement mechanism is politeness. It's always politeness."

Noll's blue held. Vale's mouth thinned. Hart moved on.

Quill kept walking.
