Near the socializing channel, Marsh was sitting on a flat stone at the water's edge with Oona Reedmoss beside her. Oona's orange throat patch was very bright. She was talking at a pace that suggested the subject was one she had been waiting to discuss for some time.

"The queue is impossible. Impossible. Before the preparations started, you could use the displacement platform in the morning and be at the Ninth Warren in time for feeding. Now there are frogs from three districts all crowding in because they have been told to report to the staging grounds, and nobody has coordinated the schedule, nobody, and when I say nobody I mean Noll has not coordinated it because Noll does not use the platform like the rest of us, Noll has priority access, which is not supposed to exist, but it does."

"Staging grounds?" Marsh said.

"For the Pruning. Everything is being prepared. I do not want to talk about the Pruning." Oona's throat patch dimmed briefly, then re-brightened. "I want to talk about the fermentation cellars. Since the preparations began, they have moved my culture stock to the lower level. The lower level! Where the humidity is wrong and the temperature is three degrees too warm and the cultures are going to sour, and when I complained, they said it was temporary, and I said, how temporary, and they said, until after, and I said, until after what, and they looked at me as though I had asked something rude."

Two frogs wading past in the shallow water stopped and turned. They were smaller than Oona, brown-green, and appeared to have opinions.

"The lower level is fine," one of them said. "My batch has been down there for two seasons and the acidity is perfectly stable."

"Your batch," Oona said, "tastes like wet stone."

"It is supposed to taste like wet stone. That is the style."

"That is not a style. That is a failure."

The third frog, slightly paler, with a yellow spot behind one eye, leaned in. "Oona, your batch tastes like you added too much bark. Everyone knows this. Nobody says it because you get upset."

"I do not get upset. I get accurate."

Marsh was watching this with her pencil behind her ear and her notebook closed in her lap. She was not writing. She was smiling in a way that Quill recognized from field stations, the expression of a naturalist who has stopped cataloguing and started enjoying.

He moved on.

The path toward the eastern corridors was busier than usual. Reed was ahead of him, following Krrik, who was trying to get to his laboratory and failing. Krrik moved in his distinctive uneven hop, one hind leg shorter than the other, his mottled brown-green body dusted with the usual pale spore residue. Every few meters, another frog intercepted him.

"Krrik, the humidity in Chamber Fourteen is dropping again and the arrays are."

"Adjust the ventilation. I showed you last week."

"You showed Tenn last week. Tenn is at the staging grounds."

"Then adjust it yourself. The dial is on the left. Turn it. Not far. A quarter rotation. If the moss starts changing color, stop."

The frog hopped away. Two steps later, another appeared.

"Krrik, the displacement calibration for the northern corridor is."

"Later."

"It has been later for three days."

"Then it will be later for four. I am working."

Reed was keeping pace behind Krrik with the attentive urgency of a graduate student trailing a professor who had not yet acknowledged his existence. His glasses were fogged. His shirt was damp. He looked profoundly happy.

"Is it always like this?" Reed asked. "People needing you constantly?"

"Yes," Krrik said, without turning around. "It is always like this. I have not had an uninterrupted working hour in six days. I am attempting to have one now. You are not helping."

"I could help. With the displacement calibration, I mean, I obviously don't know the, but the principles of substrate resonance are, at least from a theoretical standpoint, not entirely unlike."

Krrik stopped. He turned. His eyes focused on Reed for what appeared to be the first time. "You do not know what substrate resonance is."

"I know what resonance is. I know what substrates are. I am willing to learn the intersection."

"The intersection is nine years of training and a conceptual framework your species has not developed. But thank you."

He turned and kept hopping. Reed followed. Another frog appeared from a side corridor with a question about spore storage that made Krrik's skin flash a brief, involuntary orange.

Quill passed them and turned toward the southern pools.

Hart had found Talla.

Or Talla had found Hart. It was hard to tell. They were standing near a shallow overflow channel where water ran from the mediation pool into a lower basin. Talla was dark, almost black-green, smaller than Quill had expected, 4.5 centimeters at most, holding herself rigidly upright on a root ledge so that she was roughly level with Hart's knee. Her pale spots were visible across her back, faint against the dark skin. Hart was looking down at her with an expression Quill had not seen before. Interest without suspicion. Or suspicion that had found a worthy subject and relaxed into something more functional.

"You are the one who noticed the sanitation," Talla said. "Good. Nobody else here will admit it is a problem."

"It's a trench of living biological waste in a residential corridor."

"Yes."

"Next to where people sleep."

"Yes."

"And nobody else thinks that's a problem."

"They think it is a relationship with biology. I think it is a trench of living biological waste in a residential corridor. We have been having this conversation for eight years."

A frog near Talla, small, brown, sitting in the overflow channel, spoke up. "The trenches function. The maintenance schedule is the issue, not the design."

Talla did not look at the frog. Her eyes stayed on Hart. "The design is the issue. The maintenance schedule is the symptom. If you want to discuss this, you can attend the next coordination session, where you will be told the design is perfect and the maintenance is being addressed." She still did not look away from Hart. "It has been being addressed for eleven years."

The brown frog opened its mouth, closed it, and waded away.

Hart crouched down. She was closer to Talla's level now. "How bad are the eastern warrens?"

"After the wet months? Come and see."

"I will."

"Good."

They regarded each other. Two skeptics who had just discovered they spoke the same language, the language of systems that failed quietly while everyone agreed they were working. Quill watched the pairing form the way he had watched baboons in the field find their coalitions, not through introduction or arrangement but through the gravitational recognition of like by like.

He kept walking.

The shallow pool at the southern end of the chamber was still occupied. Fifteen or so frogs sat in warm water that barely covered their backs, arranged in a loose circle, their colors shifting in unison. A slow pulse of green passed through the group, starting at one end and rolling to the other, then returning, like a wave in a pond. Their breathing was synchronized. Quill could see it in the movement of their sides, the small inflation and deflation happening in perfect unison across fifteen bodies. The water between them hummed. Not a sound exactly. A vibration that traveled through the stone floor and registered in his teeth.

A breathing circle. He had heard about these. Oona had mentioned them, Noll had referenced them in passing, and twice since arriving he had heard frog voices in the corridors discussing whether the next one was at the Pools or in the Third District. But he had not seen one. He stood at the edge and watched.

The circle was ending. The synchronized pulse slowed. Colors began to differentiate, individual greens and browns and olive tones emerging from the collective wash. Frogs stirred, stretched, blinked. As they dispersed, climbing out of the shallow pool and heading in different directions, Quill heard them say it to each other. Casual. Habitual. The way humans said "see you later" or "take care."

"Croak and be croaked through."

"Croak and be croaked through."

A third frog, small and rust-colored, said it to an older one who was already wading toward the main pool, and the older one replied with a click and a color shift that seemed to mean the same thing back. The phrase traveled through the dispersing group, each frog saying it back without thinking about it.

Quill did not understand it. He filed it. The words had the texture of a pleasantry from a system of meaning he was not inside of.

One frog remained in the shallow pool after the others had left. Small. Vivid emerald green with gold flecks that caught the bioluminescent light and threw it back in fragments. One eye was slightly larger than the other, which gave the face a permanent expression of lopsided attention, as if one half of the frog was perpetually more interested than the other. He was eating an insect. The insect was still partially alive. The frog chewed with the slow, untroubled satisfaction of someone for whom eating during spiritual practice was not a contradiction.

An elderly frog, grey-brown and stiff-limbed, waded past the shallow pool and stopped. "The circle ran too long, Vesh. My joints are ruined. I can barely hop."

"Your joints are not ruined. They are remembering their age, which is what joints do. You should thank them."

"Thank my joints."

"They have carried you this far."

"They have carried me into a pool where I sat for too long listening to you breathe."

"The breathing was collective. I merely guided. The universe breathed through us. We were instruments."

"My instruments hurt."

Vesh ate the rest of the insect. He did this with complete, undistracted commitment, like some people pray. Then he turned to Quill.

"You study whether animals know they exist."

Quill's hands opened and closed at his sides. "That's one way to describe what I do."

"The answer is: they wish they did not. Sit. The moss is damp but the conversation will be dry."

Quill sat. The moss was damp. The stone beneath it was warm, the same geothermal warmth that ran through the whole settlement, and the water of the shallow pool touched the edge of his boots. From this position, the Pools spread out before him, busy and alive. He could see Marsh and Oona still at the socializing channel, the fermentation argument apparently ongoing. He could see Vale and Noll at the mediation pool, still locked in their geometry. Hart and Talla had moved closer to the overflow channel and were crouched together like two engineers studying a blueprint for something that was about to fail. Reed and Krrik had disappeared toward the eastern corridors. Everywhere else, frogs. Bathing, arguing, eating, watching, wading between pools, climbing root walls, sitting in the water channels and talking to their neighbors. A frog the size of Quill's thumb perched on a stone near the deep pool and appeared to be asleep. Another was pushing a small pale object along a water channel with deliberate, patient effort, sending it downstream toward somewhere with purpose. Three frogs on a root ledge above the main pool were watching the humans and commenting to each other in quick, low bursts that Quill could hear and not understand.

Vesh was five centimeters long. At this distance, sitting in the shallow pool, he was about the size of a large walnut. The gold flecks in his green skin shifted and brightened as he spoke, as though the words themselves generated light.

"You are Martin Quill. Your work on primate cooperation is known to us. We found it charming. Incomplete, but charming. Like a child's drawing of the ocean."

"Thank you. I think."

"It was meant kindly. The child sees the ocean. The child draws what it can. The ocean is not offended." Vesh settled deeper into the water. "I am Brother Vesh. Ritual Guide of the Third Breathing Circle. Interpreter of the Wet Way. I eat insects, I breathe in communal patterns, and I believe the self is a temporary swamp event. You are a puddle with opinions. I mean this as a compliment."

Quill's mouth opened and closed. He tried two sentences, abandoned both, and arrived at: "What does that mean? The self being a temporary swamp event."

"It means what it says. You formed. Conditions aligned. Water collected, ground dipped, and you appeared. You have a shape. You reflect things. You are real. But you were never separate from the water you are made of. When you evaporate, you have not died. You have stopped being a puddle." Vesh blinked his larger eye. "This is not metaphor. This is physics."

"It sounds like metaphor."

"Everything sounds like metaphor to a species that communicates in one channel." Vesh caught another insect from the air above the pool, a flick of the tongue so fast that Quill registered only the absence of the insect and the brief motion of satisfaction. "You use words. Only words. A single thread. We speak in sound and color and vibration and chemistry, all at once. When I say you are a puddle, I am saying it in four dimensions. You are hearing one. The other three would tell you I am also saying you are beautiful, temporary, connected, and slightly muddy. But those three you cannot hear. So. Physics sounds like metaphor. That is your limitation, not mine."

Around them, the Pools continued. A frog near the mediation pool raised its voice about something involving a sleeping chamber. Two frogs in the socializing channel were laughing, or making the sound that served as laughing, a rapid series of clicks that accelerated and then dissolved into color changes. The water hummed with ambient conversation, vibrations layering over vibrations, a constant, complex noise that Quill had stopped noticing as noise and started hearing as texture.

"What is the Wet Way?" he asked.

"The name we give to the obvious." Vesh stretched one forelimb and examined it with the detached interest of someone inspecting a rented tool. "Water flows. Life flows. Consciousness flows. Resisting the flow is possible but exhausting. The highest wisdom is to stop resisting. This is not complicated. Your species has arrived at similar ideas, in your fashion. You call it Taoism, or Buddhism, or getting very drunk. We arrived at it independently, through the experience of breathing through our skin, which makes the idea that you are separate from your environment difficult to maintain."

"So you believe in dissolving. Into something larger."

"I believe that the something larger is what you already are. The dissolving is a remembering. The separateness is the illusion." Vesh ate a leg of the insect that remained from his earlier catch. "But I also believe this insect was delicious. Both things can be true. The cosmic and the caloric are not enemies."

Behind them, near the main pool, Quill heard Oona's voice rise above the ambient noise. She was talking to Marsh, and her tone had shifted. Still warm, still gossipy, but underneath it something forced. Something lighter than the subject deserved.

"Everyone is worried. The breathing circles are more frequent. Vesh is doing three a day now, sometimes four. That has not happened since, well. Since the last time."

A frog wading past the socializing channel, grey-green, unremarkable, said to no one in particular: "The staging grounds are already being prepared. The Siberians have been in contact."

The frog kept wading. Nobody responded. The statement hung in the water like a temperature change, felt by everyone, mentioned by no one.

Quill looked at Vesh. "What is the Pruning?"

Vesh regarded him with the lopsided attention of his mismatched eyes. His gold flecks dimmed slightly, then brightened again, a correction so quick it was almost invisible.

"A maintenance task," Vesh said. "The universe grows. We trim." He caught another insect. "You will learn. Not tonight. Tonight we are talking about puddles."

At the far edge of the Pools, near the corridor that led to the deeper chambers, a figure sat alone at the water's edge. Quill had not noticed it at first because it was barely there. Faded, translucent grey-green, so pale that the bioluminescent light passed through its skin and showed the faint outline of structures beneath. It was old. Older than anything else in the chamber, by the quality of its stillness, by the way it occupied its space like a stone that had been sitting in the same spot for a very long time. Its eyes were bright. Clear and sharp in the faded body, watching everything and everyone with an attention that was not curiosity. It was something more practiced. More patient. The attention of someone who had been watching a very long time and intended to continue.

Quill looked at the figure. The figure did not look back. It watched the Pools. It watched the humans. It watched the frogs watching the humans. It sat, and observed, and was still.

He turned back to Vesh.

"You said the self is a temporary swamp event. Do you believe that? Actually believe it?"

"I have spent most of my life teaching it. If I did not believe it, that would make me either a fraud or an idiot, and I am neither. I am also both. This is the nature of belief." Vesh settled lower into the warm water. The gold flecks in his skin were steady now, glowing faintly. "You ask because you want to know if it is true. I cannot tell you if it is true. I can tell you it is useful. The difference between true and useful is a problem your species takes very seriously and mine does not."

"Why not?"

"Because we die faster. We do not have time to determine whether our beliefs are correct. We only have time to determine whether they help. If they help, we keep them. If they do not help, we let them dissolve. Like puddles." He blinked. "This is the advantage of short lives. Terrible for accumulating wisdom. Excellent for testing it."

Quill sat with that. The warm water touched his boots. The Pools murmured around him. He could hear Oona's voice, distant now, still talking to Marsh. He could hear the clicks and harmonics of frog conversation from every direction, a living noise that was not quiet enough to ignore and not loud enough to prevent thought. Somewhere in the settlement, Krrik was in his laboratory, working on something that required extended hours. Somewhere, the staging grounds were being prepared for a thing the biologists had not been told about. Somewhere, Siberians had been in contact.

Vesh ate another insect.

"You are thinking very loudly," he said. "For a species with only one channel, you are remarkably noisy."

"I'm trying to understand what I'm hearing."

"That is the first honest thing a human has said to me." Vesh's gold flecks brightened. "Most of them start by telling me what they already know. You start by admitting you do not. This is promising. This is also terrifying. But promising and terrifying are the same thing, viewed from different levels of the swamp."

The Pools hummed. The water moved. The frogs talked and ate and argued and bathed. At the far edge, the pale figure sat and watched with its bright, ancient eyes. And in the shallow pool at the southern end of the chamber, a five-centimeter frog with one eye larger than the other ate an insect and explained the universe to a man who was beginning to suspect that the universe might, in this case, be explaining itself.

Oona's voice carried once more from the socializing channel, telling Marsh about the displacement queue, and underneath the complaint, underneath the gossip, her tone was the tone of someone talking about the weather while watching clouds that were the wrong color.

---

# Chapter 6: The Machine for Distributing Power

The session had been running for twenty minutes before Vale understood that nothing was being decided.

He sat on a wet stone ledge at the chamber's edge, his trousers soaked to mid-thigh, his notebook balanced on one knee. Marsh was beside him, her own notebook open, sketching the layout of the amphitheater in quick, sure lines. Below them, in the warm mineral water of the Speaking Chambers, thirty-seven frogs deliberated on the staging logistics of the Pruning. Their colors moved in waves, rolling from one side of the basin to the other, green answering amber answering blue, and the water hummed with a low vibration that settled into Vale's molars and stayed there like a headache that had decided to become a sound.

The agenda item was displacement allocation for incoming delegations. Noll sat at the center of the lowest ring, half-submerged, his skin settled into that steady, calming blue that Vale had come to recognize as a performance of neutrality. His damaged forelimb was tucked inward, still. He had been speaking for three minutes in sentences so balanced they could have been weighed on a scale.

"The coordination framework, as proposed by the western perimeter working group, distributes platform access across a fourteen-cycle rotation. This allows each settlement delegation to arrive within an acceptable margin of."

"The margin is not acceptable," said a frog on the second ring, mottled brown, medium-sized, unremarkable except for the speed at which she interrupted. "The Seventh District has been requesting priority access for three seasons. We have supply dependencies that the rotation does not account for."

"The rotation accounts for supply dependencies through the auxiliary allocation in cycle eight," Noll said. "Your concern was addressed in the revised proposal."

"The revised proposal moved our access window to the night hours. Nobody wants to travel at night. The platform operators refuse to work double shifts."

"The operators' schedule is a separate coordination item."

"It is the same item. You separated them to make both items easier to ignore."

A ripple of amber crossed the chamber. Several frogs shifted on their rings. Two near the back had been having a side conversation in low clicks and were now paying attention, or at least performing attention, their colors tracking the argument with the idle interest of people watching a tennis match from a bar.

Vale wrote: *Procedural partitioning. Same technique as committee chairs in every parliament. Split the problem. Address each half separately. Neither half contains the actual complaint.*

Noll absorbed the objection the way he absorbed everything. His blue held. He offered a sentence that acknowledged the concern, restated the framework, and moved to the next item with the administrative grace of a man closing a door so gently you did not hear it shut.

Between agenda items, Vale leaned forward. "Who drafted the rotation framework?"

Noll turned his head slightly. "The western perimeter working group."

"And who sits on the working group?"

"That information is available in the coordination records."

"I'm asking you."

A pause. Measured. The pause of a man selecting the most boring possible answer. "The working group consists of representatives from each affected district, selected through a process of communal nomination."

"Right. So the consensus drafted a proposal, and the consensus is now debating it, and you're facilitating. Nobody decided anything. The process decided."

"That is an adequate summary."

"It's a circle, Noll. The process produces an outcome that validates the process. I've seen this before. It's called institutional self-justification."

"It is called governance, Dr. Vale. I understand the two can appear similar."

Behind them and to the left, Oona was perched on a root ledge near Marsh's elbow. Her orange throat patch was very bright. It had been bright since the session started and was getting brighter, which Vale had learned to interpret as a reliable indicator that she had something to say about everyone in the room and was waiting for someone to listen.

Marsh leaned slightly toward her. "Who's the one who interrupted? The brown frog on the second ring."

"Gren," Oona said, keeping her voice low. "Seventh District coordinator. She always objects. Not because she disagrees, usually, but because she objects. It is what she does. She has been objecting since before Noll's first cycle. She objected to the previous coordinator too. She objected to the coordinator before that. Gren objects."

"Does it work?"

"Sometimes. Not today. Today the agenda was settled before the session began. Watch the three frogs on the fifth ring, the ones who have not spoken. They will vote with Noll on everything. They always do. Denn, Lorra, and the pale one whose name I should remember but do not. They coordinate their colors before the formal consensus. You can see it if you watch. A flicker of blue, very fast, before each item resolves."

Vale was watching. He caught it on the next item. Three frogs on the fifth ring, unremarkable, silent, and their skins flickered to blue a half-second before the rest of the chamber settled. A pre-consensus. A signal.

He wrote: *Core bloc. Three confirmed. Probably more. Colors coordinate before the formal vote. The consensus is seeded.*

The next agenda item was something about nursery water temperature adjustments. Fourteen frogs weighed in. The discussion was genuine, or close to genuine, the kind of low-stakes domestic policy that democratic bodies handle well because nobody's power is at risk. Vale half-listened. Colors rippled. Water hummed. Two frogs on the upper ring got into a side argument about whether the mediation pool's mineral balance had been altered since last season and whose responsibility that was.

"That's Reth and Vann," Oona murmured to Marsh. "They have been arguing about pool maintenance for six years. Six years. Reth thinks the mineral levels are too low. Vann thinks Reth is measuring wrong. They argue about it at every session. Once they argued about it during a displacement emergency. An actual emergency. Frogs arriving from the Central African settlement with supply shortages, and Reth wanted to discuss magnesium content."

Vale turned. "During an emergency?"

"During an emergency. Noll let them speak for four minutes, then moved on. Reth was furious. Reth is always furious. The minerals are fine. Everyone knows the minerals are fine. Reth's entire identity is built on the minerals not being fine."

On the third ring, Talla sat conspicuously apart.

She was smaller than most of the chamber, dark, nearly black-green, holding herself upright on a stone ledge with the rigid posture of someone who had come prepared to be ignored. A cluster of younger frogs sat near her, four or five of them, their colors shifting in quick, sympathetic patterns that tracked Talla's movements the way an audience tracks a speaker they agree with but are not brave enough to quote.

The agenda moved to displacement platform scheduling for the Pruning staging. Noll introduced it with the same measured phrasing, the same balanced sentences, the same air of administrative inevitability. "The coordination proposes a centralized scheduling authority for platform access during the Pruning preparation period, to be administered by the logistics committee in consultation with."

"In consultation with whom?" Talla said.

The chamber shifted. Colors flickered. Not alarm. More like the collective sigh of a room that knew this was coming.

"In consultation with district representatives and affected settlement liaisons," Noll said.

"So the logistics committee decides, and then consults the districts about what it has already decided. That is not consultation. That is notification with a polite label."

"The consultation is genuine, Talla."

"Then reject the framework and let the districts propose their own schedules."

"That would create coordination conflicts between districts that the centralized authority is designed to prevent."

"The centralized authority is designed to prevent districts from disagreeing with the centralized authority. You have described a system whose purpose is itself." Talla's pale spots were visible across her back now, faint against the dark skin, flaring with the involuntary intensity of someone who had not planned to get this angry this early in the session. "The proposal says 'in the interest of harmonious coordination.' When the coordination says harmonious, it means quiet. When it means quiet, it means agreement. When it means agreement, it means this meeting is a ceremony and we are the audience."

The cluster of younger frogs near her shifted to deeper greens. Agreement. Visible, synchronized, unmistakable.

Noll did not change color. His blue held with that unsettling stability that was, Vale thought, its own kind of display. The display of not displaying. "Your concern is noted and will be incorporated into the revised framework."

"My concern was noted last session. And the session before that. It has been noted for two years. Noting is not incorporating. Noting is filing."

"The incorporation process requires."

"The incorporation process requires that I stop talking, which is the one thing the process is very efficient at producing."

A beat of silence. Two frogs near the back exchanged a glance. One of them, grey-green, elderly, shifted her color to something neutral and looked away. The chamber absorbed Talla's objection the way the chamber absorbed everything. Not with hostility. Not with dismissal. With a kind of gentle, systematic patience that said: you have been heard, you are valued, and now we are moving on. It was the most sophisticated form of silencing Vale had ever witnessed. No one argued with Talla. No one needed to. The process simply continued around her, like water flowing past a stone, and the stone could be as sharp as it liked. The water did not care about sharp.

The younger frogs near Talla held their deep green for a few seconds longer, then gradually settled back to ambient colors. They had agreed. Their agreement had been visible. It would change nothing.

Vale wrote: *Dissent tolerated. Dissent absorbed. The absorption is the tolerance. She is the designated objector. The system needs her to prove it allows objection. Her rebellion is a feature, not a bug.*

Marsh, reading over his shoulder, tapped the page with her pencil and wrote beneath his note: *Or she is right and nobody can do anything about it.*

The session continued. Reth and Vann resumed their mineral argument during a pause in proceedings. Noll waited for them to finish with the patience of a man who had been waiting for Reth and Vann to finish for three consecutive cycles and would continue waiting for the remainder of his natural life. A frog on the upper ring fell asleep and slid gently sideways into the water, causing a small splash that nobody acknowledged. Another frog, young and bright yellow, asked a question about nursery staffing that was off-agenda and was gently redirected, and the redirecting took four minutes and involved three frogs explaining the proper procedure for raising off-agenda items, which was itself off-agenda, and nobody redirected that.

Vale was beginning to appreciate the comedy of governance when the water moved.

Not the normal movement. Not the humming vibration of consensus or the ripples of thirty-seven bodies shifting on stone. Something deeper. A displacement in the water itself, as if the pool had inhaled. Colors cascaded through the chamber, a fast, involuntary wave of reaction that started at the basin's center and rolled outward through every frog in the room. For a moment the chamber was a single organism, all thirty-seven frogs flashing the same startled amber before their individual colors reasserted themselves.

And then there were more frogs.

Five of them, sitting in the basin as though they had always been there, except that they had not been there three seconds ago. The water around them was colder. Vale could see it in the way the nearest frogs flinched and adjusted, a ring of physical discomfort expanding outward from the newcomers. The water temperature of the entire pool dropped by a degree that was small and unmistakable.

The five new frogs were different. Broader. Thicker-skinned, their coloring a flat, dense grey that did not shift or ripple. Their posture was compact, tucked close, built for holding heat rather than displaying it. They sat in the water of a tropical amphitheater with the bearing of animals who considered this temperature self-indulgent.

Siberians.

The chamber adjusted. Colors settled. Noll's blue deepened by a fraction, which Vale filed as the first involuntary shift he had seen from the High Speaker during a formal session. It lasted less than a second. Then the blue was back, steady, controlled, and Noll was turning toward the delegation with the precise courtesy of a diplomat greeting allies he had not expected for another two days.

"The Siberian delegation is welcome. We had anticipated your arrival at the next coordination window."

The largest of the five, a broad grey frog with a scar across her back and eyes that moved like someone checking exits, spoke without diplomatic preamble. "The window was too slow. We came now. The northern substrates are accelerating ahead of the models. Your models. Which are wrong."

The chamber went quiet. Not the quiet of consensus-building. The quiet of a room that had just been told something nobody wanted to discuss in front of guests.

Noll absorbed it. "The models are being revised. Dr. Krrik's latest projections suggest."

"Krrik's projections are based on data we sent him four days ago. The data from this morning is worse. We will present it. Now, or after your pool-maintenance argument. Your choice."

Oona's orange throat patch was blazing. She leaned toward Marsh. "The Siberians. They are always like this. No ceremony. No greeting songs. They arrive and they start talking about the problem. The Amazon frogs find it rude. The Siberians find the Amazon frogs exhausting. This has been going on for about eighty million years."

Vale watched Noll. The courtesy was precise, controlled, and for the first time in Vale's observation, costing something. Noll's damaged forelimb twitched. Something crossed it, a color Vale could not quite identify in the dim light, there and gone. He was managing all of it, and the room had just gotten harder to manage because these frogs did not respond to his color or his cadence or his balanced sentences. They responded to urgency.

Noll yielded the floor. The Siberian leader presented numbers that Vale could not follow, substrate growth rates and displacement energy margins and calibration thresholds, delivered in flat, clipped sentences stripped of every diplomatic courtesy the Amazon frogs wrapped their information in. No "it was felt." No "the coordination suggests." The data is this. The timeline is this. Adjust.

The chamber's colors shifted through a slow, uneasy palette. Several frogs on the upper rings had stopped pretending to be engaged and were staring at the Siberians with the frank discomfort of people whose meeting had been interrupted by someone who did not care about the agenda.

Talla, Vale noticed, was watching the Siberians with a different expression. Not discomfort. Recognition. These were frogs who said what they meant. She leaned toward the cluster of younger frogs beside her and said something low and quick that Vale could not hear but could read in the way two of the younger frogs turned to look at the Siberian leader with renewed interest.

Noll closed the session seven minutes after the Siberians arrived. He closed it the way he did everything, with procedural grace, acknowledging remaining items, scheduling follow-up coordination, thanking participants. But the rhythm was off. The session had been disrupted, genuinely disrupted, not by Talla's objections, which the system was built to absorb, but by frogs from outside the system who did not perform consensus and did not seem interested in learning.

Vale closed his notebook.

"That," he said to Marsh, "was extremely interesting."

They walked back to the biologists' quarters through corridors that Vale was beginning to navigate without thinking about it, which bothered him in the same diffuse way it had days earlier, when he had first stopped noticing the strangeness of the blue-green light. The quarters were a residential chamber that the humans had tried to make livable. Someone, probably Marsh, had arranged their packs along one wall. Reed's equipment covered the stone floor in a pattern that suggested organization but resisted it. The moss sleeping pads were damp. Everything was damp. Vale had stopped expecting otherwise.

Hart was sitting on a stone ledge with her field kit open, running something through a portable test. She had not attended the session. She had, she had told Vale that morning, more productive things to do than watch frogs agree with each other.

"How was parliament?" she said.

"Productive." Vale sat on the opposite ledge, which was wet, and did not react. "In the sense that I now understand how the mechanism works."

"Enlighten me."

"The consensus is real. It's also pre-arranged. About a third of the chamber coordinates before the session. Three frogs on the fifth ring flickered to blue before every vote. Noll facilitates, which means Noll steers. Objections are absorbed, not addressed."

Hart looked up from her test. "So it's a managed democracy. That's not news. Every democracy is managed."

"It's not managed. It's performed. The performance is the management. Her dissent is load-bearing. The system needs it to prove it allows objection."

"What about the Siberians?"

Marsh had come in behind Vale and was sitting cross-legged on a sleeping pad, notebook open. "They arrived mid-session. Through displacement. Five of them. Different from the Amazon frogs. Physically denser, flat coloring, no diplomatic niceties. Their leader told Noll his models were wrong and presented data. No preamble. No ceremony."

"How did Noll handle it?"

"Perfectly," Vale said. "Which is the problem. He yielded the floor, acknowledged their data, closed the session early. It looked like adaptability. It was damage control. The Siberians brought information he didn't want discussed in open session."

Hart set down her test kit. "What information?"

"Something about substrate growth accelerating. The timelines for the Pruning shifting. I couldn't follow the specifics. The point is, the Siberians don't play his game. They don't do consensus theatre. They walk in and say the thing."

"So they're the opposition."

"They're not opposition. They're a different political culture operating within the same system. Noll can manage frogs who share his assumptions about process. He can absorb Talla because she argues within the framework, even when she's arguing against the framework. The Siberians don't argue at all. They present and expect action. It's a different operating system, and his software doesn't run on it."

Hart was quiet for a moment. Then: "So the global coordination is fragile."

"The global coordination is Noll. Which means it's only as stable as his ability to manage cultures he doesn't control."

"And the Pruning requires global coordination."

"Yes."

"And the Pruning is accelerating."

"According to the Siberians, yes."

Hart picked up her test kit. "That's not a governance structure. That's a man holding a building together by being very calm in the lobby."

The entrance to the chamber rustled and Reed came through, damp, excited, his glasses pushed up into his hair and his shirt untucked on one side. He was carrying a portable light that he had apparently borrowed from Krrik's laboratory, and his eyes had the focused, slightly manic quality of someone who had just seen something that reorganized his understanding of a field he had spent ten years in.

"The moss beds," he said.

"We're talking about governance," Vale said.

"The moss beds are doing something. Krrik showed me the calibration arrays for the Pruning. They're alive, obviously, but they're also adapting. The growth patterns are changing in response to the data the Siberians sent. In real time. The computation moss is revising its own models based on new input, and the revision is producing physical changes in the structure of the bed. The moss is thinking about the Pruning and it's growing differently because of what it's thinking."

"Reed," Hart said.

"And the energy draw, Hart, the energy requirements for global displacement coordination during a Pruning are, according to Krrik's estimates, roughly equivalent to the total energy output of."

"Reed."

"What?"

"We're talking about governance."

Reed blinked. He looked at Vale, then at Hart, then at Marsh, who was watching him with an expression that combined affection with the mild exasperation of someone who had seen this exact sequence before. He pushed his glasses down from his hair onto his nose. They were fogged.

"Right," he said. "Governance. Yes. I'll wait."

He sat on the floor, which was damp, and began cleaning his glasses on a shirt that was also damp, which produced no improvement. His leg bounced. He lasted about forty-five seconds.

"The point is that the Pruning is a biological event managed by a computational system that is itself biological, which means the governance and the biology are the same system, which means when you talk about governance you are also talking about the moss beds, which is what I was."

"Reed," said three voices.

Reed cleaned his glasses again.

Quill arrived last, quiet, settling onto a sleeping pad near the entrance. He had been at the Pools. His hands were in his pockets. He listened while Vale summarized the session again for his benefit, shorter this time, the key points only. The pre-coordinated voting. Talla's absorption. The Siberian arrival. Noll's controlled adaptation.

Quill listened. When Vale finished, he said: "And Noll? What's your read?"

Vale had been thinking about this since the session ended. He had been thinking about it since the tour, since the first Speaking Chambers visit, since the first time Noll had paused between sentences and the pause had told Vale more than the sentence.

"He's a dictator who has convinced an entire species he doesn't exist," Vale said. "It's the most sophisticated political trick I've ever seen."

The room was quiet. Reed stopped bouncing his leg. Marsh's pencil paused. Hart looked at Vale with the steady attention of someone storing a sentence for later.

Quill's hands were still in his pockets. "Is it possible he genuinely believes it? The equality. The distributed coordination. Is it possible he believes in the system and the system just happens to make him powerful?"

Vale looked at him. "That would be worse."

---

# Chapter 7: Breakfast, Childcare, Sex, Boredom

The fermentation cellars smelled like someone had buried a forest and then disagreed about how long to leave it.

Marsh followed Oona down a root-walled corridor that sloped into warmth, the air thickening with each step until breathing felt like chewing. The cellars opened into a low, broad chamber lit by the usual blue-green glow, and the smell hit her fully, and she breathed through her mouth for about three seconds before giving up and breathing through her nose because it was going to win anyway.

Oona was already talking.

"The new batch is not right. I have been saying this for four days. Nobody listens. They moved my culture stock to the lower level because of the Pruning preparations, and the humidity is wrong down there, and the temperature, and when I tried to explain this to the logistics committee they told me it was temporary." Her orange throat patch was bright. It had been bright since they left the residential warrens ten minutes ago, which Marsh was learning to interpret as Oona's resting state whenever she had an audience. "Temporary. Everything is temporary. My fermentation is also temporary if they keep it at that temperature."

The cellar was not empty. A knot of frogs had gathered around a row of stone basins where dark, wet cultures grew in layered beds. Two elderly frogs were examining a culture bed with the intensity of sommeliers at a tasting they intended to fail.

"This is sour," the grey-green one said. "This is undeniably sour. Before the last Pruning, the batches from this cellar were balanced. Rich. Complex. This is vinegar with ambitions."

"It is not sour," the brown one said. "Your palate has degraded. You are old and your tongue is dying and you are blaming the batch for the failures of your body."

"My tongue is not dying. My tongue is the most reliable instrument in this cellar. I have been tasting since before you were a tadpole, and I am telling you that this culture has turned, and the reason it has turned is that they changed the moss substrate three cycles ago and nobody consulted the people who actually know what they are doing."

Oona waded in. Not physically, though there was some wading involved, the cellar floor being, like everything in the settlement, slightly wet. She positioned herself between the two elderly frogs with the practiced authority of someone who had arbitrated this exact argument before and would arbitrate it again next week.

"The batch is adequate," Oona said. "It is not the best we have produced. It is not the worst. It is adequate, and arguing about it will not change the humidity, which is the actual problem, which I have been telling the logistics committee for four days."

"Adequate," the grey-green frog repeated. "Adequate is not a standard. Adequate is what you say when you have given up."

"Adequate is what I say when I am tired and the grubs need eating regardless of whether they meet your personal specifications for perfection."

Marsh stood at the edge of the group, her notebook out, her pencil moving. She was sketching the culture beds, the stone basins, the way the frogs arranged themselves around the food production like neighbors around a barbecue. Nobody was going to win. Nobody wanted to. The grubs were going to be eaten regardless, and the argument would resume tomorrow with slightly different phrasing and identical passion.

A younger frog at the far end of the cellar, bright green, barely six centimeters, called out without looking up from a culture bed she was tending. "Oona, your last batch tasted like bark. Everyone says so. Nobody tells you because you get loud."

"I do not get loud. I get specific."

"You get loud and specific. It is a combination."

Oona's throat patch flared. The response involved a detailed comparison of the younger frog's technique to something involving stagnant water that Marsh did not entirely follow but that made the two elderly frogs croak in what was unmistakably laughter.

They left the cellars with Oona carrying a sample of the disputed batch in a small stone vessel, still talking. The corridor outside was busy with morning traffic, frogs moving along the root paths and through the shallow water channels with the unhurried rhythm of commuters who had been commuting through the same tunnels for years.

They passed a shallow pool set into the corridor floor, and Marsh stopped.

Eight frogs sat in the water. The pool was barely deep enough to cover their backs. Some had their eyes closed. Others were eating or watching the corridor traffic pass. Their sides moved in a slow, visible rhythm, expanding and contracting in unison. One frog was eating an insect while half-submerged, chewing and breathing and talking to a neighbor simultaneously, with the ease of someone performing three ordinary activities that happened to include absorbing the world through her skin.

"Morning wetting," Oona said, barely pausing. "You sit. You breathe. You remind your body that it is not separate from the water." She said this the way someone might explain brushing teeth. "My sister does it for an hour every morning now. An hour. Since she joined that consciousness circle she does everything slower. Eating, walking, talking. Especially talking. A conversation with my sister now takes twice as long as it used to, and it used to take too long, because she has always had opinions about everything and now she has the same number of opinions delivered at half the speed."

"Is that common? The slowing down?"

"Common enough. The consciousness circles encourage you to settle into the current. Stop rushing. Let the flow move at its pace, not yours." Oona's tone carried the affectionate exasperation of someone describing a family member's diet. "My sister was already slow. Now she is geological. Last week she told me about a dream and it took forty minutes. The dream was about water. All her dreams are about water now. They used to be about food and arguments, which are better dreams."

They turned down a side corridor that grew warmer as it descended. Marsh could hear something ahead. A constant, low, layered sound made up of small movements, small splashes, small clicks. Life at a pitch too quiet to be called noise and too persistent to be called silence.

The tadpole nursery.

The chamber opened wide and low. The floor was a series of interconnected shallow pools, each at a slightly different temperature, the water clear enough that hundreds of tiny dark shapes were visible from the doorway. Tadpoles. Some barely a centimeter long, others closer to two, darting in quick patterns or drifting near the warm stones at the pools' edges. The air smelled like clean water and wet moss.

Two caretakers were working. One, a broad olive frog with steady hands, was sorting tadpoles between pools, lifting each one in a cupped forelimb and placing it in the water that matched its temperature preference. The other, younger, brown with pale yellow markings, was pressing moss beds into the warm stone at the pool edges.

Oona lowered herself into the nearest pool, submerging to her shoulders, and began helping without announcement or instruction. She knew where things went. Marsh watched her settle a cluster of the smallest tadpoles away from a warm-water inlet where the current was too strong for them, her movements gentle, practiced, entirely ordinary.

"The little ones prefer the edges," the olive caretaker said to Marsh. She had the earnest pride of someone who wanted you to understand that what she did mattered. "Too warm and they develop quickly but their coordination suffers. Too cool and they develop slowly but their color range is broader. We find the balance. Every clutch is different. You learn to read them."

Marsh crouched at the pool's edge. The tadpoles nearest to her scattered, then drifted back when she stayed still. They were dark, almost black, with the faintest suggestion of color along their tails.

"How many caretakers work here?"

"Rotating shifts. Twelve of us for this nursery, another fifteen at the western one. Everyone does time in the nurseries, eventually. Even the engineers." The caretaker's colors warmed, a flush of green-gold spreading across her back. "Some people think the nurseries are simple work. They are not simple work. Simple work is what you call the things you do not understand."

Oona, from her position among the tadpoles, added: "Gren's clutch is developing slowly this season. Gren will not admit this. Gren blames the water temperature. The water temperature is fine. Gren's clutch is developing slowly because Gren's clutch is developing slowly. These things happen."

A neighboring frog, a caretaker from the next pool over, leaned in. "Gren's clutch has always been slow. It is not the water. It is genetics, but if you say genetics to Gren she accuses you of judgment."

"Is it judgment?"

"It is observation. Observation is not judgment. But Gren does not see the difference when the observation is about her clutch."

Marsh was helping Oona sort a group of medium-sized tadpoles into a slightly warmer pool when Hart arrived.

She came through the entrance with her field kit over one shoulder and the expression of someone who expected to find something unsatisfactory. Her dark hair was damp. Everything was damp. Hart had stopped commenting on the dampness, which Marsh recognized as a milestone.

"The water supply for these pools," Hart said, addressing the room. "Where does it come from?"

The olive caretaker turned. Her colors, which had been warm and open, shifted to something cooler. "The eastern channel. Fed by the main filtration system."

"And the filtration system. Is it tested? For parasitic load, bacterial contamination, waterborne pathogens?"

The caretaker looked at Hart the way a chef looks at a health inspector who has arrived at a kitchen that has been operating successfully for a hundred years. "The water is clean."

"I'm not asking if it's clean. I'm asking if it's tested."

"The water is clean. The filtration was designed by engineers who understood water before your species understood fire. The tadpoles are healthy. You may observe this."

Hart crouched at the pool's edge and took a water sample in a small vial from her kit. The younger caretaker watched this with open offense.

"You are testing our water."

"I'm collecting a sample."

"In front of the tadpoles."

Hart looked up. "The tadpoles don't know what a sample vial is."

"The tadpoles are in our care. We maintain this nursery. The water is balanced, the temperature is regulated, and the moss is changed on a seven-day cycle. We have not lost a clutch in four seasons. You are standing in a nursery with a perfect record and you are putting our water in a container as though you expect to find something wrong with it."

"I expect to find out what's in it. That's different."

"It is not different to us."

Oona leaned toward Marsh, her throat patch bright. "She does this. Your Hart. She did this at the bathing pools last week. Tested the water and told Renn that the mineral balance was off and Renn has not spoken to her since. Renn has maintained those pools for nine years. You do not tell Renn the minerals are off."

Hart pocketed the vial and stood. The tadpoles swam, indifferent to the diplomatic incident occurring above them.

"Thank you," Hart said, in a tone that was, for Hart, conciliatory. "The nursery is clearly well maintained."

The olive caretaker's colors did not warm. "Yes. It is."

Hart left. The olive caretaker returned to sorting tadpoles with pointed, deliberate competence.

"She means well," Marsh said.

"She means accurately," the olive caretaker said. "That is not the same as well."

They stayed in the nursery for another hour. Marsh stopped taking notes after the first twenty minutes. She helped Oona move a clutch of the smallest tadpoles to a fresh pool, cupping them in her hands, their tiny bodies cool and flickering against her palms. The caretakers watched her do this and said nothing, which felt like approval.

They walked from the nursery into the residential warrens, and the sound changed. The nursery had been a contained, humid quiet. The warrens were everything else. Croaking, clicking, conversations happening through openings between chambers that flowed into each other like rooms in a house where someone had forgotten to install doors.

Oona moved through the warrens like someone walking through her own kitchen. A pair of young frogs on a ledge above the corridor were watching foot traffic with the bored attention of teenagers sitting on a fence, and Oona told Marsh their names and the fact that one of them had recently begun practicing mating calls that the neighbors considered premature and structurally unsound.

"He is too young," Oona said. "His vocal sac is not fully developed and the harmonics are thin. It sounds like a complaint, not a call. His mother has spoken to him about it but he continues, and the frog three chambers down has asked him to stop because her partner finds it distracting, and the partner says he does not find it distracting, he finds it amusing, and now they are arguing about whether finding it amusing is the same as finding it distracting, which it is not, but try telling her that."

"What makes a good mating call?"

Oona's throat patch blazed. This was, apparently, a topic.

"Depth. Resonance. The best calls make the pool vibrate. But that is only the sound. There is also the display. Colors. You want range, control. A good display moves through four, five shades in a sequence that shows you can hold each one without bleeding into the next. Sloppy transitions suggest a sloppy mind. That is what they say. Personally I think sloppy transitions suggest a frog who is nervous, and nervous is not the same as sloppy, but standards are standards."

"And the arguments? About what counts as attractive?"

"Constant. Everyone has an opinion. My partner, when he was alive, he had a very traditional call. Low, steady, three colors held long. Some frogs thought it was old-fashioned. I thought it was honest. He did not need to show off. He just needed to show up." A brief pause. Her colors shifted to something softer. Then back. "That was four years ago. It sounds like a long time in your counting but it is not so long in ours. We live fast. We grieve fast. It does not mean we grieve less."

Quill appeared at the far end of the corridor. He paused when he heard Oona talking, then sat on a root ledge nearby. His hands were in his pockets.

"Is the call taught or instinctive?" he asked.

Oona's throat brightened. Nobody from the coordination committee or the engineering team ever asked about mating calls. "Both. The basic call is instinctive. Every frog can croak. But the shaping, the refinement, that is taught. Mothers teach. Caretakers teach. A frog with no teaching can call, but the call is rough. Like speech without grammar."

Quill's hands moved in his pockets. "So there's a cultural transmission of aesthetic standards. Beauty is partly biological and partly inherited socially."

"I do not know what cultural transmission means. I know that my mother taught me what a good call sounds like and I taught my sister's children and they will teach theirs. If that is what you are saying, then yes."

Quill nodded slowly, the way he nodded when something was settling into a place he had been preparing for it. "Thank you, Oona. That's genuinely fascinating."

"Of course it is fascinating. It is the most important thing we do and nobody ever asks about it." Her throat was very bright. "You are looking for Vesh?"

"I am."

"He is at the Third Pool. He is always at the Third Pool. He will tell you something about the universe being a puddle and you will find it interesting. Go. But come back and ask me more questions. Your friends ask about politics and technology. You ask about how we live. This is better."

Quill smiled, a small, genuine thing, and moved on down the corridor.

Oona watched him go. "I like that one. He listens with his whole body. The tall one, your Vale, he listens with his arguments already prepared. This one listens to hear."

They settled on a root ledge near Oona's sleeping chamber, indistinguishable from every other sleeping space except for a small stone vessel of fermented grubs that Oona kept tucked into a root nook and defended with the territorial instinct of a frog who understood that communal ownership had its limits.

"The engineer three warrens down," Oona said, gesturing vaguely to the left. "She sleeps alone. Alone. She built a root partition across her chamber entrance. Not a wall, technically, but a suggestion of a wall. People talk about it."

"Is that unusual?"

"It is very unusual. We sleep near each other. We always have. The warmth, the sound, the, you know." She gestured at her own skin. "You feel people around you even when you sleep. Their breathing, their chemistry. It is not a choice you make. It is how bodies work when they are close. Sleeping alone means you wake up with only your own chemistry, your own breathing. People say it makes you dry. Closed. She says it helps her think. She is an excellent engineer. She is also strange, and everyone agrees about both of those things."

Marsh wrote in her notebook. Not field observations. Not data. Just the sentence: *sleeping alone makes you dry.* It was the kind of fact that could only arrive through sitting on a root ledge with someone who trusted you enough to say the ordinary thing instead of the impressive one.

A frog passed by in the corridor carrying a bundle of moss. She stopped, looked at Marsh, looked at Oona, and said: "The contentment of daily life is the highest form of the flow."

Oona blinked. "Thank you, Dess. Nobody asked."

"I am just saying."

"You are always just saying. Go and deliver your moss."

Dess went.

"She does that. Pronouncements. She has been attending Vesh's breathing circles three times a week and now she dispenses wisdom like someone handing out damp leaves. Last week she told me that suffering is a temporary shape and I told her my joints hurt and she said the joints are also temporary and I said my joints hurt now, not temporarily, now, and she said now is also temporary, and I asked her to leave."

Marsh laughed. Actually laughed, not the polite exhale of a researcher maintaining rapport, but the real sound, startling in the low warren corridor. Two frogs in a nearby chamber looked up.

"Oona," Marsh said. She was not sure why she asked. It was not in her notebook, not on any list. "Are you happy?"

Oona went still. Her throat patch dimmed, then held at a steady, middling orange.

"I am content," she said. "Is that the same thing? I do not always know. But the fermented grubs are good this season and my joints do not hurt today, so perhaps it does not matter." She looked at Marsh with both eyes, which frogs did when something was serious and worth the focus. "Content is not a small thing. Content is knowing that the water is warm and the nursery is full and the arguments about fermentation will continue tomorrow because nobody has died and nobody has left and the batch, even the inadequate batch, will be eaten. Is that happy? You tell me. You are the one who studies how things live."

A frog passed behind them in the corridor. He overheard enough to stop. "Contentment is the puddle accepting its depth," he said.

"Go away, Mott," Oona said.

Mott went away.

Oona looked at Marsh. "You see what I deal with."

They sat. Somewhere nearby, the young frog practiced his mating call again, the thin harmonics carrying through the root walls like a teenager learning guitar in the next apartment.

Then a caretaker Marsh did not recognize came through the corridor, moving quickly. She was carrying a bundle of bedding and two small containers. Behind her, another frog. Then three more. They were heading toward the eastern section of the warrens, and they were not talking.

Oona watched them pass. Her throat patch dimmed.

"The section past the fourth junction is being cleared," she said. "For the staging preparations. Equipment, coordination, the frogs coming in from the outer districts." Her voice was light. Deliberately light. "They always move us when it is time. It is temporary. We go to the western warrens, or the overflow chambers near the Pools. It is not far."

A family group passed. Two adults and three very young frogs riding on the larger one's back, gripping the wet skin with the tiny, determined hold of creatures who had decided to trust the carrier.

"Is it always like this?" Marsh asked. "Before a Pruning?"

Oona did not answer immediately. Her colors had shifted. The warm olive-green had cooled, and underneath it, flickers of something Marsh could not name.

"It is always something," Oona said. "Every time. Different rooms, different families, but always the moving, always the making space. Always the look on the young ones' faces when you carry them somewhere new and tell them it is fine."

She looked at the corridor where the last of the families had passed. Her throat patch was almost dark.

"It is fine," she said.

---

# Chapter 8: Competitive Vibrations

The arena had been designed by someone who understood that the best sporting events are the ones you can feel in your kidneys.

Marsh sat on a root ledge above the largest pool she had seen in the settlement, her notebook balanced on her knee, her trousers soaked past the point of caring. Below her, packed into interconnected basins that stepped down toward a central channel, somewhere around eighty frogs watched the competition with the focused intensity of a crowd that had been arguing about the rules for forty million years and intended to keep arguing for forty million more.

The game, as far as she could piece together, worked like this: two teams of four frogs positioned themselves at opposite ends of a water channel, and one team produced a vibrational pattern, a sequence of pulses that traveled through the water with enough force to make the stone ledge hum beneath Marsh's thighs. The opposing team had to decode the pattern, replicate it with a variation, and send it back. The variation had to be, according to the rules, "structurally responsive but creatively distinct."

What counted as "structurally responsive but creatively distinct" was, as far as Marsh could tell, the subject of ongoing litigation.

A frog on the near team pressed low into the water and produced a rolling pulse that Marsh felt in her sternum. The counter came back within seconds, a modified rhythm with a syncopated accent on the third beat. The crowd turned shades of approving green. A cluster of elderly frogs went blue with technical admiration. One of them leaned toward his neighbor and said something that made the neighbor turn orange with disagreement so vivid it was practically a rebuttal.

"That was clean," Oona said from beside Marsh, her throat patch glowing. She had been providing commentary since they arrived. Silence during a sporting event was, for Oona, a moral failure. "The return variation was clean. Good accent on the third beat. Now watch, because the judges will call a foul."

"Why?"

"Because the third-beat accent is disputed. The current rules say structural accents must fall on even beats. But the previous rule committee changed it to odd beats, and the committee before that did not specify, and the committee before that dissolved in a jurisdictional argument about whether beats should be counted from the pulse origin or the pulse arrival, which are not the same thing because water has drag, and the drag varies with temperature, and." She stopped. "Nobody knows the rules. The rules have been under continuous revision for approximately forty million years. The rule committee meets once a season and produces a document that contradicts the previous document, and then a new committee produces a document explaining that the contradiction was intentional, and then a third committee forms to investigate whether the explanation was authorized."

An officiating frog at the pool's edge raised a forelimb. A low click carried across the arena.

The crowd erupted.

Not in sound, or not only in sound. Colors rolled through the packed basins like weather, waves of outraged amber and indignant yellow rippling from three separate sections simultaneously. A frog on the upper ledge turned a shade of orange so intense it looked like a medical event. Three frogs in the middle section began croaking in overlapping rhythms that were unmistakably the frog equivalent of "you cannot be serious." The officiating frog absorbed it all with the flat composure of someone who had made this call before and did not care about any of them.

"I told you," Oona said. "Foul. Third-beat accent. Even beats only."

"But you just said the rule was changed to odd beats."

"It was changed back. Or forward. There is some disagreement about whether the current revision is the revision or the reversion. The rule committee has opinions. Nobody reads the opinions. The games continue." She watched the officiating frog with open admiration. "Fenn is very brave. She officiated the last three tournaments. After the second one, someone left a dead beetle in her sleeping chamber as a protest. She ate it."

Reed was two ledges below, sitting at the water's edge with his feet in the pool, glasses fogged. He was talking to a medium-sized green frog who was not so much watching the competition as sitting near it.

"The propagation rate," Reed was saying. "Through interconnected water bodies at this temperature, with this mineral content, the vibrational frequency should dissipate within, what, eight meters? Ten? But the channel extends at least twenty meters and the signal clarity at the far end is still." He paused. "How is the signal clarity still that high?"

The green frog looked at him. "Because the channel is good."

"Right, but physically. The medium is water. Water absorbs vibrational energy at a rate proportional to."

"The channel is very good. They built it well."

"Who built it?"

"Someone. Before my time. It works well. Are you enjoying the game?"

Reed pushed his glasses up. "Enormously. But the propagation."

"You are thinking too hard about the water. Watch the game. Bett is about to do the descending spiral. Nobody does the descending spiral anymore. It is old-fashioned but when it works it is beautiful."

Reed turned to watch. He lasted about four seconds before reaching for his notebook.

On the far side of the arena, on a dry stone ledge conspicuously above the waterline, Hart sat with her legs crossed and her field kit on her lap. The only person in the arena, human or frog, who was not wet. Two frogs sat near her, maintaining a safe but curious distance.

Hart's left hand was gesturing at the communal pools below. The two frogs were looking at the pools, then at Hart, then at each other.

One of them turned alarmed green. The other turned amused brown.

"She is telling them about parasites," Oona said. "Your Hart. She told Menn last week that communal pools at this temperature are ideal breeding conditions for seventeen different parasitic organisms. Menn has not been in the pools since. Menn's partner is furious."

"Is she wrong?"

"She is probably not wrong. She is also not helpful. We have been sitting in these pools for two hundred million years. If the parasites were going to kill us, they would have done it. But your Hart says it as though she has just discovered death and wants to make sure everyone knows."

The alarmed frog on Hart's ledge stood up, looked at the pools, and sat back down. His colors had gone a dull, grieving grey. The amused frog had not moved.

"That one is Gorr," Oona said. "He sits on dry ledges at every competition. Not because of parasites. Because he does not like being touched. He will tell your Hart she is the first sensible person he has met, and they will sit together being dry, and it will be the most intimate friendship either of them has had in years."

Below them, teams rotated. The new team on the near end included a small, very dark frog who produced vibrations with a precision that made the water surface shiver in geometric patterns. The crowd went quiet. Colors shifted to attentive blue-green across the arena, a wave of focus passing through the audience like a breath. For a moment, everyone was the same color.

Quill was sitting three ledges away, visible in profile, his hands in his pockets, watching the crowd instead of the game. His head moved slowly across the packed basins, tracking the color shifts, the synchronized waves that rolled through the spectators whenever something happened in the channel. The crowd's colors moving together, involuntarily, like a current passing through them.

His face had gone still. Whatever he was recognizing in the crowd's colors, it did not seem to please him.

Marsh filed it. Quill got it when something that should be simple turned out to contain something else, and he was not sure yet what.

The game ended. Or paused. Immediately a knot of frogs near the middle basin began arguing about whether the final sequence had been a legal variation or an illegal repetition, and the argument generated its own audience, and the audience generated opinions, and the opinions branched into secondary arguments like tributaries from a river that had no interest in reaching the sea.

A frog climbed onto a ledge near the central basin and began delivering a formal complaint about scheduling. The vibration organizers, four frogs wearing their authority in the form of colors so rigid they looked painted on, listened with the patience of officials already composing their response.

"The fermentation competition," Oona said, standing up. "This is Grenn. He organizes the vibration tournaments. He is upset because the fermentation tasting was scheduled the same evening, which splits the attendance. He has been upset about this for three seasons. The fermentation committee says the conflict is unavoidable. Grenn says they are doing it on purpose. They are not. But try telling Grenn. He will argue about this until one of them dies, and the fermentation committee has more members, so the numbers are against him, but Grenn has conviction, which is worse."

The adjacent chamber was connected to the arena by a waterway that smelled like someone had concentrated every fermentation cellar in the settlement into a single room and then disagreed about the results.

Dozens of small batches in carved stone vessels lined the chamber's edges, each tended by a frog who stood beside their entry with the nervous pride of a baker at a county fair. Three judges moved among the entries with slow deliberation, tasting everything, enjoying nothing.

Oona positioned herself beside her entry, both forelimbs on the stone slab, throat patch at maximum brightness. "This batch is exceptional. I adjusted the moss substrate after they moved my culture stock. The lower level has worse humidity but better mineral seepage, and the minerals changed the fermentation in ways that were initially catastrophic and then became interesting and then became, I believe, superior."

"What does it taste like?"

"Perseverance," Oona said. "Also, slightly of copper."

Three vessels down, Krrik stood beside a colleague presenting a batch. His mottled skin was cycling through rapid, irritated color shifts. The colleague did not appear to notice. Or had stopped noticing years ago.

"This is not chemistry," Krrik said. He was looking at the tasting entries with the expression of a physicist at a horoscope convention. "This is decay with pretensions. You are cultivating rot and calling it cuisine."

"It is fermentation," the colleague said.

"Fermentation is controlled decomposition. This is not controlled. This is optimistic decomposition. You have placed organic material in a vessel and hoped."

"I have placed organic material in a vessel and managed the conditions for six weeks."

"You have managed nothing. You have observed. Observation is not management. The microorganisms did the work. You watched." Krrik's shorter hind leg shifted, and he resettled himself with the asymmetric precision that was as much his signature as his irritability. "If you presented this in my laboratory, I would ask you to identify the variables you controlled, and you would say 'the vessel' and 'the location,' and I would ask you to leave."

The colleague tasted his own batch, considered it, and offered some to Krrik.

Krrik recoiled. "No."

"It is good."

"It is decomposed insects in mineral water."

"Yes. That is what fermentation is."

"I know what fermentation is. I object to calling it food."

"You object to everything."

"I object to imprecision. This is imprecise food."

The colleague ate his batch with visible satisfaction. Krrik watched him eat it, every color on his body communicating that he was witnessing a grave and voluntary error.

One of the judges arrived at Oona's entry, a small pale frog whose spots shifted through careful greens as she tasted. She held the sample for a long moment. Oona's throat patch was so bright it was nearly yellow.

"The mineral profile is unusual," the judge said.

"The lower cellar," Oona said. "Better seepage."

"The acidity is high."

"The acidity is intentional."

"I did not say it was not intentional. I said it was high."

Oona's colors shifted in the rapid sequence of a defense attorney's opening statement. "The acidity complements the copper notes. The copper comes from the mineral seepage. The seepage is a function of the location, which was forced on me by the logistics committee, which moved my culture stock without consulting me, so the acidity is, in a sense, the logistics committee's contribution, and I think we can all agree that the logistics committee's contributions are rarely intentional but occasionally excellent."

The judge moved on without committing to an opinion. Oona watched her go.

"She liked it," Oona said.

"How do you know?"

"Her spots held green. If she disliked it, the spots would have gone grey. If she was neutral, she would have said 'adequate,' which is what judges say when they want to move on without insulting you to your face."

A frog at the far end of the tasting chamber dropped a sample vessel into the water channel. The vessel sank. The frog looked at it. A neighboring frog looked at it.

"Wet in, wet out," the neighboring frog said.

Several frogs nearby turned the color of suppressed laughter.

They drifted back toward the arena, where the vibration games had resumed, or had never stopped, or had evolved into something that was partly competition and partly argument and partly the natural state of eighty frogs in a shared space with strong opinions about rhythm.

The courtship display was happening at the arena's southern edge, in a shallow pool separated from the main channel by a low stone ridge. A young frog, bright green and trembling with effort, was performing.

His skin moved through colors in a sequence that was partly rehearsed and partly improvised. Greens deepening to blue, blue cycling to a warm amber, the amber held for a long moment before shifting through three shades of violet in quick succession. The transitions between amber and violet wobbled slightly, the way a singer's voice wavers on a high note they are not quite sure they can reach.

A female frog on the far ledge watched him. Her colors held neutral. You watched, you assessed, you gave nothing away. The neutrality was itself a display: impress me.

Three frogs near the young suitor were coaching. Loudly. Publicly. Without any indication that this was unusual.

"Too much amber in the third sequence," said the first, an older frog with faded green coloring that suggested she had been coaching since before the suitor was a tadpole. "The amber overwhelms. Pull it back."

"Your grandmother did the cascade better," said the second, a compact frog with an air of personal investment in the outcome. "She did the violet transition in two beats, not three. Three looks hesitant."

"Ignore them," said the third, younger, brighter, leaning forward with the conviction of someone who knew exactly what they were talking about regardless of whether they did. "The amber is bold. Bold is good. Nobody remembers the cautious ones."

The young frog held his amber. It shivered at the edges.

"Pull it back," said the first coach.

"Bolder," said the third.

"Your grandmother," said the second, "would be mortified."

The young frog let the amber go and cycled into a rapid descent through blues and greens that was either a deliberate stylistic choice or a panicked retreat. The crowd near the courtship pool turned various shades of opinion. A frog two ledges up offered an unsolicited critique to nobody in particular. A frog beside her disagreed. A third told them both to be quiet because the boy was doing his best.

Oona leaned toward Marsh. "The amber was good. Not traditional, but good.
