The cascade was sloppy, but he is young, and young frogs always rush the cascade because they think speed compensates for control, which it does not, but by the time they learn this they are old and their color range has narrowed and they do the cascade slowly and call it dignity."

"What does she think?"

"She thinks he is interesting. Her forelimbs are forward. If she was bored, they would be tucked. If she was offended, her spots would darken. She is watching closely, which is the best a young frog can hope for on a first display."

"Is it always this public?"

"How else would you do it? You display. People see. People help. A private display is just practice. A public display is a commitment. You are saying: this is who I am, and I am willing to be seen being it, and if the amber wobbles then the amber wobbles and that is part of the honesty."

Marsh watched the young frog try again. The three coaches offered conflicting advice simultaneously. The young frog's colors flickered with what might have been irritation or gratitude or both.

On the far side of the arena, Vale was sitting on a ledge with his notebook. He had not been watching the games or the courtship. He had been watching where the frogs sat.

The frogs did not have assigned seating. The best positions were claimed by arrival, except that the same frogs arrived first every time. When Marsh had asked Oona about this, Oona had said that certain frogs simply preferred certain spots and everyone knew this and it was not a ranking, it was a habit, and habits are not the same as hierarchy, except that they produce exactly the same result, which Oona said without appearing to notice she had said it.

Vale would be writing that down. His note would say something about informal seniority systems, and it would be correct, and it would also miss something, which was that the frogs at the edges were having just as good a time as the frogs near the center.

She turned back to the courtship pool. The young frog had finished his display, or been advised to stop by the first coach, who had placed a forelimb on his back with the firm gentleness of someone ending an audition. The neutral frog on the far ledge held her composure for another moment, then let her colors shift, just slightly, to a warmer green. The second coach said something about the grandmother. The third clapped the young frog on the back. His skin went through six colors in a second, none of them deliberate.

The evening wound down. The vibration games continued at reduced intensity, the remaining competitors playing to a crowd that had thinned and softened. The fermentation tasting produced a winner that Marsh did not catch because Oona was too busy objecting to the judging criteria to report the results. Conversations broke into smaller clusters along the ledges and in the shallow pools.

The attendance was lower than usual. Oona had mentioned this early in the evening, casually, the way she said things she wanted to seem casual about. "Fewer people this season. The staging preparations. People are tired." Her throat patch had dimmed when she said it, and she had brightened it deliberately. Near the back of the arena, a group of frogs sat together in the upper basin, quiet, colors muted. Operators from the staging grounds. They were not watching the games. They were present because the community was gathering and this is what you did, even when your body wanted to be somewhere horizontal and dark. One of them had her eyes closed. They had come. That was the thing.

Marsh put her notebook away.

Quill was still watching the crowd. During the final moments of the last game, when the winning team produced a vibration that made the central channel ring like a bell, the audience had reacted. All of them. At once. Their colors had shifted together, dozens of frogs turning the same shade of surprised gold in the same instant, involuntary and gone before it fully registered.

Like the breathing circles, Marsh thought. Except this was a game.

Quill had seen it. His hands were in his pockets, his body very still, watching the crowd's colors settle back into their separate patterns.

She stood. Her legs were stiff. Oona was arguing with a neighboring frog about whether the fermentation judges had confused complexity with acidity, a distinction both frogs considered critically important and that the judges would probably have to answer for at next season's committee meeting and possibly for the rest of their natural lives.

The young suitor from the courtship pool walked past with two of his coaches. His colors were calm. The amber was gone. He looked tired and pleased and slightly dazed.

---

# Chapter 9: Show Me the Mechanism

The lab smelled like a compost heap that had been struck by lightning.

Reed stood in the doorway trying to locate the source of the ozone and failing, because the source was everywhere. A faint hum in his sinuses and the fillings of his back teeth, the kind of charge he would have attributed to faulty wiring if anything in this room had wires. Nothing did. The closest thing to a wire was a thread of pale fungus connecting two moss beds across a gap of three meters, and the moss beds were, according to Krrik, thinking.

"Not thinking the way you mean thinking," Krrik said without looking up. He was crouched over a shallow stone basin at the center of the room, adjusting something inside it with both forelimbs. His mottled brown-green skin was dusted with spore residue, the same color as the residue on every surface in the lab, which gave the impression that Krrik and his workspace had been growing into each other for years. His shorter hind leg was braced at an angle against the basin's edge, the asymmetric stance so habitual that it looked more stable than standing straight. "Thinking the way thinking works. Chemical gradients. Electrical propagation through living tissue. Pattern recognition at molecular density. Not thoughts. Processing."

"Right, right, but the output. The output is functionally equivalent to."

"The output is not equivalent to anything you have. Do not compare it to your computers. Your computers are arithmetic performed on sand. This is." Krrik paused. A sharp orange flicker crossed his skin. "There is no analogy in your framework. It is what it is."

Reed pushed his glasses up. The lab stretched in three directions, built into and grown out of the root system, with no clear walls, only gradual transitions from workspace to corridor to what appeared to be another experiment. Half-finished devices sat on stone ledges alongside what looked like lunch. Near the far wall, four moss beds lay in tiered stone containers, each bed a different shade of green, each one faintly luminous, and each one apparently containing more processing power than every computer on the planet combined, being kept alive by a drip-feed irrigation system made of hollowed-out roots.

The drip system was leaking. A bead of nutrient solution fell from a cracked root joint and splashed onto the moss bed. The moss rippled. Not from the impact. The ripple moved outward in a pattern too regular to be hydraulic, too fast to be growth, and too purposeful to be random. The color shifted from pale green to something warmer for about two seconds, then settled back.

"Wait, wait, wait." Reed stepped closer. "Did it just reconfigure its own architecture based on that chemical input? Because that's not a computer, that's a, that's a living system that rewrites itself in response to."

"It is doing what it does. Please do not touch it."

Reed pulled his hand back.

"I was not going to touch it."

"You were reaching. This array is calibrated for voss growth modeling. If you introduce your skin oils it will spend three days processing the chemical signature of primate sweat instead of doing something useful."

A young frog appeared in the corridor opening. Small, bright green, carrying a flat stone tablet covered in markings Reed could not read. Her colors were cycling through nervous greys.

"Dr. Krrik. The modulation report from the western sector."

Krrik did not look up. "Summary."

"Growth rates within projected parameters for subsections one through six. Seven through twelve show fourteen percent above median, with structural anomalies in the deep root layer that."

"Incompetent."

The grey in her colors deepened.

"The anomalies are genuine," she said.

"The anomalies are genuine and the analysis is incompetent. They are measuring surface density when the growth is propagating through the mineral layer. I told Henn this four days ago. Did Henn adjust the sensor depth?"

"I do not know."

"Then find out."

The young frog left. Her colors had not recovered.

Krrik hopped from the basin to a stone ledge beside the largest moss bed. The hop was lopsided, his shorter leg producing a distinctive hitch that he navigated with the automatic ease of someone who had stopped noticing it decades ago. He pressed one forelimb flat against the moss surface.

The bed responded. Colors shifted through the green in a slow wave, and something moved beneath the surface. Tiny threads of light, branching and reconnecting, tracing paths that looked almost like neurons firing in a brain scan, except the brain was a meter-wide patch of living moss in a stone container in a room that smelled like ozone and compost.

"It's modeling the voss growth."

"Obviously."

"In real time."

"In something more useful than real time. It is modeling probability spaces. Fourteen hundred variables. Atmospheric chemistry, geothermal flux, cosmic radiation intensity, root system connectivity, water table depth, spore count, mineral composition. Your species models weather. This models the behavior of a living planetary substrate that has been growing for two hundred million years." Krrik removed his forelimb. "And the models are failing."

He said this the way Reed might say that his coffee was cold. A fact. An inconvenience. But underneath the flatness, his skin was cycling through browns faster than Reed had seen before, an unconscious churn of processing that his body broadcast even when his voice did not.

"Why are they failing?"

"Because the growth is not following established patterns. The substrate is behaving as though the radiation peak is three times the projected intensity, but our astronomical monitoring confirms the peak is within historical range. Something else is accelerating the growth. I do not know what. The models do not know what. This is not a comfortable scientific position."

A thick-bodied frog appeared at the main corridor entrance. Dull yellow, officious coloring held rigid. A middle manager in any species.

"Dr. Krrik. The coordination committee is meeting in forty minutes. Your attendance is requested for the displacement scheduling review."

"No."

"The committee requires input on displacement capacity for the eastern sector Pruning operations."

"The committee can read the capacity report I submitted two days ago. The numbers have not changed. Tell the committee I am working."

The logistics frog absorbed this. She had delivered this message before. She had received this answer before. "I will relay your response."

"Relay also that if they schedule another meeting during peak modeling hours I will submit my capacity reports in a Threnn-song dialect the committee members are too young to read."

The logistics frog left.

Reed sat on a root stool near the largest moss bed. The stool was uncomfortable, built for bodies that squatted rather than slouched.

"The displacement mechanism. Can I see it?"

Krrik looked at him for the first time in several minutes. Both eyes. "Why."

"Because I want to understand how you move matter across a continent using technology that looks like rocks in a puddle."

"The file on your species describes you as intermittently clever primates with an impressive tolerance for self-destruction. Your technology assessment takes up less space than your conflict history." He hopped down from the ledge. "Come."

The displacement apparatus was in an alcove off the main lab, separated by a root wall with a gap sized for frogs. Reed crouched, ducked, and eventually crawled through on his hands and knees into a space he could barely stand upright in.

It was a ring of stones. Dark, wet, each one the size of his fist, arranged in a circle roughly two meters across. Between and across them ran a network of fungal threads, pale and faintly luminous. The threads pulsed with a rhythm so slow it was almost invisible. The whole arrangement sat in a shallow depression filled with about two centimeters of clear water.

The most advanced transportation technology on Earth looked like a rock garden someone had forgotten to drain.

"It looks like wet rocks."

"It looks like what it needs to look like." Krrik settled beside the ring. His forelimbs rested on the nearest stone. "What did you expect?"

"I don't know. Something that looked like it could fold space."

"It does not fold space. That is your analogy. Your species uses 'fold' because you imagine space as a flat thing that bends. Space is not flat and it does not bend. What this does is." His skin went through three shades of brown. "Do you understand resonance at the substrate level? Harmonic correspondence between molecular states across distance?"

"I understand resonance. I do not understand how wet rocks produce it at scales that should require more energy than the sun."

"The energy comes from the voss. The stones are interface. The fungal network is conductor. The water is medium. The operator is the." Orange flashed across his skin. "I do not have a word in your language. The operator is the thing that makes the intention real. Not consciousness. Not will. Something between. Your language does not have a word because your species does not have the capacity."

Reed pushed his glasses up, wiped them on his shirt, pushed them back. "You're saying the mechanism requires a cognitive input that humans cannot produce?"

"I am saying that explaining this to you is like explaining color to a species that sees in two dimensions. I can describe it. You can understand the description. You cannot experience the referent."

A voice from the main lab, rich and unhurried.

"The frustration is the teaching."

Krrik's colors went solid orange. Reed turned. Brother Vesh sat on a ledge near the alcove entrance, compact, vivid emerald green with gold flecks that caught the bioluminescence. One eye slightly larger than the other. He was eating something small and winged.

"I was looking for Pem," Vesh said. "But I heard you explaining displacement and thought I would help."

"You cannot help. You do not understand displacement."

"I understand displacement as the universe understands it. As flow. As the natural tendency of coherent matter to respond to coherent intention. The stones do not move things because of physics. They move things because the universe is already moving, and the stones are how we ask politely."

"That is not what happens."

"That is exactly what happens, described correctly. You describe it with substrate resonance and harmonic correspondence, which are accurate and incomplete. I describe it with flow and intention, which are imprecise and complete. Together we are almost useful."

"We are not together. You wandered in. I did not invite you. The stones operate on measurable principles that do not require your cosmological narration."

"And yet the operators who meditate before displacement achieve fourteen percent better accuracy than those who do not. This is in your own data."

Krrik's entire body went a shade of orange so vivid it was almost red. "That is a correlation. Correlation is not causation. Meditation affects neurochemistry. Neurochemistry affects operator function. This is biology, not the Croaking."

"The Croaking is biology. That is my point." Vesh ate the rest of the insect, chewing with serene deliberation. "The universe does not recognize the distinctions you insist on making. Engineering, metaphysics, biology. These are filing cabinets. The universe does not file."

"The universe also does not eat insects during scientific discussions."

"The universe does whatever the universe does. I am part of the universe. The insect was part of the universe. Now the insect is part of me. This is also displacement, of a kind." Vesh's gold flecks intensified. He looked enormously pleased with himself. "I will leave you. Your human is very attentive. He reminds me of a tadpole discovering water. Tell him about the growth problem. He may see it differently."

Vesh hopped from the ledge and vanished into the corridor, having contributed nothing to the engineering discussion and having irritated Krrik with a precision that suggested long practice.

Krrik sat still. His colors cooled slowly from orange through amber to his usual mottled brown.

"I apologize," he said.

"For what?"

"For Vesh. For his existence. For the fact that he says things that are wrong in ways that sound right, and I cannot refute him without engaging his framework, and engaging his framework requires accepting premises I do not accept. He knows this. It delights him."

Reed grinned. "He's not entirely wrong, though. About the meditation correlation."

"Do not start."

They went back to the main lab. Two things had changed. First, a new cluster of frogs had gathered around the largest moss bed, five of them, all engineers by the look of them, skin cycling through anxious greens as they studied what the moss was displaying. Second, Marsh was standing near the entrance with Oona, who was looking at the lab the way someone might look at a room where a small, controlled disaster had been lovingly curated over many years.

"Krrik," Oona said. Her orange throat patch was very bright. "This is why engineers should not live alone. I have seen cleaner moss trenches."

"I do not live here."

"You do not live anywhere. You exist near equipment. There is something growing in that water channel that I do not think you put there on purpose."

"It is a controlled mycological experiment."

"It smells like an uncontrolled mycological experiment." She turned to Marsh. "He is brilliant and his workspace looks like something died in it. He will tell you it is all intentional and labeled. He will be lying about the labeled part."

Krrik's skin flickered. "The labeling system is internal."

"The labeling system is in your head. When you die, nobody will find anything in here." She looked around the lab with frank assessment. "I do not understand anything in here and I do not want to. Marsh wanted to see the moss beds."

Marsh was already at the tiered stone containers, crouching to look at the largest bed. She did not reach for it. She had heard the skin-oils lecture from the corridor, apparently.

"It's warm," she said. "Is it supposed to be warm?"

"It is alive," Krrik said. "Living things are warm. This is not a revelation."

Marsh looked at it for a long moment. Marsh was not trying to understand the mechanism. She was trying to understand what it was like to tend a living thing that thought.

"Do you talk to them?" she asked.

Krrik's orange flashed so fast it barely registered. "They are computation media. They do not have opinions. They do not have feelings. They process inputs and generate outputs. I do not talk to them."

"He talks to them," Oona said. "Everyone knows this."

Krrik said nothing, which was, Reed thought, louder than anything he could have said.

Oona and Marsh left. The lab settled back into its baseline hum, the moss beds pulsing, the drip system leaking, the fungal threads carrying information or nutrients or both between surfaces that looked dead and were not.

Reed joined the cluster of engineers at the voss models.

He could not read the moss bed's display directly, but he could read the engineers. Their colors were wrong. The anxious greens had deepened into grey. Reed had seen it before, in labs at MIT, in conference rooms when the numbers went bad. The look of people staring at data they had trusted, and that was now telling them something they did not want to hear.

"Show me," Reed said.

Krrik did not look at him. He was looking at the bed. His forelimb rested on the surface, and the display shifted, and Reed could see, in the branching patterns of light through the moss, a shape that was either a map or a model or both. Root networks spreading outward from a central mass. The central mass was bright, almost white. The outer networks dimmed as they extended, fading into nothing at the edges of the display. Except that the edges were not where they should have been.

"This is the voss growth pattern for the current cycle," Krrik said. His voice was flat. No irritation. No impatience. Just flat. "The bright center is the primary substrate mass beneath us. The network is its extension through the root system. Normally, during a radiation peak, the growth extends approximately forty percent beyond its resting boundary. That is manageable. That is what the Pruning manages."

He touched the moss, and the display shifted. The bright center expanded. The network pushed outward, further, brighter, thicker.

"This is the current growth. Sixty-seven percent beyond resting boundary and accelerating. At this rate, within two of your months, the substrate will have consumed the primary root system of this section of forest. Within four months, the growth will be visible at the surface. Within a year."

He stopped.

"Within a year?" Reed prompted.

"Within a year we will know whether our methods are sufficient. We have done this before. We have always done this." Krrik looked at the display. The junior engineer from earlier had returned and was standing at the cluster's edge, her colors grey and still. "But 'always' is a comfort, not a guarantee."

Reed sat with that. The engineers around the moss bed were quiet. Their colors said what their voices did not.

"I have surface data," Reed said.

Krrik looked at him.

"Root density measurements. Canopy health assessments. Soil chemistry profiles. Six months of data collected from the surface. We documented changes in root density and soil composition without knowing what was causing them. We were looking at voss growth from outside without knowing what voss was."

"Your instruments are crude."

"My instruments measure what they measure. They measured something you did not measure, because you were inside it."

Krrik's colors cycled. Brown, green, brown, the rapid unconscious processing pattern Reed had learned to recognize as Krrik thinking hard about something he did not want to think about.

"The data would need calibration. Your measurement scales are not compatible with ours."

"I can provide the raw numbers. Your moss beds can handle the conversion."

"My computation arrays can handle the conversion." A pause. Something very like a smile crossed Krrik's face, which on a frog was a slight relaxation of the jaw muscles and a brightening of the skin around the eyes. "Your species' instruments are rudimentary. But a perspective from outside the system is. Not useless."

"High praise."

"It is not praise. It is a statement of non-dismissal. There is a difference."

Reed stood alone in the lab after the engineers dispersed to relay Krrik's instructions about sensor recalibration. Krrik had gone to examine a voss sample in a side chamber, his lopsided hop carrying him through the low doorway without hesitation.

The displacement apparatus was visible through the gap in the root wall. Wet stones. Fungal threads. Two centimeters of still water. The thing that moved matter across continents, between hemispheres, between star systems. Built by a species that lived thirty-five years and argued about fermented insects and could not keep fungus out of their bathing chambers.

Reed's entire career, the accumulated scientific knowledge of his species, was a subset. Not a rival tradition. A subset. The frogs understood evolution better than he did, because they had been doing it for two hundred million years, because they had visited other solar systems and found them disappointing and come home to argue about food.

His hands were shaking. Not from fear. Everything he thought he knew was still true, but in the context of what surrounded him, it was so small that the word "small" did not do it justice. Footnote. His work was a footnote. And the footnote might be useful, which was its own guilty thrill, because the most terrifying scientific crisis in the history of a two-hundred-million-year-old civilization was also, from where Reed stood squinting at wet rocks through fogged glasses, the most extraordinary laboratory on Earth.

The glasses went up. The notebook came out. He began writing down every question he did not yet know how to ask.

---

# Chapter 10: The Fungal Audit

Hart had identified eleven species of fungus in the settlement's corridors before breakfast, and nobody seemed concerned.

She stood in the biologists' shared quarters with her field notebook open on a borrowed moss cushion, her sample kit laid out with the precision of a surgeon who expected the operating room to be filthy. Reed was sitting on the floor retying a boot. Marsh was writing in her own notebook at the low stone table, her pencil moving in the small, steady hand of someone who had been documenting animal behavior in field conditions for fifteen years and did not intend to stop because the animals had started talking back.

"I am conducting a field epidemiological survey of this settlement," Hart said. "Whether anyone wants to join or not."

Reed looked up. "Right now?"

"Right now. I have been here eleven days. I have catalogued fungal growth on every wall I have touched, in the water channels, on the food preparation surfaces, and in the communal pools where everybody sits in each other's chemical runoff and calls it socializing. Nobody has tested any of it. Nobody appears interested in testing any of it. I am interested."

"Hart, we are guests in a civilization that can fold space."

"And that civilization has mold on its breakfast tables. You owe me. I catalogued your root toxins for three weeks in Manaus."

Reed pushed his glasses up. This was true.

Marsh closed her notebook. "I'll come. I want to see how the practitioners interact with patients. Whether they touch them, how close they stand, whether they talk during treatment or stay quiet."

"Bon. Reed, you handle the biochemistry questions. I handle the epidemiology. Marsh documents the behavioral component." Hart snapped her sample kit shut. "We are not asking permission. We are going to walk in and look competent until someone stops us."

Nobody stopped them. The treatment chambers were not chambers at all, not in any sense Hart recognized. They were open, connected pools stepped into the root-wall system, warm and shallow, each one roughly two meters across and separated from the next by low stone ridges worn smooth by centuries of use. The air smelled of mineral salts and something astringent, like wet copper mixed with bruised leaves. Patients were visible to anyone passing through, and anyone passing through had opinions.

A frog with a crushed forelimb sat in the nearest pool while a practitioner applied something thick and dark green to the injury site. Two pools over, an elderly frog with labored breathing soaked in water that had been treated with something that turned it faintly amber and smelled of pine resin. In a shallow basin near the corridor entrance, a young frog sat very still while a second practitioner examined his throat, which had turned an unhealthy grey.

"That one swallowed something during a fermentation experiment," said a passing frog, pausing to observe Hart the way one might observe a large, unusual insect that had wandered into the kitchen. "He is always swallowing things. Last season it was a sample jar lid."

The practitioners moved between patients without hurrying. They had been doing this a long time and did not need to perform urgency. Three were currently working, all roughly the same small size, their skin colors muted to a steady professional green that Hart was beginning to recognize as the frog equivalent of a white coat. They noticed the humans immediately and did not appear alarmed.

Hart began asking questions.

The first practitioner, who introduced herself as Tivven, was treating the crushed forelimb. The treatment involved a paste of biological compounds that, as far as Hart could determine, was stimulating cellular regrowth directly at the injury site.

"The digit will regenerate," Tivven said. "Slowly. Perhaps four of your weeks. The pain is managed through the water's mineral content."

"You can regenerate a lost limb."

"A digit. A full limb takes longer. Several of your months, depending on the patient's age and general condition."

Hart looked at Reed. Reed's glasses had fogged. He was writing in his notebook so fast the pencil was making dents in the paper.

"And cancer?" Hart asked.

Tivven's colors shifted slightly, the way a person might tilt their head at an odd question. "Unregulated cell growth? We identify it early through chemical monitoring. The body's signaling systems usually catch it. When they do not, we intervene biochemically. It is not difficult. Your species finds it difficult?"

"We find it extremely difficult."

"Your skin is very thick," Tivven said. She appeared to mean this literally. "You cannot read your own bodies. We breathe through ours, absorb through ours, signal through ours. If something grows wrong, the body broadcasts it. Your bodies do not broadcast. They. Contain." She said the word "contain" the way someone might say "suffocate." "How do you know when you are ill?"

"We wait until it hurts," Hart said.

Tivven's spots flared with something between alarm and pity.

A second practitioner, younger and slightly larger, had been listening from the adjacent pool. He hopped onto the stone ridge between them, water dripping from his hindquarters.

"Explain your medicine," he said. "How you treat injuries."

Hart looked at Marsh. Marsh gave her the slightest nod. Go ahead.

"Surgery," Hart said. "For serious injuries, we cut the body open. We use anesthesia to render the patient unconscious. We make incisions, repair the internal damage manually, suture the tissue closed, and monitor recovery in a sterile environment."

Tivven stopped applying the paste. Her colors went through a slow cascade, greens to yellow to a pale amber that Hart read as horror. The second practitioner's spots darkened.

"You cut the body open," Tivven said.

"Yes."

"On purpose."

"On purpose."

"And the patient agrees to this."

"The patient is unconscious."

Tivven turned to the second practitioner. Her colors said everything her voice did not.

"That is not better," Tivven said.

The second practitioner leaned forward. "Wait. The logic. You cannot regenerate tissue, so you must access the damage directly. Through the barrier of the skin. Because your skin is a barrier, not a, not a." He searched. "Your skin is a wall. So you go through the wall."

"That is barbaric," Tivven said.

"That is engineering around a constraint," the second practitioner said. "Brutal, but there is logic. If I could not signal through the skin, if the body did not broadcast, if I had to reach the injury blind." His colors shifted through something almost like professional respect. "I would also go through the wall. I would hate it. But I would do it."

"You would not."

"I would. What else could you do? Leave the damage inside and hope?"

A third practitioner arrived, older, moving slowly, her skin faded to a translucent grey-green that reminded Hart of Pem. She had been listening from the corridor.

"All medicine is violence managed with good intentions," she said.

The pool went quiet for a moment.

"You cut to heal," she continued, settling onto a ledge. "We dissolve barriers to heal. Both methods damage something to repair something else. The debate is about which damage you accept." She looked at Hart with clear, bright eyes. "What do you accept?"

"Sterility," Hart said. "Controlled environments. Sealed wounds. Containment."

"Containment." The old practitioner said it the same way Tivven had said it. Like a diagnosis. "You contain the patient. You contain the infection. You contain the body from its environment."

"We keep things clean."

"You keep things closed."

Reed was still writing. Marsh had stopped. She was watching the practitioners' colors with the focused attention she usually reserved for primate mothers handling their young.

Hart moved deeper into the treatment chambers. The crushed-digit frog was flexing his forelimb experimentally, the paste already working. The elderly frog in the amber water was breathing more easily, the labored quality fading. The fermentation-experiment frog was being given something to drink that turned his throat from grey to a cautious green, accompanied by a lecture from the practitioner that sounded identical in tone to every "this is why we do not eat things off the lab floor" speech Hart had heard in graduate school.

She noticed the fungus at the third pool.

It was growing on the stone rim, a thin, persistent film of something pale and fuzzy. She had seen the same species on the corridor walls. On the food surfaces. On the edge of the communal bathing pools. The practitioners were working directly beside it without any apparent concern.

"This," Hart said, crouching beside the pool rim. "This fungal growth. On every surface. In the treatment areas."

Tivven glanced at it. "Yes."

"You are aware of it."

"Of course."

"And you are not treating it."

Tivven's colors did something complicated. "We are aware of it. It is not. It is not what you think it is."

"What I think it is, is a fungal infection colonizing every shared surface in your medical infrastructure. In a human facility, this would be a biohazard. We would shut down the treatment area, sterilize every surface, and investigate the source."

"You would seal everything."

"We would clean everything."

The old practitioner had followed Hart. She stood at the pool's edge, her faded skin catching the bioluminescence.

"The fungal growth persists because we allow it to persist," she said. "We could eliminate it. We have the biochemistry. Krrik's laboratory could produce a compound that would kill every fungal colony in the settlement within a day." She paused. "It would also require sealing the skin."

Hart waited.

"We breathe through our skin. You know this. But the skin does more than breathe. It is how we signal, how we read each other, how we synchronize during the displacement operations. It is how we." She stopped. "It is how we are ourselves. Together."

"And the fungus gets in through the same openness."

"The fungus gets in because the door is open. If you close the door, you stop the fungus. You also stop everything else. No chemical signaling. No color communication. No shared breathing. No synchronization. No merging." The old practitioner looked at Hart with steady, clear eyes. "A sealed frog is a safe frog. A sealed frog is also a deaf frog, a mute frog, and a frog who will never feel another mind touch theirs. We chose. We chose a long time ago."

"You chose infection."

"We chose connection. The infection is the cost."

Reed had stopped writing. His pencil was suspended above the notebook. Marsh was looking at Hart with an expression Hart could not read, or did not want to.

Hart straightened up. The fungal film on the stone rim caught the bioluminescence and glistened, pale and persistent and everywhere.

"Flow, don't push," Tivven said to a patient who was resisting the mineral soak. It sounded like medical advice. It also sounded like a prayer. Hart could not tell the difference.

The argument about the moss compress started because of Oona.

Oona arrived at the treatment pools twenty minutes later, her orange throat patch at maximum brightness, which Hart had learned meant either gossip, strong opinions, or both. She was visiting a neighbor, a squat olive frog soaking in a warm pool with stiff joints.

"Brenk, I brought the compress," Oona said, producing a wad of damp moss from somewhere Hart did not want to think about. "Apply it to the left side. Leave it for an hour. The pain will improve."

"What is in that?" Hart asked.

Oona looked at her. "Moss."

"What kind of moss? Treated with what? Applied based on what evidence?"

"Applied based on the evidence that my grandmother used it, my mother used it, I have used it for nine years, and it works."

"That is not evidence. That is anecdote."

"Anecdote is what evidence looks like before scientists get involved and make it complicated."

Tivven made a sound that might have been a laugh or a professional sigh. Two frogs in adjacent pools turned to watch. The neighbor, Brenk, accepted the compress and applied it to his left side without apparent concern about the medical debate unfolding over his body.

"The moss compress has no demonstrated biochemical mechanism," Tivven said. "We have tested it."

"You have tested it and found nothing because you tested the wrong thing," Oona said. "You tested whether the moss kills infection. It does not kill infection. That is not what it does. It helps with the pain. My grandmother did not care about the mechanism. She cared about the pain."

"Oona, I respect your grandmother."

"You did not know my grandmother."

"I respect the concept of your grandmother. But the compress is not medicine. It is tradition."

"Tradition is what medicine looks like before it has a budget," Oona said. Her throat patch flared. Brenk, beneath the compress, appeared to be improving, or at least was enjoying the attention.

Hart was agreeing with an alien medical practitioner and disagreeing with a frog housewife about moss, which was not a scenario McGill had prepared her for.

"The compress is a placebo," Hart said. "Or the warm water is doing the work, and the moss is decorative."

Oona turned to her with the full force of a personality that had been winning domestic arguments for sixteen years. "You have been here eleven days. I have been applying this compress for nine years. Brenk has had stiff joints for three years. Every time I bring the compress, the stiffness improves. Every time the practitioners give him the mineral soak alone, the stiffness returns within a day. You may call that placebo. I call that a pattern."

Three frogs nearby murmured approval. A fourth, sitting in a shallow pool with what appeared to be a mild skin irritation, offered: "My aunt uses the same compress. She is seventy-four."

"Seventy-four what?" Hart asked.

"Seasons. She is very old and her joints are perfect."

"Her joints are not perfect," Oona said. "But they are better than Brenk's, and she uses the compress twice a week."

Krrik arrived.

He hopped into the treatment area with the lopsided gait Hart recognized from the laboratory, his mottled skin dusted with spore residue, both eyes fixed on a stone tablet he was carrying. He appeared to be there to collect a biochemical analysis from the practitioners, not to participate in anyone's argument.

"Krrik," Hart said. "The moss compress. Superstition or medicine?"

Krrik did not look up from his tablet. "The moss compress is folk practice unsupported by controlled observation. It persists because anecdote is more emotionally satisfying than data. It has no demonstrated mechanism of action. I do not have time for this."

Oona's throat patch dimmed. Tivven looked vindicated.

"However," Krrik said, still not looking up, "the mucilage layer of that particular moss species contains three compounds with measurable anti-inflammatory properties. The concentrations are low but consistent. Whether these compounds are absorbed through the skin at therapeutic levels depends on exposure duration and the patient's individual permeability, which varies. I mention this because it is true, not because it is relevant to anyone's argument."

He collected his analysis from Tivven, exchanged two sentences about a dosing calculation, and left.

The treatment pool was silent for a moment.

"So the compress works," Oona said.

"He said the compounds exist. He did not say they work at therapeutic levels," Tivven said.

"He said anti-inflammatory. He said consistent. He said absorbed through the skin. The compress works."

"The compress might work. Through a mechanism you did not know about and cannot control for."

"I do not need to control for it. I need Brenk's joints to stop hurting."

Brenk, beneath his compress, had closed his eyes. He looked comfortable. Whether this was the anti-inflammatory compounds, the warm water, the placebo effect, or the simple pleasure of being fussed over by someone who cared, Hart could not determine. She found this more annoying than she expected. She had wanted to be right. Oona had wanted to be right. Krrik had made them both right and neither of them happy, and he had done it without noticing. She was fairly sure he had not noticed.

Hart spent the next hour walking the treatment pools, cataloguing what she saw. Reed trailed her with his notebook. Marsh sat with Tivven and the old practitioner, asking questions about how practitioners were trained, how they communicated during treatment, whether they spoke to patients or stayed silent.

The daily flow of the chambers was larger than Hart had expected. Beyond the routine cases, there were injuries from the Pruning staging grounds: a frog with a stress fracture in her hind leg from repeated jumping drills, two displacement operators with the glassy-eyed disorientation that followed extended synchronization practice, a young frog who had strained something in his back carrying equipment. The staging injuries were handled with the same unhurried competence as everything else, but Hart noticed the volume. More injuries than the chambers were designed for. The practitioners absorbed the increase without complaint, without visible strain, and without enough staff.

Two older practitioners argued quietly about dosing philosophy while treating adjacent patients. One favored lower concentrations over longer periods. The other preferred higher concentrations with shorter exposure. The argument had been running for years. It would continue running until one of them died, at which point the survivor would continue it with someone else.

"Is the current generation healthier than the last?" Hart asked the old practitioner.

"Every generation asks this question. Every generation believes the answer is no. The archive contains data suggesting the answer is consistently yes, but nobody reads the archive data because it contradicts the satisfying belief that everything is getting worse."

"So things are getting better."

"Things are getting different. Better and worse are categories I have stopped finding useful." She adjusted the mineral content of a treatment pool with a casual precision that Hart found both impressive and slightly alarming. "Your species measures health by the absence of disease. We measure it by the quality of connection. A frog with a fungal infection who can feel every mind in the breathing circle is healthier, by our measure, than a frog with perfect skin who has sealed herself off."

Hart wrote this down. She wrote it in French, because some things were easier to think about in a language that did not belong to this conversation.

The debrief happened at the biologists' quarters that evening. The low vibration of the water channels hummed through the floor. Hart sat on a root ledge. Reed was cross-legged on the floor with his notebook. Marsh leaned against the wall, her own notebook closed. Vale arrived mid-sentence, settling onto a stone shelf near the entrance with the practiced ease of a man who had been walking into rooms late his entire career and never apologized for it.

Quill was in the doorway. He did not sit. He leaned against the root-frame and listened.

"Their medicine is more advanced than ours," Hart said. "Regeneration. Cancer. Pain management through water chemistry Reed says is centuries ahead of anything we have. And they are covered in fungus. Every surface, every pool, every treatment area. Fungal growth that any hospital on Earth would shut down for."

"Because they can't eliminate it?" Vale asked.

"Because they chose not to. The fungus gets in through the skin. Close the skin, kill the fungus. Close the skin, kill everything else. No signaling, no breathing circles, no synchronization for the Pruning. They have a phrase for it. A sealed frog." Hart paused. "They say it like a diagnosis."

Quill, in the doorway, shifted his weight. He did not speak.

"So they live with the infections," Reed said.

"They live with constant, low-grade fungal colonization of every shared surface and call it the price of being open. Their old practitioner told me that health, by their measure, is not the absence of disease. It is the quality of connection. A frog with a fungal infection who can feel every mind around her is healthier than a frog with clean skin who is sealed off."

"That is philosophy, not medicine," Vale said.

Hart looked at him. "That is what I thought. For about an hour. Then I watched them regenerate a crushed digit in real time, treat cancer like a scheduling problem, and manage pain through water chemistry that Reed says is two centuries ahead of anything at MIT. And the same people, the same practitioners, in the same room, looked at the fungus on their treatment surfaces and said: 'Yes. We know. That is what openness costs.' They are not ignorant. They are not negligent. They made a decision, and the decision is philosophically consistent with everything else about them, and I find that." She stopped. "I find that very uncomfortable."

The water channels hummed beneath the floor. Marsh was watching Hart with the quiet attention she usually gave to things she was not ready to say aloud. Vale's eyes had narrowed, which meant he was processing something he did not like. Reed was writing. Quill stood in the doorway, his hands in his pockets, his face still.

"The frogs' relationship with disease is not negligence, it is philosophy," Hart said. "And I do not know which is worse."

---

# Chapter 11: The Temporary Swamp Event

The pools were busiest at evening. Quill had noticed this over the past several days, how the communal water filled after the light in the settlement shifted from its dim daytime blue-green to something warmer, amber at the edges, the glowing fungi responding to temperature changes nobody had explained to him. The frogs came to the pools like Londoners going to pubs, not for any singular purpose but because the alternative was staying home and the water was warm and there were things to discuss or not discuss, depending on the evening and the company.

He sat at the edge of the largest pool with his feet on dry stone, notebook open on his lap, pencil behind his ear. Not in the water. He had been in the water once, on his second day, and had spent the following hour feeling as though the boundary between his thoughts and the ambient sounds of the settlement had gone slightly porous. Hart had later told him the mineral content was mildly psychoactive, which he found both reassuring and insufficient as an explanation.

The pool tonight held perhaps thirty frogs across its interconnected sections. Some floated in loose groups, their conversations overlapping in the clicks and harmonic tones of Threnn-song with occasional patches of English when they remembered or bothered. Two young frogs were having an argument near the far edge that involved rapid color changes and what appeared to be competitive splashing. An older frog was asleep on a warm stone ledge, her colors cycling through slow, dreamy greens. Three frogs near the center were singing something low and sustained, not for an audience but for the vibration of it, how the sound traveled through water and stone and into the bones of anyone sitting close enough.

Vesh was in the warm section, roughly chest-deep for a frog, surrounded by a loose ring of about eight others who drifted in and out of his orbit like iron filings around a magnet that kept pausing to eat insects. His emerald green was vivid in the low light, the gold flecks in his skin catching the bioluminescence and throwing it back in tiny points that made him look, Quill thought, like a small, damp cathedral window. One eye was slightly larger than the other. It gave him a permanent expression of lopsided wonder, which was either a physical characteristic or a philosophical commitment. With Vesh, the distinction was unclear.

"The question you are asking," Vesh said to no one in particular, though Quill suspected it was aimed at him, "is whether we are afraid of dying. This is a very human question. You ask it because you treat death as an interruption. Something that stops you. We treat death as punctuation. A period at the end of a croak."

He caught an insect from the air, snatched with a speed that interrupted nothing. He chewed with slow deliberation.

"That croak continues," he said. "The Croaking continues. Everything croaks. The stones croak slowly. Water croaks by flowing. The voss croaks by growing. You croak by sitting on that rock and refusing to get in the water, which, by the way, is a very dry way to spend an evening."

A frog near him, rust-brown and unhurried, made a sound that was either laughter or agreement. "Very dry," she confirmed.

"I'm observing," Quill said.

"Observing is the driest activity. You watch the water and remain on the rock. This is the human condition in four words."

Across the pool complex, in a shallower section separated by a low stone ridge, Hart had her boots off and her feet in the water up to her ankles. She was sitting with Talla, who floated at the surface with the boneless ease frogs managed when they stopped caring about posture. Hart was studying the water with her usual focused suspicion of someone waiting for the other shoe to drop, or in this case, the other pathogen to bloom.

"The mineral content," Hart said. "Specifically the lithium analog. It's in the range that would require monitoring in any human therapeutic context."

Talla's dark skin caught the light. She was nearly black-green in the evening glow, compact, rigid even while floating. "Of course it is. Everything here is slightly psychoactive. They call it 'ambient harmonization.' I call it municipal drugging."

"Has anyone tested the parasitic load?"

"Nobody tests the parasitic load. Testing implies something might be wrong. Nothing is wrong. The water is harmonized." Talla's pale spots flickered. "You will notice that the most important conversations in this civilization happen in pools where the water loosens your objections. This is not an accident."

Hart looked at her feet. They were still in the water. She did not remove them.

Quill heard the exchange, filed it, and turned back to Vesh, who was now explaining something to a young frog, barely metamorphosed, who had settled at the ring's edge with the wide-eyed stillness of someone encountering an idea for the first time.

"Tell me about the self," Quill said. He said it simply because he had been thinking about it for days and the pool seemed like the kind of place where the question might land differently than in his notebook. "Your concept of it. How it works."

Vesh's gold flecks brightened. The effect was subtle but unmistakable, a faint intensification that spread from his throat to his back, making the emerald glow warmer.

"You treat your self as a possession," Vesh said. "Something you own, protect, lose. You spend your lives building a self like you build a house. Walls. Roof. A door you control. You decide who enters. You are terrified of leaks."

He paused to eat another insect. This one crunched.

"We treat the self as weather. It arrives. It persists. It passes. You do not own a storm. You do not protect a sunny afternoon. The interesting question is not 'who am I' but 'what is the climate doing.' A puddle forms when conditions are right. Rain falls, ground dips, water collects. The puddle has a shape. It reflects things. It is real while it lasts. But it was never separate from the water it came from, and when it evaporates or overflows, it has not died. It has stopped being a puddle. The water does not care about the shape."

The rust-brown frog nodded. Two frogs further out in the pool shifted their colors in what Quill was learning to read as contemplative agreement. The young frog at the edge had not moved.

An elderly frog, so old his skin had gone translucent at the joints, raised himself from a stone ledge on the pool's far side. His voice carried across the water with the particular authority of someone who had been correcting people for a very long time.

"The original phrasing," the elderly frog said, "is not about weather. The archive preserves it. 'The self is a borrowing from the wet.' You have changed it, Vesh. You always change it."

"I have improved it."

"You have dramatized it. The original says nothing about weather or climate. The original says the self is borrowed. Like a tool. You use it, you return it. Weather is your addition. Weather is more entertaining but less precise."

Vesh turned to face the elderly frog with the elaborate respect of someone who had been having this argument for years. "Borrowed implies obligation. Weather implies none. The self does not owe anything. The self simply happens."

"The self owes the wet," the old frog said. His colors went through a sequence Quill could not read but that several pool frogs apparently could, because three of them made sounds of agreement and one made a sound that was clearly a heckle.

"Vesh thinks he is the Wet Way," the heckler said. "The Wet Way thinks Vesh is a puddle with a speaking problem."

Laughter from the nearby frogs, warm and overlapping. Vesh absorbed it magnificently. Being laughed at was, as far as he was concerned, a form of spiritual practice.

"Everyone is a puddle with a speaking problem," he said. "I am simply honest about mine."

Oona appeared at the pool's edge carrying a shallow stone dish piled with something that smelled fermented, sharp, and faintly sweet. Her orange throat patch was at full brightness, which Quill had learned to associate with social energy rather than any specific emotion.

"Grubs," she announced. "Evening batch. Quill, you should try one."

"Are they safe?"

"They are fermented. Nothing fermented is safe, that is what makes it interesting." She set the dish on the stone ledge. Several frogs reached for it. "For humans, I would describe the experience as educational."

Quill took a grub. It was soft, damp, and tasted like mushrooms soaked in vinegar and then left in someone's pocket for a week. He ate it because refusing would have been impolite and because the flavor, while objectively terrible, had a complexity that he suspected would haunt him.

"That is the face," Oona said. "Every human makes that face. The trader from the river made that face. He ate them for twenty-two years and never stopped making it."

She distributed grubs to the pool, exchanged three sentences with the rust-brown frog about someone's sleeping-chamber dispute, and left with the energy of someone who had six more stops to make and opinions to deliver at each one.

The young frog at the edge of Vesh's ring spoke for the first time. Small voice, barely above the pool's ambient murmur.

"Is the puddle afraid? When it starts to dry?"

Vesh went still. Genuinely still, not performing it. His gold flecks dimmed slightly, which Quill had never seen before.

"That," Vesh said, "is the best question anyone has asked me this season."

The young frog's colors shifted through uncertain, pleased greens.

"The puddle does not fear drying," Vesh said. "Because the water does not stop being water when the puddle goes away. The shape ends. The water continues. This is the Croaking. Everything that exists is the sound the universe makes when it is happening. The stone croaks slowly. Water croaks by flowing. A dying frog croaks by dying, which is just the universe changing the note." He looked at the young frog with a seriousness that Quill recognized, suddenly and with unsettling clarity, as the real thing. "You are afraid because you think the note is you. But you are not the note. You are the singing. The singing does not stop."

The pool was quiet. Not silent. The water still moved, the background frogs still murmured, the singing trio in the center still hummed. But there was a quality of attention that had settled over Vesh's section, a listening that Quill could feel in the warmth of the stone under his hands.

From across the pool complex, Hart's voice, dry and clear: "Ask him how many people the weather kills."

Several frogs turned. The attention shifted. Vesh looked toward Hart without irritation, without surprise. He had been fielding this category of objection for longer than Hart had been alive.

"Ask him," Hart repeated, louder. She was leaning forward, her feet still in the water. Talla beside her had gone very still, her pale spots visible even at this distance. "If the self is weather, and weather is natural, and natural things just happen, ask him what the body count is. For the weather."

Vesh caught another insect. Chewed it slowly. The gold flecks in his skin were steady, neither brightening nor dimming.

"Your objection," he said, "is that treating the self as impermanent is a way of making death acceptable. And that making death acceptable is dangerous because it makes killing acceptable. Or at least makes the dying easier to ignore."

"That is exactly my objection."

"It is a good objection. It is also a description of every religion your species has ever produced. Denial of death dressed up as transcendence. You have done it with resurrection, reincarnation, eternal souls, heavenly kingdoms, seventy-two variations on the idea that the note does not end. We do not deny death. We deny that the note was ever the point." He wiped his mouth with one forelimb. "Perhaps your objection is really that we are honest about what your religions only pretend."

Hart opened her mouth. Closed it. Next to her, Talla said something too quiet for Quill to catch. Hart looked at Talla, then back at the pool. She did not respond.

Quill sat with the silence Hart had left, turning the idea over slowly, from every angle, looking for the seam where it might come apart.

"That sounds like nihilism," he said. "With better marketing."

Vesh looked at him. Both eyes, the larger and the smaller, focused with an attention that made the rest of the pool feel distant.

"Nihilism says nothing matters," Vesh said. "We say nothing matters the way you think it matters. You think matter is the way it matters. Permanence. Duration. How long the puddle lasts. We say the mattering is the part where the puddle reflects something. One moment of reflection. That is enough. Or it is not enough, but it is what we have."

He paused. His colors shifted through a register Quill could not name, warmer than emerald and older than the conversation.

"Two hundred million years we have been trying to merge. To dissolve the puddles back into the water. To hear the chord resolve. Individual selves, synchronized, opened, reaching for the moment where the boundaries soften and the separate notes become one sound." The gold flecks in his skin were vivid now, not beautiful exactly, more like something burning at low temperature, steady and consuming. "We call it the Almost. Because it almost happens. Every time. For two hundred million years. The chord almost resolves. It never does."

The singing trio in the center of the pool had stopped. They were listening.

"Nihilism would say: stop trying," Vesh said. "We say: the trying is the thing. The chord is the trying. The Almost is enough. Or it is not enough. But we have been reaching for it longer than your species has existed, and we have not stopped."

He caught another insect. This one he did not eat immediately. He held it, examined it, then ate it with the thoughtful deliberation of someone making a point about the relationship between the cosmic and the ordinary, or possibly just hungry.

"The Almost," Quill said. The word sat strangely in his mouth. Too simple for what it named.

"You are thinking about it already," Vesh said. "I can tell because you have gone very still, which is what humans do when an idea enters them. Frogs change color. Humans go still. Both are involuntary. Both are honest."

Quill was still. This was accurate. He was thinking about two hundred million years of reaching for something that never arrived, and he was thinking about the word "enough," and about whether a civilization could be built on "almost," and whether that was the most absurd or the most beautiful foundation for a species he had ever encountered. He was thinking in spirals, which was what he did when an idea was too large to approach directly. Circle it. Come at it from the side. See if it holds.

It held. That was the problem.

Vesh's voice changed. A degree less performative. The gold flecks dimmed to their resting state. He looked at the pool surface.

"The work," he said. "The Pruning. You have heard about it."

"Some," Quill said.

"The displacement operations require merging. Real merging. Not the breathing circles, not the evening soaks where you feel your neighbor's mood and call it community. The operators link their consciousness and hold it linked while they coordinate displacement across hemispheres. Hundreds of minds, thinking as one, for hours."

He ate the insect he'd been holding.

"Some of them do not come back. Not many. But some. The work demands it. We do not discuss this at breathing circles. It would be unkind."

The pool went quiet.

Not the warm, attentive quiet of before. Something sharper. The frogs nearest to Vesh did not move, did not shift color, did not react in any visible way. The rust-brown frog looked at the water. The elderly frog on the far ledge closed his eyes. The young frog at the edge of Vesh's ring pulled slightly closer to the surface, reflexive, like an animal tucking itself smaller when something large passes overhead.

Three seconds. Four. Five.

Then the rust-brown frog said something to her neighbor about the temperature of the eastern pools, and the neighbor replied, and two frogs near the singing trio resumed a conversation about fermentation storage, and the young frog asked the frog beside him whether the grubs were still available, and the pool was alive again, was social, was evening. As if by agreement. As if the silence had been a breath the pool took and released, collectively, without discussion.

Quill sat on his rock. His notebook was open. He had not written anything.

He left a few minutes later, not abruptly, not dramatically. He said something to Vesh about the hour, a meaningless courtesy, and Vesh nodded with the serene attention he gave to everything, meaningful or not, sacred or trivial, which for Vesh seemed to be the same category.

The corridor outside the pool complex was cooler. The water sounds softened behind him. Fungal light lined the walls in dim blue-green, and the stone was damp under his shoes, and the air tasted of minerals and wet earth and something faintly sweet that might have been the voss or the fermentation cellars or just the smell of a civilization that had been breathing in this space for longer than mammals had existed.

He walked.

His thoughts did not organize. They circled. The puddle. The weather. The Croaking. Two hundred million years of almost. The young frog's question. The pool's silence. The speed with which the silence was filled, the collective agreement to cover what Vesh had said, the casual mention of frogs who went into the merging and did not come out, dropped between bites of insect, between the cosmic and the ordinary, between ideas he could not yet hold and a world that held them without strain.

He was not converted. He was certain of this in the same way he was certain his feet were on the stone. But something in the architecture of his thinking had shifted, a wall that had been solid now showing a crack, or not a crack exactly, more like a place where the mortar had softened and light was getting through from the other side. He did not know what was on the other side. He suspected Vesh did not know either.

He stopped in the corridor. Behind him, the pool sounds continued, faint and warm. Ahead, the settlement stretched in its ancient, damp, glowing permanence.

He had walked into the pool an observer. He was walking out as something he did not have a word for yet.

He suspected the Wet Way would say he did not need one.

---

# Chapter 12: Wet In, Wet Out

Lenn was dead, and Oona was carrying food.

She arrived at the biologists' quarters with a shallow stone dish balanced on one forearm and her orange throat patch at its dimmest, which Quill had never seen before. She did not knock. The frogs did not knock. Doors, where they existed, were open or absent, and the concept of a closed room struck most Threnn as architecturally hostile.

"Lenn has dissolved," she said. "The mourning pool is being prepared. You should come."

Marsh looked up from her notebook. "Who was Lenn?"

"Elderly. Displacement operator when he was young, before the joints went. Later he worked the fermentation cellars. He burned the first batch of everything he ever made and blamed the humidity. He was seventy-one seasons." Oona set the dish on the stone table. The food was something dense and dark, shaped into small mounds. "These are for after. You will want them. The ritual is short but the walk back is long."

"How did he die?" Reed asked. He was sitting cross-legged on the floor, his notebook open, his glasses pushed up.

"Old age. His breathing slowed over two days and then it stopped. The practitioners offered to extend it. He said the water temperature in the treatment pools was wrong and he would rather dissolve than continue complaining about it."

She said this with a flatness that was not cold. It was the flatness of someone reporting a fact that was sad and finished and did not require decoration.

"When is the mourning?" Quill asked.

"Now. Within the hour. The pool is already filling."

Vale, leaning against the root-frame of the doorway, looked at Hart. Hart looked at the food Oona had brought. Neither of them moved.

"You don't have to come," Oona said. "But you should. Lenn would not have cared either way. He did not care about most things. But the mourning is for the living, and you are living here now, so."

She left the sentence there. The throat patch flickered once, a faint pulse of orange, and went dim again.

They went.

The mourning pool was set apart from the communal bathing areas, deeper in the settlement, down a corridor where the bioluminescent light thinned to a pale wash and the stone walls were older, smoother, worn by water and time into surfaces that looked almost polished. The air changed. Warmer. Denser. A faint smell that Quill could not immediately place, something sharp and green, like crushed stems left in standing water.

Frogs were already gathering when they arrived. Not in a procession. Just arriving, because this was a place they had been before and would come to again. Some were already in the pool, floating at the surface with their eyes half-closed. Others sat on the stone rim, their hind legs trailing in the water. A group near the far edge was talking in low Threnn-song, colors shifting in slow, muted patterns. Two young frogs hovered at the entrance, unsure of their positioning, nudging each other toward the water with the awkwardness of beings attending something solemn for the first time.

Quill counted. Forty frogs, then more arriving. Forty-five. Close to fifty by the time the water stilled.

The five biologists spread out along the pool's edge. Marsh sat near Oona. Reed found a spot where he could see the water clearly. Vale positioned himself where he could see the room, who stood where, who arrived in what order. Hart stood with her arms crossed, examining the pool's surface with the focused restraint of someone who had decided, for once, not to test the water chemistry. She had stopped herself. Quill saw it happen: the instinct, the pause, the hands dropping to her sides.

Quill sat at the pool's rim, his feet on dry stone, his notebook in his lap. He did not open it.

Noll was there, near the center of the gathering, his muted green-grey coloring steady and composed. He stood on a stone ledge that gave him a slight elevation, and the effect was of someone presiding without admitting to presiding. He did not speak. His presence was the statement. One damaged forelimb held inward, as always, the old injury catching the light.

Talla was there, against the far wall, her dark skin nearly invisible in the dim corridor behind her. Pale spots hidden. She had come. Oona had mentioned days ago that Talla attended every mourning without exception. "Even I admit the dead deserve a proper dissolving," Talla had said. "It is the one thing we do that I do not have a complaint about. Give me time."

Krrik stood at the pool's edge nearest the exit, his mottled brown-green dusted with spore residue, his posture rigid in the way of someone who wanted to be elsewhere but could not justify leaving. He had known Lenn. Decades ago, when Lenn ran displacement operations and Krrik was calibrating the systems Lenn operated. They had not been close. They had been colleagues: proximity over time, shared work without shared sentiment. Krrik was there because it would have been noticed if he were not.

Pem was near the water. The archivist's faded, translucent skin caught the bioluminescence and held it like fog holds light, diffuse and sourceless. Pem's eyes were bright and steady. Hands ready. This was work. Sacred, repeated, necessary work, performed for the ten-thousandth time with the same precision as the first.

An older frog, compact and rust-colored, was telling a story to nobody in particular from a ledge near the pool's center.

"Lenn fermented a batch of grubs the year I moved to the Ninth Warren," the older frog said. "He had been working on the recipe for three seasons. It was, he insisted, going to be the finest preparation the eastern cellars had ever produced. He burned it. Completely. The smell lasted six days. He blamed the stone temperature, the ambient moisture, the quality of the grubs, and at one point the Siberians, who had not been involved. He then made a second batch, which was worse."

A few frogs nearby made soft sounds. Not laughter, not quite. Recognition.

"His third batch was acceptable. He was so surprised that he accused the grubs of cooperating out of spite."

A nursery caretaker arrived, slipping into the pool quietly, her small body barely disturbing the surface. She had been waiting for a colleague to take over the tadpoles. She was late and did not apologize. Nobody expected her to. The tadpoles came first. Everyone understood this.

Vesh moved to the pool's center.

He was smaller than most of the frogs around him, five centimeters of compact emerald green, the gold flecks in his skin dimmed to something muted and warm. Not the vivid brightness Quill had seen at the pools the night before. This was a different register. Practiced. Quill thought: he has done this before. Many times. He will do it again. And then someone will do it for him.

Vesh settled into the water until it reached his throat. The pool quieted. Not silence, not the sharp quiet of the merging-deaths revelation the night before. A gentler settling, like a room drawing a breath.

"Wet in, wet out," Vesh said.

His voice carried across the water. Not loudly. The pool's acoustics were designed for this, the warm stone and the still surface carrying vibration like a bell carries its ring.

"Lenn came from the water. He has returned to the water. The puddle has formed. The puddle has dissolved. The water does not mourn the shape."

He paused. Around the pool, frogs were still. The two young frogs near the entrance had stopped nudging each other. One had settled into the shallows with careful, deliberate movements.

"The Croaking was loud in Lenn," Vesh said. "It is quiet now. It will be loud again in others. The note changes. The singing does not stop."

A low harmonic passed through the water. Not from Vesh. From the gathered frogs. A communal vibration, sub-vocal, felt more than heard. Quill felt it in his hands where they rested on the stone rim, a faint tremor that traveled through the rock and into his palms and wrists. Around the pool, the frogs' bodies were transmitting something his ears could not fully capture but his bones registered. A chord. Sustained. Not musical in any way he could describe, but harmonically present, like standing near a cathedral organ, something that made your sternum hum.

Lenn's body was brought to the water.

Two frogs carried it, carefully, the small form cradled between them. Lenn was perhaps six centimeters. His colors had faded to a uniform grey, the vibrancy gone, the signaling skin quiet for the first time in seventy-one seasons. He looked, Quill thought, like what he was: a small dead animal. And then he thought: no. He looked like a person who had stopped. The difference between those two thoughts was the distance his mind had traveled in the last two weeks, and he did not know what to do with that.

They placed Lenn in the water. The body floated, then settled, half-submerged, in the center of the pool where Vesh waited.

"May you dissolve gently," Vesh said.

Then nothing happened. And then something did.

Quill did not see it start. He felt it. A change in the water's smell, the sharp green scent intensifying, becoming more specific, more present. The frogs in the pool shifted. Not in position. In attention. Their skin, their colors, their breathing oriented toward the center where Lenn's body rested.

The chemical sharing. He had read about it in Marsh's notes, heard it referenced in Oona's stories, understood it intellectually as a biological process: the dead frog's body releasing its final chemical signature into the warm water, the living frogs absorbing it through their permeable skin. Chemistry. Not metaphor.

But understanding it intellectually and sitting at the edge of a pool while it happened were not the same thing.

The frogs nearest Lenn's body went still first. Their colors shifted, briefly, through something Quill could not read. Then the stillness spread outward, ring by ring, as the chemical signal moved through the warm water and reached each body in turn. The frogs absorbed it as they absorbed everything, through the skin, through the permeable boundary between self and environment that was not a boundary at all but a door that was always open.

Quill, at the rim, his feet on dry stone, his hands on wet rock, caught a trace.

It was faint. He was not in the water. His skin was not theirs. But the stone was wet, and his palms were flat against it, and something traveled through the moisture and reached him.

A feeling. Mild, specific, and slightly annoyed.

The water was too warm.

That was it. That was Lenn's final broadcast. His last chemical signature, the final biological output of a seventy-one-season life that had included displacement operations and burned fermentation batches and complaints about humidity and stone temperature and the Siberians: irritation about the temperature of the pool.

Several frogs near the center made small sounds. Not quite laughter. Closer to recognition. The sound people make when something is exactly what they expected and still catches them off guard.

Oona, floating near Marsh, whispered something. Quill heard it because the pool was so quiet that whispers carried.

"He always complained about the water temperature. Even at the end."

Marsh made a sound that was half a laugh and half something else. She did not finish it.

And then the colors changed.

Quill saw it happen. The gathered frogs, forty-seven or forty-eight of them, spread across the pool and around its rim, their bodies still processing the chemical trace of a dead frog's last irritation, synchronized. Their colors shifted. Not deliberately. Not as part of the ritual, not as a breathing circle or a merging exercise. Involuntarily. The way a crowd flinches at the same sound or turns toward the same light. Fifty frogs, for perhaps three seconds, turned the same shade of blue-green. A deep, still, luminous blue-green that was not any individual frog's natural coloring but something produced collectively, a frequency none of them owned alone.

The Almost. Appearing uninvited. In grief.

Quill recognized it. He had heard Vesh describe it the night before, the chord of separate selves almost resolving, two hundred million years of reaching. He had heard it as philosophy. Now he was seeing it as something that happened to bodies, involuntarily, in a pool where a dead frog's last complaint about water temperature was still dissolving through the living.

Three seconds. Maybe four. Then it passed. The individual colors returned. The blue-green faded into forty-eight separate patterns of emerald and olive and brown and rust and the dark near-black of Talla against the wall and the faded translucence of Pem near the water's edge.

Reed, across the pool, was leaning forward. His glasses had fogged. He was not writing.

Vale was watching the frogs' faces. The social choreography. Who had expected this. Who had not.

Hart stood with her arms at her sides and her mouth closed.

Marsh had put her hand flat on the stone rim the way Quill had, and he wondered if she had caught the trace too, if her palms had picked up the same mild irritation through the wet stone, and what she had made of it.

Vesh waited until the colors settled. Then he spoke to the young frog who had waded into the shallows at the start of the ritual, the one who had been nudging his companion at the entrance.

"You felt it," Vesh said. Not a question.

The young frog's skin was cycling through confused greens and ambers. "I felt. Something. About the water being wrong."

"That was Lenn. His last feeling. It is in you now. A drop." Vesh's gold flecks were steady, dim, warm. "You carry a drop of everyone who dissolves near you. This is why old frogs are heavy with the dissolved."

The young frog's colors shifted through something that was not quite wonder and not quite nausea and settled on a pale, shaken green.

Pem moved forward.

The archivist entered the water with the slow economy of a body that rationed its movements, each one chosen. Pem's translucent skin flickered, briefly, with colors that were not Pem's own. Faint echoes. Lenn's grey, passing through the keeper's faded membrane and registering there like light through tissue paper. Pem received the biological imprint the way an archivist receives a document: with steady hands and an attention that did not require emotion to be present but did not exclude it either. The record of who Lenn was, compressed into chemistry, into the last traces the water held, absorbed through skin that had absorbed ten thousand such traces before.

Pem said nothing. Pem's bright eyes were open and focused on the water. When it was done, the archivist withdrew, slowly, carrying what had been deposited.

The water was cycled.
