Fresh water entered through channels Quill had not noticed, and the mourning pool's contents flowed outward, carrying the diluted remnants of Lenn's final broadcast into the settlement's water system, into the channels and pools and walls where it would disperse and thin and eventually be indistinguishable from everything else. The water that had held a life's last feeling would become the water someone bathed in tomorrow, or drank, or soaked tadpoles in. This was not symbolic. It was plumbing. It was also, Quill thought, the Wet Way in four pipes and a drainage channel.

The community began to leave.

Not slowly. Not with the heavy, reluctant movements of human mourners filing out of a church or standing too long at a graveside because leaving felt like an admission that life continued and continuing felt like betrayal. The frogs left the way they had arrived: practically, without ceremony, because there were things to do.

A group near the far edge climbed out of the pool and headed toward the Pruning staging grounds, their conversation shifting to displacement drill schedules before they reached the corridor. A nursery worker left to relieve the colleague who had taken over her tadpoles. Two frogs from the fermentation cellars discussed whether Lenn's last unfinished batch should be completed as a tribute or discarded as a health risk. They appeared to be genuinely divided on this question.

Oona climbed out of the pool and sat on the rim near Marsh, water dripping from her small, round body. Her throat patch was still dim.

"Is that all?" Marsh asked. She said it quietly, not as criticism, not as judgment. As a question she needed answered.

Oona looked at her. "What else would there be? He was here. Now he is in the water and the archive. Holding the shape of a dissolved puddle is unkind to the puddle and to yourself."

Marsh closed her notebook. She had opened it at some point during the ritual. Quill had seen her writing. He had also seen her stop.

Reed was talking to one of the frogs who had carried Lenn's body, asking quiet questions about the chemistry of the release, how the signal propagated through warm water, what determined which emotional state was broadcast. The frog answered patiently. Reed was processing. This was how Reed processed: by asking how the mechanism worked, because the mechanism was easier to hold than the thing the mechanism carried.

Vale had moved to the exit corridor. He stood near Talla, who was leaning against the wall with her forelimbs folded, braced, ready to argue even when there was nothing to argue about.

Hart said something to Talla. The first and only thing Hart had said since arriving at the pool.

"That was the most efficient grief I have ever seen."

Talla did not move. Her pale spots flickered once.

"Efficient grief is what you get when you have thirty years and cannot afford to spend three of them crying."

Hart looked at her. Talla looked at the pool where two maintenance frogs were already checking the water channels.

"Do you grieve him?" Hart asked.

"I did not know him well. I knew his fermented grubs. They were bad." Talla paused. "I grieve the system that makes this necessary. The speed. The moving on. Some of us would like to sit with the dead a little longer, but there is a Pruning to prepare for and displacement drills to run and a civilization that does not stop because one old frog complained about water temperature for the last time."

Her spots flared. She folded them back. She left.

Quill sat at the pool's edge after the others had gone.

Not all the others. A few frogs remained, drifting in the cycled water, not mourning exactly, just present. The older rust-colored frog who had told the fermentation story was still on his ledge, his eyes half-closed. One of the young frogs from the entrance was sitting very still in the shallows, absorbing what he had absorbed, his colors settling slowly into something stable.

The pool was quieter now. The bioluminescence on the walls pulsed in its slow, ambient rhythm. The sharp green smell had faded. The water smelled like water.

Quill had witnessed a civilization's entire relationship with death compressed into an hour.

It was not cold. He kept coming back to this. The efficiency looked like coldness from the outside, from the place where human grief stretched across months and years and sometimes never ended, where the shape of the dead was held and held and held because letting go felt like a second death. But the frogs did not hold the shape. They let the shape dissolve because that was what shapes did, because the water that had been Lenn was still water, because the irritation about pool temperature was now distributed across forty-eight living bodies who would carry that drop of him in their chemistry until they dissolved in turn.

He thought about the Puddle. About Vesh's voice saying "the water does not mourn the shape." He thought about the Almost, the involuntary synchronization, fifty frogs turning the same color for three seconds because even in grief the collective pull was there, the chord reaching for resolution. He thought about Lenn's last feeling passing through wet stone and into his palms, a dead frog's annoyance about temperature registering in the hands of a human biologist from Cork who studied animal consciousness and could not, at this moment, locate the boundary between studying a thing and being inside it.

He did not know if this was wisdom or the most sophisticated way of managing loss that any species had ever developed, a philosophy of grief designed around the biological fact that the grievers would themselves be dead within a decade or two.

He suspected the Wet Way would say those were the same thing. That there was no difference between a religion that helped you live and a truth that happened to be useful. Wet in, wet out. The puddle forms. The puddle dissolves. Holding the shape is unkind.

He sat with the question. He did not answer it. The water moved through the channels, carrying whatever remained of Lenn out into the settlement, into the ancient, damp, glowing infrastructure of a civilization that had been dissolving its dead and resuming its arguments for longer than his species had been walking upright.

Somewhere in the corridors behind him, frogs were returning to work. Somewhere, Oona was distributing food. Somewhere, Krrik was in his laboratory, having attended because it would have been noticed and left because the voss would not wait.

Quill sat at the edge of the pool with his notebook closed and his hands still wet and no idea what to do with what had passed through them.

---

# Chapter 13: Every Elegant System Leaks

Hart had been awake since before the fungal light shifted, which she had started using as a clock because her actual watch had died on Day 9 and nobody in a civilization capable of folding space had offered her a battery.

She met Talla at the junction where the main corridor split toward the communal pools and the narrower passages that led, as far as Hart could tell, to the parts of the settlement that did not appear in Noll's curated tour. Talla was waiting with the rigid posture of someone who had been standing still for exactly as long as her patience allowed.

"You said you wanted to see the edges," Talla said. "The edges are this way."

"Bon. Lead."

They walked. The corridors narrowed within fifty meters, the root walls closer together, the bioluminescent growth thinner and patchier. The light went from the settlement's usual dim blue-green to something sparser, concentrated in streaks rather than the even wash Hart had gotten used to. The stone underfoot was damper. Not the controlled dampness of the communal areas, where the water channels were engineered and the humidity managed. This was leaking. Moisture on walls that should have been dry. A faint smell Hart recognized from fieldwork in flooded forest: cellular stress, the sharp green chemical tang of plant tissue breaking down under pressure.

Frog life continued around them. This was not an abandoned sector. A family group occupied a shallow alcove to the left, two adults and three juveniles, the juveniles chasing something small and alive across the wet stone floor. Further on, an older frog sat on a ledge sorting dried insect preparations into shallow dishes, his movements unhurried, his olive skin showing the kind of chronic pallor that Hart associated with sustained low-level stress. He glanced at them as they passed. His colors did not change. He went back to sorting.

"This section houses roughly two hundred," Talla said. "Mostly families relocated from the western perimeter when the staging grounds expanded. The coordinators moved them here because the space was available. The space was available because nobody wanted it."

"Why not?"

Talla pointed at the wall. Hart looked. The root structure, which in the settlement's center was smooth and hardened to a warm, living solidity, was here swollen. Distended. Pale threads of something luminous pushed through the surface, pulsing faintly with a rhythm that was too slow to be mechanical and too regular to be random. The voss. Growing where it should not be growing, pressing into the architecture the way tree roots pressed into old foundations, except these roots were warm to proximity and pulsing with their own rhythm.

Hart crouched. The growth was warm to the touch. Warmer than the surrounding stone. She could feel it through her fingertips: a low, steady vibration, biological, unmistakable.

"How fast?" she asked.

"Two centimeters in the last four days along this corridor. Faster near the water channels. Slower where the stone is denser."

Hart stood. She wiped her hand on her trousers. Force of habit. Contamination protocol for a substance she could not classify using any framework McGill or the London School had given her.

The maintenance crew was three corridors further, where the voss growth had pushed through a wall joint and was reaching across the ceiling in pale, branching tendrils that caught the thin bioluminescence and threw it back in soft, pulsing waves. Two frogs were working on the growth directly, one applying a dark paste to the base of the intrusion while the other redirected a water channel away from the affected area. A third sat on a stone shelf nearby, eating, watching them work, and offering commentary.

"You are applying too much," the third frog said. He was compact, brownish, and clearly considered himself the authority on every aspect of maintenance work.

"I am applying the correct amount," said the frog with the paste, not looking up.

"The correct amount is less than that. If you saturate the base, the retardant seeps into the water channel and we spend the next two days flushing it out. I have told you this."

"You have told me this. You are wrong."

Hart watched them for a moment. She recognized the argument. She had witnessed identical disputes in every field hospital, every water treatment facility, every decontamination unit she had worked in. Two professionals, one methodology, no agreement. The specifics were alien. The dynamic was universal.

"What are you using?" she asked the frog with the paste.

Both workers turned. The frog on the shelf stopped eating.

"Chemical retardant," said the one applying it. "It slows the growth by disrupting the substrate's nutrient uptake. Temporarily. The effect lasts perhaps six to eight days. Then we reapply."

"And has it been working?"

"It has been working for eleven cycles." The frog sat back on her haunches. Her skin was a tired, washed-out green. Long shifts. "It has been working less well this cycle. The growth rate is faster. The retardant concentration was designed for standard expansion. This is not standard."

The frog on the shelf made a sound. "Tell her the rest."

The worker looked at him. Then at Hart. "The coordinators do not visit this side."

She said it without bitterness. Without drama. The way someone reports that the supply truck does not come on Tuesdays. A fact about how things are organized, observed without expectation that it would change.

Talla did not comment. She stood at the corridor's edge, her dark skin nearly invisible against the dimmer walls, her pale spots hidden. She let the worker's observation sit where it landed.

Hart took a sample of the retardant paste and a small scraping of the voss growth and put both in her field kit. She asked two more questions about the growth pattern and the application schedule. The workers answered directly, with the frankness of people who were used to explaining their problems to each other and had no practice explaining them to administrators.

The third frog finished eating. "Last cycle, maintenance had six teams on the outer sections. This cycle we have four. Two were reassigned to staging-ground support. The growth is faster and we have fewer people. This is not a complaint. It is arithmetic."

"It sounds like a complaint," said the frog with the paste.

"Arithmetic and complaints have the same structure. Both describe a situation where the numbers do not add up."

Hart looked at Talla. Talla's expression was carefully blank. She did not say I told you so. She did not need to.

They moved on. The breathing circle attendance station was not where Hart expected it to be. She had imagined something institutional, a desk or a counter or at least a visible administrative presence. It was an alcove. A small, recessed space off the main corridor that connected the outer residential sections to the communal pool complex. A single frog sat in it on a moss cushion, with what appeared to be a flat stone tablet and a stylus made from a hardened reed. He was small, pale green, with the mild, unremarkable coloring of someone who blended into any room he occupied.

Hart almost walked past it. That was, she realized, the point.

"What is this?" she asked.

Talla stopped. "Ask him."

Hart looked at the frog. "What are you recording?"

The frog looked up. His expression was pleasant. Patient. He was not thinking about the task very hard. "Participation," he said. "I note who attends the breathing circles. Names, pool affiliation, frequency."

"Why?"

"Because it is noted." He said this with the simplicity of someone stating that water was wet. "The coordinators review the patterns. Community wellness. It is helpful to know who participates and who does not."

"And what happens to the frogs who do not participate?"

The frog blinked. His colors did not shift. "I simply record. What is done with the records is not my concern."

The words hung in the damp air. Hart looked at Talla. Talla looked back. Neither of them needed to speak. The logging frog returned to his tablet and made a small notation about a name Hart could not read.

Hart had encountered this before. Not the specific mechanism. The specific quality. The mild, procedural compliance of someone performing a function whose consequences were someone else's responsibility. She had seen it in pharmaceutical companies, in hospital administrations, in government health agencies where the person filing the report and the person acting on it existed in separate moral universes connected by paperwork. The logging frog was not sinister. He was clerical. The system did not require sinister. It required bland.

They were past the attendance station and into the corridors leading to the relocated residential areas when Marsh found them.

She came from the direction of the eastern nursery, her field notebook in her back pocket, carrying a shallow stone dish of something dark and fermented that smelled sharp enough to make Hart's eyes water.

"Oona's grubs," Marsh said. "For the families in the relocated section. She sends them every other day. She says the children need the protein and the coordinators have not adjusted the food distribution to account for the new residents yet."

"How long have they been relocated?" Hart asked.

"Three weeks. Some longer."

"And the food distribution has not been adjusted."

Marsh set the dish down on a ledge near a family alcove, where two young frogs immediately approached it with the cautious enthusiasm of children assessing something they had been told was good for them. "The official line is that the relocation is temporary and the standard distribution will resume when they return to their original sections. The families say they have been told this twice before."

Hart looked at the alcove. It was smaller than the residential spaces she had seen in the settlement's center. Three adults and four juveniles shared it. The adults' skin showed the muted, unsettled patterns she had noticed on the older frog sorting insects earlier. Stress colors. Not dramatic. Not the flaring alarm signals she had seen in the treatment chambers. The low, persistent color shifts of people living with sustained uncertainty. Their children's colors were brighter, less affected, but the juveniles pressed closer to the adults than Hart had observed in the main nursery areas, where tadpoles and young frogs moved freely and independently.

"They look like refugee housing," Marsh said quietly. "I have seen this in Queensland after floods. The temporary camps that become permanent because nobody makes the decision to move them back."

Talla had been silent since the attendance station. She spoke now, and her voice was flatter than Hart had heard it. Not angry. Tired. "The relocation is real. The staging grounds need the space. The Pruning is real. The voss growth is real. Everything is real. That does not mean everything is fair."

"The two are not related," Hart said.

"The two are always related. The emergency is the reason. The unfairness is the cost. And the cost is paid by the people who live on the edges, not the people who coordinate from the center."

They stood in the corridor, the three of them, the muted sounds of displaced families behind them, the faint pulse of voss growth audible if you listened for it, a low hum transmitted through the damp walls.

"So what is the alternative?" Hart asked. "No breathing circles. No tracking. No synchronized displacement. What happens when the voss grows through this wall and the one behind it and the nursery floor?"

Talla's spots flared. She controlled them. "You sound like Noll."

"I sound like someone who has run disease response in four countries and learned that the people who complain about the quarantine are sometimes right and sometimes dead. I am asking you a real question, Talla. If you dismantle the chain, what holds?"

"I do not want to dismantle the chain."

"Then what do you want?"

Talla was quiet for a moment. In the alcove behind them, one of the young frogs had taken a grub from the dish and was eating it with total concentration, not yet old enough to eat and think at the same time.

"I want the chain to be visible," Talla said. "I want the frog who logs attendance to know what is done with his records. I want the families in these corridors to know when they will go home, or to be told honestly that they will not. I want the breathing circles to be what they claim to be, voluntary, or to stop claiming it." She paused. "That is the cruelty of it. The chain is also the rope that pulls you from the water."

Marsh, who had been listening with the particular stillness she used when she was thinking, said: "Maybe the tracking is not for control. Maybe it is for care. If you know who is not participating, you know who is struggling. Who is isolated. Who needs help."

Talla looked at her. "That is a kind reading."

"It is a possible reading."

"It is also the reading the coordinators prefer, because it makes surveillance sound like love. Hart, tell her."

Hart did not want to take sides. She took one anyway, because she had been thinking about it since the logging frog and the thought had arrived fully formed. "Tracking is control. It does not matter what the stated intent is. The moment you record who participates and who does not, you have created a mechanism for pressure, whether or not anyone applies the pressure today. The infrastructure is the coercion. The filing system is the threat. You do not need to punish anyone. You just need them to know the file exists."

Marsh did not argue. She did not concede. Her face was careful, listening. Not ready to accept or reject. She looked at the families in the alcove, at the children eating Oona's grubs, at the walls where the voss growth pulsed faintly through root and stone.

"Both things can be true," Marsh said. "Care and control. At the same time. In the same system."

"Both things are always true," Hart said. "That is what makes it difficult."

A frog passed them in the corridor, older, her skin a deep rust-brown mottled with age spots, her gait unhurried. She had been listening. Hart had not noticed her approach, which meant either the frog moved quietly or Hart had been too focused on the conversation. Probably both.

The older frog stopped. Her look at Talla carried the particular patience of a neighbor who had been overhearing these arguments for years.

"Talla, the chain also held when the water rose last cycle. My children are alive because of the chain."

Talla said nothing. Her spots flared and she let them. The older frog did not wait for a response. She continued down the corridor toward the family alcoves, carrying a bundle of dried moss under one forelimb.

The silence between the three of them held for five seconds. Six. The hum of the voss growth filled the space that words would have occupied.

Hart did not break it. There was nothing to break it with. The older frog was not wrong. The chain had held. Her children were alive. The breathing circles worked. The displacement operations saved the settlement every generation. The system that tracked attendance and relocated families and ran on the labor of four maintenance teams instead of six also kept two hundred million years of civilization from being swallowed by its own energy source.

Talla resumed walking. Hart and Marsh followed.

They walked back through the narrowing corridors, past the maintenance crew still arguing about retardant application, past the attendance station where the logging frog was making another notation, past the family alcoves where displaced frogs were eating and talking and living with the compressed patience of people waiting for a temporary situation to end.

Hart said nothing for the rest of the walk. She was composing.

The debrief happened that evening in the biologists' quarters. The stone floor was cool. The water channels hummed their low, constant note beneath the walls. Hart sat on the root ledge she had claimed as hers on the second day, her field notebook open, her sample kit beside her. The voss scraping and retardant paste sat in sealed containers. She did not know what she would do with them. She had taken them because taking samples was what she did when she did not know what else to do.

Reed was on the floor, cross-legged, his glasses pushed up, his notebook open to a page covered in diagrams that appeared to involve the voss growth rate and three different mathematical models. Vale sat against the far wall, his long frame folded into the low space with angular discomfort. He had been designed for lecture halls, not root chambers. Marsh was at the stone table, her own notebook closed, her pencil behind her ear.

Quill was in the doorway.

Hart told them. She started with the maintenance crew and the understaffing. She described the voss growth rate, two centimeters in four days along the outer corridors, faster near water. She described the relocated families, the unchanged food distribution, the stress patterns visible in every adult's skin. She described the attendance station and the logging frog and his flat, unremarkable compliance.

"The breathing circles are tracked," she said. "Participation is recorded. Non-participants are noted. The system is voluntary the way tax filing is voluntary. Nobody will arrest you. Everyone will know."

Vale nodded. His eyes were narrow and his mouth was set in the thin line that meant he had already arrived at the conclusion and was waiting for everyone else to catch up.

"And Talla?" Reed asked. "What does she propose instead?"

"She does not have an alternative. That is the problem. The Pruning requires the merging. The merging requires the circles. The circles require participation. She cannot oppose the mechanism without opposing the survival tool. She knows this." Hart closed her notebook. "The frogs are not lying about the Pruning. But they are using it. And the people who see that most clearly are the ones with the least power to do anything about it."

Vale's nod was small. He did not add anything. He did not need to. Hart saw it in his face: recognition. He had been mapping this structure since the first Speaking Chambers session. She had just walked through it.

Reed looked at the voss sample in Hart's kit. His discomfort was visible in the way he adjusted his glasses, pushed them up, adjusted them again. The cracks complicated his scientific admiration, and he was not ready to let the admiration crack.

Marsh said nothing. She was looking at her closed notebook. Hart suspected she was thinking about the children eating Oona's grubs in the alcove, about the care that existed alongside the control, about both things being true at the same time.

Quill looked out through the doorway at the bioluminescent pathways beyond.

He did not speak.

The fungal light pulsed its slow rhythm on the corridor walls. The water channels carried their quiet sound through the stone. Somewhere deeper in the settlement, a breathing circle was beginning, and somewhere on the outer edges, a maintenance frog was reapplying retardant to a wall that would need it again in six days, and somewhere in a recessed alcove, a mild green frog was recording who attended and who did not, and he was not concerned about what was done with the records, because that was not his job, and he was right, and that was the problem.

---

# Chapter 14: The Other Root

The displacement platform looked like a puddle someone had arranged on purpose.

Six flat stones, dark and wet, set in a rough circle on the floor of a chamber that smelled of ozone and stale water. Pale threads of fungal growth connected the stones in a pattern that might have been deliberate or might have been neglect. The chamber itself was unremarkable: low ceiling, damp walls, a single bioluminescent streak that provided the kind of light you would use to find your shoes, not to illuminate a miracle. Vale had seen more impressive bus shelters.

The guide was already there. She was compact, perhaps four centimeters, with flat, muted coloring that did not shift or signal or do any of the things Vale had learned to read in the other frogs. Her skin was the chromatic equivalent of a closed door. She sat on the edge of the nearest stone with the posture of someone who had been waiting for exactly this long and would wait exactly this long again tomorrow.

Marsh stood beside Vale, her field notebook already in her hand. She had not opened it. She was holding it the way some people hold a railing before a steep descent.

Talla arrived last, her dark skin catching almost no light in the dim chamber. She nodded at the guide. The guide did not nod back. They appeared to know each other in the way that people know each other when the knowing is professional and unrewarding.

"I have business at the station," Talla said. To nobody in particular.

"You do not," the guide said.

"I have business adjacent to the station."

The guide looked at her for one second, then turned to Vale and Marsh. "Stand on the stones. Do not think about your name. It will come back."

Vale looked at the stones. "What happens if I think about my name?"

"You lose it for longer. It is not dangerous. It is inconvenient."

Marsh stepped onto the nearest stone. It hummed. Not loudly. Vale felt it in his molars, a vibration that traveled up through the wet surface and into his feet and settled somewhere behind his teeth like a sound his skeleton was receiving without consulting his ears.

He stepped onto the stone beside Marsh. The humming increased. The fungal threads between the stones glowed, faintly, a pale luminescence that pulsed once, twice, and on the third pulse the chamber was not there.

Nothing was there.

Vale's first thought was that his first thought was missing. He reached for something, some word, some anchor, and there was a blank space where the anchor should have been. He existed. He was certain of this. He was less certain of who was doing the existing. The word for himself, the name, the label he had carried for forty-seven years, was gone, gone, like forgetting a colleague's name at a conference dinner, except the colleague was him.

Then the floor was wet and cold and his name was Thomas Vale and his knees were buckling.

He sat down. Not deliberately. His body made the decision and informed him afterward. The mind was still reassembling its filing system. The floor was stone. Cold stone. Different stone. The smell was different. The light was different. The ceiling was lower and rougher and the chamber was a fraction of the size of the one he had left, and he had left it, he was certain, approximately one second ago, though the second had contained a gap he could not measure because the instrument for measuring had briefly not existed.

Marsh was on her knees beside him, one hand flat on the stone, the other gripping his forearm. Her eyes were wide and unfocused. She was present but not entirely arrived.

"The nausea passes," the guide said. She was standing on a stone two meters away, upright, unbothered, as if she had stepped off a kerb. "The existential discomfort is more persistent but also passes. Please watch your step; the floor is wet."

Vale looked at his hands. They were his hands. He turned them over. Still his. The relief was disproportionate and real.

"My name," he said. "I lost it."

"Yes. It comes back. It always comes back. The stones take the label, not the person. You are still you. You are simply unnamed for a moment." The guide was already walking. "Most frogs find this refreshing. Humans do not. We attribute this to your attachment to nouns."

Marsh had gone quiet. Not the focused quiet of observation. A different quiet. She was staring at the wall of the new chamber with an expression Vale had not seen on her before: a kind of startled recognition, aimed at something that was not visible.

"Marsh."

She blinked. "I saw something. During the. In the middle." She stopped. Started again. "A sunset. Over canopy. From low down, from very close to the ground. The warmth of stone against skin. Not my skin. Skin that breathed. And a feeling of. Age. Patience. Something very old and very still."

The guide, who had begun walking toward the chamber's exit, paused. "Residue. The displacement field carries traces. Old travelers leave impressions. Like a fingerprint on a doorknob."

"Whose memory was it?" Marsh asked.

"Nobody's. Everybody's. The field does not label them." The guide resumed walking. "This way. Watch the ceiling. It is low and you are tall."

Talla had arrived on the stones behind them without apparent discomfort. She stepped off the platform the way someone steps off a train they take every week. Her spots were invisible in the dim light. She said nothing about the displacement. She was, Vale suspected, performing indifference, and performing it well.

The monitoring station was not a room so much as a long, low alcove carved into a root system, wider than it was tall, lit by streaks of bioluminescence and the faint glow of a patch of thinking moss in the corner. Six frogs occupied it. They were working. The word "working" covered a range of activities: two frogs sat near biological sensors embedded in the root walls, watching patterns that meant nothing to Vale. A third tended the thinking moss with a small vessel of water, adjusting the moisture levels with the absent focus of someone watering an office plant. A fourth was eating. A fifth was asleep, curled in a nesting hollow lined with dried insects arranged in careful geometric patterns that suggested either decoration or compulsion.

The sixth frog sat apart from the others, near a section of wall where something had been carved into the root surface. Vale could not read it. The guide, passing, said: "Poetry. Of a kind."

The station smelled of ozone and something organic and sharp. Vale counted the sensors. Twelve visible, embedded at intervals along the root walls, their surfaces faintly responsive to vibrations Vale could feel but not interpret.

"What are you monitoring?" he asked.

The frog nearest the sensors turned. She was olive-green with brown flecks, compact, with the unhurried posture of someone who had answered this question before. "Radio frequencies. Satellite transmissions. Ground-penetrating radar surveys. Logging activity. Mining prospectors. Research expeditions. Indigenous community movements. Individual human behavioral patterns within the alert perimeter."

She recited this like a customs officer listing prohibited items. Thoroughly. Without enthusiasm.

"Everything, then," Vale said.

"Within our range, yes. We maintain biological sensors in root systems and soil across approximately nine hundred square kilometers. The data integrates in real time." She gestured at the thinking moss. "She processes the patterns."

"She?"

"The array. We call her Mott. She is mostly reliable. She has moods."

The frog who tended the moss looked up. "She does not have moods. She has nutrient fluctuations that produce inconsistent outputs. That is not the same as moods."

"Last month she classified a toucan as a military reconnaissance aircraft."

"That was a nutrient fluctuation."

Vale looked at the frog who was eating. "And you? What do you monitor?"

The eating frog swallowed. "I am on break."

"From monitoring what?"

"From monitoring everything. It is the same data. We rotate positions every four hours. Except Giss." He nodded at the sleeping frog. "Giss takes the night shift and sleeps through the day and talks to her wall decorations. We have stopped asking about the decorations."

Marsh had drifted toward Giss's alcove and was examining the dried insect arrangements with the particular intensity she reserved for domestic detail. The insects were small, dark, arranged in spiraling patterns on the curved root wall. Not random. Intentional. Someone had spent hours on this, in a place where hours were the main available currency. Near the bottom of one spiral, a single bright wing from something larger had been fixed to the wall with resin. It caught the bioluminescence and threw back a faint blue.

"How long do postings here last?" Marsh asked.

The olive-green monitor considered. "Six to ten months, typically. Rotation depends on personnel and the coordination office's schedule, which is to say it depends on when someone remembers we are here."

"Do you like it?"

The monitor's colors did not shift. "It is quiet. The work is consistent. The food is adequate. I do not miss the settlement's social obligations. The breathing circles, the ceremonies, the endless conversations about fermentation technique." A pause. "I will deny having said that if asked in the presence of a coordinator."

The frog near the poetry wall had not spoken since they arrived. He was small, brown, and still, sitting with his hind legs tucked beneath him. When Marsh looked at his carvings, he turned half away. The marks were shallow, scratched into the root surface in tight rows. Not Threnn-song notation. Something private.

"Is that yours?" Marsh asked.

He did not look at her. "It is writing. I am not sure it is poetry. The guide is generous."

"What is it about?"

The frog considered this for several seconds. "Distance," he said. He did not elaborate. He turned back to the wall. Marsh let him.

Vale looked at the thinking moss. Mott. Six frogs in a damp alcove with a moody patch of moss, monitoring every human being within a day's walk of the settlement. The most comprehensive surveillance operation he had ever encountered, and it had the staffing levels of a rural post office: minimal, grudging, with one person on permanent break.

Talla, leaning against the alcove wall with her forelimbs folded, said: "Who do you report to?"

"Coordination."

"Which means Noll."

"Which means the coordination office. We file reports. The reports are reviewed. Decisions about response are made above our level."

"And who reviews the reports?"

"Coordination."

"And who reviews coordination?"

The monitor looked at Talla, recognized where the conversation was going, and returned to her sensors.

Talla turned to Vale. "Who watches the watchers?" she said. Not to Vale, exactly. To the room.

The guide, who had been sitting on a stone near the exit, cleaning something from between her toes with methodical attention, spoke without looking up.

"Nobody. That is the point. If you watch the watchers, you need watchers for the watchers, and eventually everyone is watching and nobody is doing anything useful."

Talla looked at her. "And if the watchers see something inconvenient?"

The guide's skin flickered. One shift, fast, a pale wash that crossed her muted coloring and vanished. She did not answer. She returned to cleaning her toes.

The silence sat in the alcove for three seconds. Four. The thinking moss pulsed softly in the corner. The sleeping frog turned in her insect-decorated hollow. The frog eating paused, glanced at Talla, and resumed chewing.

Vale filed it. The non-answer. The skin flicker. The guide's hands returning to a task that did not require her attention but gave her somewhere to put it. He had spent twenty years studying how institutions processed inconvenient information. The mechanisms varied. The silence was universal.

Marsh touched his arm. "Come outside."

They climbed through a narrow passage that angled upward through root and soil and stone, the guide ahead, Talla behind. The passage smelled of earth, rich and warm, warmer than it should have been. The soil beneath Vale's fingers, where he braced himself against the walls, was body temperature. He was crawling through something alive.

They emerged into forest.

The light was amber, late afternoon filtering through canopy that was thinner than Vale expected. He stood, brushed soil from his knees, and looked.

The root systems were wrong.

He was not a biologist. He could not have named the species or measured the deviation from baseline. But he could see that the ground was swollen. The forest floor bulged in long, slow ridges, root systems pushing upward like veins under skin, distending the soil in patterns that were too regular and too warm. He crouched and pressed his palm to the earth. Warm. Not sun-warm. Internal warm. The heat of something metabolizing beneath the surface.

Through cracks in the soil, light. Faint. Pale. The luminescence he had seen in the settlement's walls, the glow of voss threading through root systems, was here too, visible in narrow lines where the earth had split under pressure. The ground was glowing at the seams.

Trees were stressed. He could see that much. Canopy gaps where branches had thinned or dropped. One trunk, large, had tilted fifteen degrees from vertical, its root ball lifting from the soil on one side, displaced by something growing beneath it that did not care about the tree's schedule. Insects had gathered along the soil cracks, drawn to the warmth or the light. Small things, clustering at the edges of something they could not understand, which Vale considered a reasonable response.

Marsh had her notebook out. She was writing fast, her pencil moving in short strokes. She crouched beside a soil crack and held her palm over the luminescence.

"Warm," she said. "Warmer than the surrounding soil by at least five degrees. And there's something underneath, not just light. I can feel the hum. The same frequency as the settlement."

She moved to another crack, compared, wrote more. Talla watched her with her forelimbs folded and her spots still hidden.

"This is what Reed documented," Marsh said. "The anomalous root density. The canopy stress. He saw it from up there and didn't know what he was looking at."

Vale stood and looked at the forest. He was not thinking about biology. He was thinking about what Krrik had told Reed. About growth rates. About projections that kept failing because the voss kept exceeding them. About a living energy source that had powered a civilization for two hundred million years and was now, for reasons nobody fully understood, growing faster than the systems designed to manage it.

He was thinking about what it meant politically. A crisis that could not be denied because it was pushing through the floor. A crisis that required coordination, which required authority, which required the breathing circles, which required participation, which required the tracking Hart had already found.

This is what they're fighting, he thought. Not a metaphor. Not a political abstraction. A planet-sized organism pushing up through the soil.

The guide sat on a root and watched them. She had seen this view many times. It no longer registered.

"How far does it extend?" Vale asked.

"The growth pattern? This manifestation is approximately six kilometers along the root corridor. There are others. Many others. The coordination office tracks forty-seven active growth zones in the Amazon basin alone."

"And globally?"

"I am a displacement guide, not a surveyor. The Siberians report similar patterns. The African settlement has been relocating subsections. I do not have global figures. Nobody does. That is part of the concern."

Talla was standing at the edge of the cleared area, looking at the swollen ground. Her spots had appeared, pale against her dark skin, and she was not hiding them.

"Every generation," she said. "They push it back. It costs them. They push it back. It grows again. And every time, the response requires more coordination. More merging. More control." She looked at Vale. "The emergency is always real. The power it justifies is always permanent."

Vale did not answer. He looked at the ground, warm and luminous at the cracks, a living infrastructure bulging toward the surface like something that wanted to be seen, or did not care whether it was seen, which was worse.

Marsh closed her notebook. She had stopped writing mid-sentence. She was looking at the tilted tree, at the roots exposed by the displacement, and Vale thought she was seeing something he was not. Something domestic. How displacement upset a home.

"We should go back," the guide said. She had stood from her root and was already walking toward the passage. "The return is easier. The body learns."

They descended. The passage was warmer on the way down. The soil breathed against Vale's hands.

The return platform was identical to the one at the station: six stones, fungal threading, the faint hum. They stood on the stones. Vale braced himself.

It was easier.

The dissolution was briefer. Or it felt briefer. The name went and came back in the same instant, a blink rather than a blank, and the reassembly on the other side was smoother, the nausea a ripple rather than a wave. He stood on the Vreth-in-the-Root platform and his knees held and his hands were his hands and his name was where he had left it.

Marsh was steadier too. She did not grab for his arm. She stood, blinked twice, and looked at the guide. Her expression was careful, inward. Whatever she had caught in the field, sunset and stone and patience, it had come through again, or it had not. Vale could not tell and did not ask.

"The body learns that dissolving is temporary," the guide said. "Whether the self agrees is a personal matter."

She stepped off the platform and walked toward the corridor that led to whatever her next task was. She did not say goodbye. She did not offer reflections. She had transported two large primates and a dissident to a monitoring station and back, and now that task was complete, and there were presumably other tasks, and the tasks did not require commentary.

Talla was already in the corridor, moving in the opposite direction. She had shown them what she had come to show them. The monitoring station with its six frogs and its nine hundred square kilometers of total surveillance and its cheerful worker who would deny her own contentment in front of a coordinator. The soil glowing at the seams. The guide's silence when asked about inconvenient observations. She did not turn around. She did not check whether the lesson had landed. She walked.

Vale and Marsh stood on the platform for a moment. The fungal threading was dimming between the stones, the hum fading.

"That memory," Marsh said. "During the first displacement. It was so specific. Sunset. Warm stone. Patience. Someone sat in that exact spot and felt that exact thing, and it stayed in the field like a thumbprint." She looked at the stones. "I keep thinking about who it was. How long ago. Whether they're alive."

Vale did not know what to say to that. He dealt in systems, not ghosts. But the looseness was still in him, the thing the displacement had done to his edges, a softness in the joints of his certainty that he had not invited and could not entirely dismiss. For a few seconds, in the space between one floor and another, he had not been Thomas Vale. He had been a warm blank, a name-shaped hole, and the hole had not hurt. It had been, briefly and alarmingly, a relief.

He did not say this to Marsh. He was not sure he would say it to anyone. He straightened his shoulders and walked toward the corridor, and Marsh walked beside him, and neither of them discussed what had passed through them, or what had, for one measureless instant, stopped.

---

# Chapter 15: The Archive Remembers Selectively

The corridors grew quieter in layers.

First the voices dropped away. The overlapping conversations, the frog-to-frog croaking that echoed through the residential warrens at all hours, the distant splash of someone entering or leaving the communal pools. Then the water channels narrowed and their sound thinned from a constant murmur to a single thread of moving water, barely audible, running along the base of the wall like a whispered aside. Then the bioluminescence changed. Not dimmer, exactly. Slower. The fungal light in the main settlement pulsed with a rhythm Quill had started calling municipal, the reliable flicker of a civilization keeping time with itself. Here the light held still. It clung to the walls in pale, unmoving patches, patient, settled, old.

The stone underfoot was smoother than anywhere else in the settlement. Worn not by crowds but by the passage of individual feet over generations, the same path walked by archivists who came and went and were replaced by other archivists who took the same name and walked the same corridor and tended the same living records until they died and someone else inherited the title and the route. Quill's boots made a sound the stone was not accustomed to. Too heavy. Too deliberate. A human noise in a space that had been calibrated, over millions of years, for something smaller and quieter.

He had not planned this visit. He had not planned anything since the mourning ritual. That had been five days ago, maybe six. He had lost the certainty of counting. The accumulated weight of the past weeks sat in him without sorting itself into chronology. Vesh's philosophy. The breathing circle where something in his edges had softened. The mourning song where Lenn's irritation had passed through his skin and into his chest and sat there still, a dead frog's annoyance lodged in a living man's ribcage. Hart's debrief from the outer corridors, her flat delivery of facts that amounted to a verdict: the system works and the system costs and the people who pay are the people who always pay. Vale's quiet nod. Reed adjusting his glasses against a discomfort he had not earned. And Quill, in the doorway, silent, watching the others process what he could not yet name.

He had walked this morning without a destination and ended up here. That was not quite true. He had a destination. He did not have a question.

The archive entrance was not an entrance. It was a transition. The corridor widened gradually, the ceiling lifting, the walls pulling apart to reveal a space that had no clear boundary between where the passage ended and where the archive began. Root structures, thick and ancient, rose from the floor and disappeared into the ceiling, forming columns that were not columns because they were still alive, still growing, still carrying sap or moisture or something less classifiable through their pale, veined surfaces. Between the roots, shelving. But not shelving. Living tissue, arranged on stone platforms at various heights, each piece distinct, each piece growing.

Some of the tissue was the color of old paper. Some was translucent, threaded with faint luminescence. Some was dark, almost black, and pulsed with a slow rhythm that matched nothing else in the room. The air smelled of old mushrooms and mineral water and something faintly electrical, a static charge that Quill associated with thunderstorms and that seemed, here, to come from the tissue itself, from the stored memories running their quiet current through the archive's living substrate.

A junior archivist was crouched near a low platform, feeding a thin stream of nutrient solution from a clay vessel into a tissue culture. The tissue was pale green and glossy, its surface trembling faintly where the solution touched it. The archivist was young, small, with the bright, attentive coloring of someone recently assigned. She glanced at Quill with the brief, assessing look of a librarian noticing someone who did not have a card.

Pem was further in, sitting on a flat stone near the largest of the root columns. Sitting was approximate. Pem occupied the stone the way old furniture occupies a room, with a settled quality that suggested the stone had formed around Pem rather than the other way around. The oldest living frog in Vreth-in-the-Root was small and faded, the once-vivid coloring now a translucent grey-green, thin enough that Quill could see the faint shadow of internal structures beneath the skin. Bones, or their equivalent. Organs, or their approximation. The architecture of a body that had outlasted its species' usual warranty. Pem's eyes, though. The eyes were bright and clear and entirely present, fixed on Quill with an attention that contained no urgency.

Pem's skin shifted. A faint wash of color, so slow it was almost geological, passed from throat to forelimb. The junior archivist looked up, set down her clay vessel, and left without a word. Quill watched her go. The dismissal had been so quiet he almost missed it. A color change that meant: we will be alone now.

The archive settled around them. The tissue cultures pulsed on their platforms. The root columns breathed their imperceptible exhalations. Somewhere, water moved.

"You have been here before," Pem said.

"Once. Briefly. With Reed. You showed us the song archives and the spore records."

"I showed you the rooms. You were not ready for the archive."

Quill did not ask what that meant. He suspected it was not a riddle. Pem spoke the way geological formations spoke, by existing at a pace that made questions about timing feel beside the point.

"I wanted to ask about memory," Quill said.

Pem regarded him. "You may ask."

Quill looked at the tissue cultures. Each one was a preserved experience, a life compressed into chemistry, a dead frog's knowledge stored in something that was still growing. The scale of it defeated him when he tried to hold it whole. Two hundred million years. Hundreds of millions of individual lives, each one lived in the span of a few decades, each one carrying knowledge that would evaporate without the archive, without this room, without the frogs who sang to the tissue and listened for what came back.

"How does a species that lives thirty-five years maintain continuity across two hundred million?"

Pem did not answer immediately. Pem rarely answered immediately. The silence was not hesitation. It was the silence of someone whose internal timescale operated in generations, not sentences.

"We remember what we choose," Pem said. "Every generation curates its inheritance. What you call 'history' we call 'the story the archive tells this year.' It changes. Not the facts. The emphasis."

"The emphasis."

"A generation that faces the Pruning will call for records of previous Prunings. The archive obliges. The records are accurate. The selection is not neutral. A generation at peace will call for records of art, of philosophy, of daily life. Also accurate. Also not neutral."

Quill sat on the stone floor. Not because he needed to be lower but because standing in the archive had begun to feel presumptuous, as if his height was a form of interruption. From the floor he was closer to the tissue cultures on their low platforms. Their warmth reached him. Faint, biological, the heat of something metabolizing in slow time.

"So the archive decides what matters."

"The archivist decides what is available. What matters is decided by whoever is asking." Pem's bright eyes held Quill without blinking. Frogs did not blink the way humans did, Quill knew. The membrane passed laterally. But Pem's stillness was not anatomical. It was attentive. "The archive preserves everything it can. The archivist decides what to bring forward. These are different acts."

"Who decides what to preserve?"

"The dying. A frog approaching death may deposit memories into the substrate. The process is imperfect. Emotional coloring transfers. Factual detail drifts. What survives is not a record. It is an impression. A feeling with information attached."

Pem turned toward the nearest tissue culture, the dark, slow-pulsing one on the stone platform. Pem's mouth opened. What came out was not speech.

The sound was low, sustained, a single note that contained overtones Quill could hear and undertones he could feel in his sternum. Pem was singing. Not performing. Retrieving. The sound passed from Pem into the air and from the air into the tissue, and the tissue responded. Its surface rippled. The dark coloring shifted, paling in bands, and something moved through it, not physically, not visibly, but it was there, a change in the room's temperature, a faint smell of wet stone and sun-warmed leaves, and beneath that, like a photograph seen through water, the impression of sitting somewhere high and looking down at a canopy that stretched to the edge of vision, and a feeling that was not his feeling, a contentment mixed with something sharper, an awareness of running out, of time narrowing, of one more season and then the archive and then the long storage and then whatever came after storage, which was either continuation or silence depending on who you asked and which generation was asking.

Pem stopped singing. The tissue settled. The impressions faded. Quill sat on the stone floor with the residue of a dead frog's final afternoon still cooling in his chest.

"Whose was that?" he asked.

"An archivist. Three hundred years ago. Her name is in the record. She preserved her last season." Pem's skin showed a faint wash of blue, the color Quill had learned to associate with archival concentration. "The feelings transfer more reliably than the facts. She was content. She was also afraid. The archive holds both."

Quill was quiet. He was thinking about his father, who had died six years ago in a hospital in London, who had been content and afraid in roughly equal measure, who had told Quill in the last week that the morphine was making the ceiling interesting, which was either a joke or a genuine observation or both, and whose memories were stored in the only substrate available to humans: the imperfect tissue of his son's brain, degrading slightly each year, the emphasis shifting with each remembering.

He did not say this to Pem. He was not sure Pem would understand the comparison, or would understand it too well, which was worse.

"I wanted to ask about the humans."

Pem's eyes did not change. The bright, clear gaze held steady. But Pem's forelimbs, resting on the stone, shifted. A repositioning so slight it was almost nothing. The archivist's body adjusting to a subject it had anticipated.

"The humans who came before," Quill said. "Who found you. Who stayed."

"There have been several. Across the settlements. Across the centuries."

"What happened to them?"

"They lived among us. They were given shelter, purpose, companionship. They aged. They died. Their imprints are in the archive."

"Are the imprints available?"

Pem considered. The consideration took long enough that the nearest tissue culture completed a full pulse cycle, dark to pale to dark, before Pem spoke.

"The imprints are available. They are also private. Memory belongs to the remembered."

Quill recognized the deflection. It was institutional, not hostile. The same tone he had heard from university ethics boards, from hospital administrators, from anyone whose job was to hold the boundary between what was preserved and what was shared. The archive was generous with its method and careful with its contents. The machine was visible. It would not open.

"Can you tell me anything about them? Not the imprints. What you know."

Pem was still for a long time. Quill waited. He had learned, over these weeks, that waiting was itself a form of conversation in this place. The Threnn did not fill silences. They used them.

"The woman from the continent you call Africa. She was found by the Rift Pool settlement approximately four thousand of your years ago. She was a herbalist. She lived among them for eleven of your years. She learned our language to a functional degree. She sang poorly but with conviction. The archive preserves her singing. It is not good. It is valued."

Quill almost smiled. He held it. Smiling at a four-thousand-year-old woman's bad singing felt like the wrong register, except that it also felt exactly right, because what else survived of a life that distant except the specific quality of how someone sang when they were trying.

Pem continued without prompting. "The trader from the river. He arrived approximately eight hundred of your years ago. He had been separated from his party during a journey. He found the outer edge of this settlement. He was absorbed."

"Absorbed."

"Given shelter. Work. Companionship. Language. He found the food acceptable and the sleeping arrangements unbearable."

"Did he try to leave?"

The question sat in the archive's quiet. The tissue cultures pulsed. The root columns stood in their patient, living stillness.

"That information is part of his imprint," Pem said. "It is private."

Quill let it go. The shape of the answer lived in the refusal. The trader had tried to leave. Or had not tried. Either way, the leaving had not happened, and the archive held the details in the same careful, curated silence with which it held everything else.

"Was he happy?"

Pem's skin shifted. A slow, complicated movement of color that Quill could not read, grey-green deepening to something warmer, almost amber, and then fading back. Quill did not know if this was emotion or memory or the involuntary chromatic signature of an old body processing a difficult subject.

The silence lasted. Ten seconds. Fifteen. The archive breathed around them. Somewhere in the tissue, the stored impressions of thousands of dead frogs carried on their slow chemical persistence, and among them, perhaps, the impressions of a trader who had come from the river eight hundred years ago and lived the rest of his life in a place where nothing he knew applied and everything he learned could not be shared with anyone who would understand what he had lost.

"The trader from the river," Pem said. "He lived here for twenty-two of your years. Near the end, he told the archivist of his time that he had stopped missing his people. He was not sure if this was peace or damage. The archivist recorded both interpretations."

The words entered the archive's silence and stayed there.

Quill did not respond.

He sat on the stone floor with the warm tissue cultures pulsing around him and the root columns breathing their ancient patience and the sound of water somewhere distant and the words in him, peace or damage, the trader's words from eight centuries ago, a man who had stopped missing his people and did not know what that meant, and the archivist had not known either, and so both possibilities were preserved, because the archive's job was not to decide but to hold, and Quill was sitting in the place where both interpretations lived side by side in the same substrate, growing, pulsing, maintained by a frog who would die soon and be replaced by another Pem who would sing to the same dead and retrieve the same ambiguity for whoever came next.

He thought about his father again. The morphine and the ceiling. The joke or the observation. The emphasis shifting with each remembering.

He thought about himself sitting in this archive, his boots too heavy for the stone, asking about a man who had been dead for eight hundred years because the man's uncertainty was the thing Quill recognized most clearly in this entire civilization. Not the technology. Not the philosophy. Not the rituals or the governance or the impossible energy source or the frogs who folded space and defecated in trenches. The uncertainty. The man who could not tell the difference between peace and damage. That was the thing that fit.

He stood up. His knees were stiff from the stone floor. The archive did not care about his knees.

"Thank you," he said.

Pem said nothing.

The silence communicated. It said: I have given you what I chose to give. It said: what you do with it is yours. It said nothing at all.

Quill walked back through the transition where the archive became the corridor and the corridor became the settlement and the settlement's sounds returned in layers, the reverse of his arrival. First the water channels thickening. Then the bioluminescence quickening to its municipal pulse. Then voices, distant, overlapping, the hum of a civilization continuing its business in the usual way, which was the only way it knew, which was the only way it had known for longer than the human species had existed.

He walked back to the communal quarters.

The noise hit him at the doorway. Reed was standing near the root table, his glasses pushed up into his hair, his hands moving in the rapid, illustrative gestures that meant he was explaining something he found exciting and expected everyone else to find equally exciting. He was holding a flat piece of dried moss and pointing at patterns on its surface.

"The growth rates on the eastern arrays have diverged from Krrik's model by nearly twelve percent in the last four days, which means either the model is wrong, which Krrik will not admit, or the eastern substrate is responding to a variable we have not identified, which Krrik also will not admit but which he is obviously considering because he has reassigned two computation banks to pattern analysis without telling anyone, and I only know because I was in the laboratory this morning and noticed the nutrient redistribution, and the point is..."

Reed paused. Not because he had run out of momentum. Because he needed to breathe.

"The point is," he continued, "the thinking moss is finding something. The patterns are clustering. Something in the eastern growth is behaving differently from the rest, and nobody knows why yet, and I think the surface data I collected before we came down here might actually be relevant, which would mean that my original root density measurements, the ones everyone thought were boring..."

"Nobody thought they were boring," Marsh said, from the stone bench against the far wall. She was sitting with her legs crossed, her field notebook closed on her knee. Her pencil was behind her ear.

"Vale thought they were boring."

"I did not think they were boring," Vale said. He was on the floor against the opposite wall, his long legs extended, his back against the root surface. Hart sat on the ledge beside him, her arms folded, her field kit at her feet. "I thought they were irrelevant to the question of who was governing us. I have been corrected by events."

"The point is," Reed said again, "the moss is finding something, and I want to talk to Krrik about it, and he will be annoyed, and I do not care."

Hart looked at Vale. "He built a displacement system that folds matter across hemispheres. I am confident he can survive Reed's enthusiasm."

"The displacement system is elegant," Vale said. "Reed's enthusiasm is not."

"My enthusiasm is perfectly appropriate given that we are living inside the most significant biological discovery in the history of..."

"Elias." Marsh's voice was warm and steady. "You said the point. What is the ask?"

Reed stopped. Blinked. "I want someone to come with me tomorrow. To Krrik's lab. To look at the pattern clustering. In case I need a witness."

"A witness to what?"

"To Krrik admitting I was right about something. It has not happened yet. I want it documented."

Hart made a sound that was almost a laugh. Vale shook his head. Marsh smiled. Reed put down the dried moss and pushed his glasses back onto his nose and launched into a description of the nutrient redistribution pattern that Quill could follow for approximately thirty seconds before the technical detail outpaced his interest.

Quill sat down.

He sat on the root ledge near the doorway, the same spot he had occupied during Hart's debrief two days ago, the spot that was close enough to the group to be present and far enough from its center to be peripheral. He leaned back against the wall. The root surface was warm. Everything in this settlement was warm. The walls, the floors, the water, the air, the tissue cultures in the archive where a dead trader's uncertainty was being kept alive by a frog who would die soon and be replaced by a frog with the same name.

Nobody asked him what was wrong.

Reed was still talking. Vale was listening with the precise, critical attention he gave to anything that might reveal a power structure. Hart was watching Reed's hands as they drew invisible diagrams in the air, her expression carrying the particular skepticism she reserved for information she had not verified personally. Marsh had opened her notebook and was writing something that might have been notes on Reed's presentation or might have been her own observations from the day. She glanced at Quill once, briefly, and went back to writing.

Quill sat with the trader's words.

Peace or damage. Eight hundred years in the archive, held in both interpretations, growing in the dark, neither one winning, neither one fading, the ambiguity itself preserved and tended and passed from one archivist to the next like an heirloom nobody could appraise.

He was not sure if this was peace or damage.

The fungal light pulsed on the corridor walls outside. Reed's voice filled the room with patterns and percentages and the particular joy of a man who had found something he could measure. The water channels carried their sound through the stone. The settlement continued. It always continued. It had been continuing for longer than Quill could hold in his mind without the number becoming abstract, without the scale of it collapsing into a feeling rather than a fact, and the feeling was the one the trader had described, or the one Pem had described the trader describing, the selected emphasis, the story the archive told this year about a man who stopped missing his people and could not tell if that was freedom or erosion.

Quill sat among his friends and held it alone.

---

# Chapter 16: The Siberian Argument

The largest Speaking Chamber in Vreth-in-the-Root could hold two hundred frogs in its tiered mineral pools. Reed counted sixty-three on his way in and stopped counting because more kept arriving.

They came from two directions. The Amazon frogs descended the root-carved steps in clusters of three and four, their colors conversational, shifting through greens and ambers and the occasional flare of orange that meant someone had just said something worth repeating. The Siberians materialized on the displacement stones at the chamber's western end in groups of five, compact and grey and still, stepping off the platform with the unhurried economy of commuters exiting a train they had taken a thousand times. The water temperature dropped a degree each time a new group arrived. Nobody adjusted the heating. The Siberians, apparently, did not consider the cold worth mentioning. The Amazon frogs considered it a cultural aggression too petty to acknowledge and too annoying to ignore.

Reed sat on an upper ledge near the chamber's eastern wall, his notebook on his knee, his pen already moving. The ledge was wet. Every surface in the settlement was wet. He had stopped caring about this roughly nine days ago and now occupied the damp stone with the resigned acceptance of a man who had reclassified "dry" as a theoretical state.

Marsh and Hart were across the chamber, two ledges below. Hart was leaning toward Marsh and saying something that made Marsh glance at the Siberian delegation with the focused calm she used when cataloguing a new variable. Vale was further down, near the second-ring ledges, positioned with a clear sightline to both Noll at the center and the Siberian contingent at the western pools. He had his arms folded and his head tilted at the angle that meant he was mapping power dynamics before the first word was spoken. Quill sat alone near the top of the chamber, watching the room fill, his hands in his lap, his expression the quiet, interior look that Reed had stopped trying to interpret because Quill's interior had become a place Reed could not follow.

Below, in the warm mineral water, sixty-odd frogs arranged themselves across the tiered pools with the practiced informality of a species that had been holding these meetings for longer than mammals had existed. The Amazon frogs spread across the upper and middle tiers. The Siberians occupied the lower western pools in a tight formation that was not exactly military but was not exactly casual. Between the two groups, a strip of unoccupied water ran like a border nobody had drawn but everyone respected.

The hum was enormous. Not loud. Enormous. Sixty-plus frogs vibrating at slightly different frequencies, their bodies producing overlapping pulses that traveled through the mineral water and up through the stone and into Reed's teeth. It was the sound of a building-sized tuning fork operated by a committee that disagreed about the pitch.

Noll opened the meeting with three sentences. Reed could not follow the formal phrasing because Noll's diplomatic register required a fluency in bureaucratic indirection that Reed had never developed and refused to cultivate. Something about coordination. Something about shared purpose. Something about the honored presence of the Permafrost delegation and the sustained collaborative relationship between the Federations.

The Siberian lead did not wait for Noll to finish his fourth sentence. She was the same scarred female who had disrupted the planning session weeks ago, broader than the Amazon frogs, her flat grey coloring absorbing light in a way that made the vivid Amazon greens look theatrical by comparison. She spoke from the western pool without rising, without preamble, without any of the chromatic signals that the Amazon frogs layered onto every public statement.

"The growth models your engineer has shared are inaccurate. The Siberian monitoring network confirms a divergence of nineteen percent from the projections you circulated. This is not a criticism. Your models were built on historical patterns. The patterns have changed."

The chamber's colors shifted. A ripple of amber and pale green passed through the Amazon contingent, moving from the lower tiers upward, a visible wave of reaction that reminded Reed of nothing so much as a stadium crowd responding to a disputed call. Several frogs near Noll turned to look at Krrik, who was sitting at the chamber's edge near a slab of thinking moss, his brown-green skin cycling through rapid unconscious patterns that meant he was processing something and had probably not noticed that sixty frogs were now looking at him.

Noll's blue held. Steady, controlled. His damaged forelimb showed a faint amber flicker, which Reed had learned was the only unrehearsed signal in Noll's entire repertoire.

"The coordination office has noted the divergence," Noll said. "Updated projections are being prepared. The collaborative framework accommodates new data without requiring a reassessment of foundational methodology."

The Siberian lead's grey skin did not shift. "Your foundational methodology includes mandatory consciousness-synchronization for displacement coordination. We coordinate displacement technically, without the synchronization requirement. We have done so for sixty million years. Our displacement accuracy exceeds yours by a factor I will not state in open session because your frogs will find it humiliating."

A sound moved through the Amazon frogs. Not a word. A vibration, low and collective, carrying offense in its frequency. Reed's sternum registered it before his ears did.

The Siberian lead continued without acknowledging the reaction. "You do not need to merge consciousness to coordinate displacement. You need to merge consciousness because you enjoy it and have confused enjoyment with necessity."

The chamber erupted. Not into shouting. Frogs do not shout. Into color and vibration, dozens of bodies producing overlapping signals in the pools, greens brightening, ambers flaring, a physical argument conducted simultaneously through three channels humans could only partially read. Two Amazon frogs on the fourth tier began a rapid exchange in Threnn-song that was plainly combative, their colors flickering through sequences Reed associated with strong disagreement. A cluster of Siberians in the lower western pool sat motionless, their grey untouched, watching the display with the patient boredom of people who had caused this reaction before and intended to cause it again.

On the far side of the chamber, Marsh leaned toward Hart and said something. Hart was watching the Siberians with the narrow, cataloguing attention she gave to organisms she had not yet classified. Reed knew what she was seeing: the physical differences. The Siberians were built differently. Broader, thicker-skinned, their bodies denser and more compact, adapted for temperatures that would kill most Amazon frogs in hours. Their colors did not shift because their skin was engineered for insulation, not communication. They expressed nothing on their surfaces. This, Reed understood suddenly, was why the Amazon frogs found them so irritating. A frog that did not display was a frog that could not be read, and a frog that could not be read could not be trusted, and a frog that could not be trusted was standing in your pool making accurate observations about your foundational methodology.

Then Talla spoke.

She was in the middle tiers, near a group of younger Amazon frogs who shifted slightly to give her space. The small adjustment had a practiced quality. They knew what was coming. Her dark skin caught almost no light. Her pale spots were visible, which meant she was not controlling them, which meant she did not care who knew she was feeling strongly.

"The Siberians coordinate displacement without the merging protocol," Talla said. Her voice was flat, directed at the room, at nobody in particular. "They have been doing this for longer than the current merging requirement has existed. Their accuracy is documented. Their operators are not hospitalized." She paused. "The consciousness-merging may improve coordination. That is debatable. What is not debatable is that the mandatory participation cannot be justified by the operational need when an alternative exists."

The chamber went quiet. Not the vibrating quiet of sixty frogs humming at different frequencies. Actually quiet. The water stopped carrying competing signals. Reed could hear, for the first time since he had entered, the ambient sound of the settlement's water channels behind the walls.

An Amazon frog aligned with the Siberians against her own settlement. Reed did not need Vale's political expertise to understand what had just happened. Across the chamber, Vale leaned forward, hands unclenching from where they had been folded, his head turning between Talla and Noll and back again with the rapid calculation of someone watching a move he had not anticipated.

The Siberian lead looked at Talla. Her grey skin showed no signal, but her posture adjusted, a fractional reorientation toward the dark frog in the middle tiers that carried, in the rigid Siberian body language, the approximate weight of a standing ovation.

Noll's blue did not waver. "Talla-of-the-Third-Pool's perspective is valued and has been registered in the coordination record."

"It has been registered," Talla said. "It has not been addressed."

From the eastern wall of the chamber, near Krrik and the thinking moss, a Siberian frog Reed had not noticed before turned and looked at him. The frog was smaller than the other Siberians, mottled grey with flecks of dark blue at the joints, and was clearly at this meeting under protest because the meeting was happening where the data was. An engineer. Reed recognized the type because he was the type.

The Siberian engineer hopped from the pool edge to the dry stone beside the thinking moss and said, without introduction: "You are the human who collected the surface data."

"I am. Reed. Dr. Elias Reed. I collected root density, soil chemistry, and canopy behavior data during our primate research before we, before we came down here, and the patterns in the soil chemistry were showing accelerated mineral displacement along the root corridors, which at the time I attributed to seasonal variation, but now."

"Show me."

Reed showed him. He had been carrying the data for days, transcribed onto a dried moss sheet Krrik had given him because human electronics did not function in the settlement's humidity and because Krrik considered electronic data storage "touching, in the way a child's drawing is touching." The Siberian engineer studied the transcription while the political argument continued behind them. Noll was saying something about procedural frameworks. The Siberian lead was saying something about procedural frameworks being decorative. Vesh had begun speaking from the upper pools, his gold flecks bright, his voice carrying the serene, rolling cadence of a frog explaining cosmic unity to frogs who considered cosmic unity a quaint regional preference.

"The merging is not a tool," Vesh said, and Reed heard it over his shoulder while the Siberian engineer traced a root-density gradient on the moss sheet. "The merging is the purpose. The displacement coordination is what it does. The Almost is what it is. To say 'you can coordinate without merging' is like saying 'you can eat without tasting.' Correct. Also missing the point of being alive."

The Siberians' response to Vesh was visible even from across the chamber.
