Their grey surfaces remained unchanged, but three of them turned fractionally away from him mid-sentence, the Siberian equivalent of walking out of a lecture. One Siberian near the front produced a single, brief color change: a flat wash of blue-grey that came and went in half a second. Reed did not know the Siberian chromatic vocabulary, but it looked like the visual equivalent of someone checking their watch.

"Your surface readings," the Siberian engineer said, pulling Reed's attention back. "The depth markers. These indicate root-level voss activity visible from above the soil."

"Yes. Canopy stress, displaced root balls, luminescence through soil cracks. I documented it during our initial survey. I thought it was geological. It is not geological."

"It is not geological." The engineer's grey skin did something Reed had not seen a Siberian's skin do: it shifted. Briefly. A pulse of darker grey that moved across the engineer's back and vanished. "Our subsurface sensors have not registered surface emergence. In any settlement. In the full archive of monitoring data."

Reed's glasses were already in his hair. He pushed them up without thinking, found hair instead of nose, and left them there. "Wait, wait, wait. You're saying surface-level voss emergence has never been recorded?"

"I am saying it has never occurred."

"In how long?"

"In the monitoring record. Which begins approximately one hundred and fifty million years ago, with gaps."

Reed sat with this. The political argument behind them had moved on. Talla was saying something sharp about operational transparency. The Siberian lead was asking Noll a question that was shaped like a question but functioned as an accusation. Forty frogs were producing overlapping vibrations of agreement, disagreement, and what were almost certainly hunger-related complaints from a small group near the food stores who had been there since before the meeting started and were growing restless.

But here, on the stone ledge beside the thinking moss, the Siberian engineer was studying Reed's surface data with a careful, unhappy focus. Her models had just failed in the most informative possible way.

"Your instruments are primitive," the engineer said.

"Extremely."

"Your methodology is crude."

"By your standards, everything we do is crude. By our standards, we mapped the human genome with the same basic approach. Pattern recognition and patience."

The engineer's dark blue joint-flecks pulsed once. "Your primitive instruments looked where ours did not. We monitored from below. You documented from above. The voss has reached the surface. This has never happened." A pause. "Your data is useful. I will not say it is good. It is useful in the way that a broken window is useful for telling you about the wind."

Reed almost smiled. The grudging acknowledgment carried more weight than praise would have. He had lived in academia long enough to know that the most honest compliment a scientist could receive was someone reluctantly admitting they needed your data.

Krrik materialized at the edge of the thinking moss. Reed had not seen him move. Standard for Krrik. The frog navigated the settlement like a chess piece, arriving at positions without appearing to cross the intervening squares.

"You are looking at the surface emergence data," Krrik said to the Siberian engineer.

"I am looking at data that should not exist."

"The growth rate in the eastern sectors has exceeded my projections by twenty-three percent in the last six days. I have revised the model four times. Each revision has been wrong within hours."

The engineer's grey shifted again. That brief, dark pulse. "Our northern quadrant shows seventeen percent. The Rift Pools report twenty-one. The Canopy Clusters have not reported. They do not report on time. They have never reported on time. This is a separate complaint."

A frog from the crowd, small and olive-green, perched on a ledge above them and interjected: "The Canopy Clusters believe timely reporting is a form of spiritual rigidity. They have held this position for forty million years. We have stopped arguing."

Krrik ignored this. "If I combine Reed's surface data with your subsurface readings and the Rift Pool reports, the growth curve suggests this is the most severe Pruning in the accessible archive. Centuries. Possibly millennia."

The word landed in the space between the three of them and sat there.

Reed looked at the chamber. The political argument was still running. Noll was speaking in careful, measured sentences. The Siberian lead was responding in sentences that were neither careful nor measured. Vesh had said something that prompted a Siberian to observe, loudly enough for the entire western pool to hear, that the Amazon frogs had spent eighty million years croaking about oneness while simultaneously traveling to other stars to confirm that nobody out there wanted to hear about it either.

The laughter from the Siberian contingent was brief, controlled, and devastating.

But beneath the political noise, something was happening. The data was converging. Reed knew it the way he always knew good data, in the pattern that emerged when you stopped trying to make the numbers tell a story and let them tell their own. Three independent monitoring networks, plus his surface observations, plus the thinking moss's own pattern analysis, all pointing at the same conclusion. The growth was accelerating. The growth was anomalous. The growth had reached the surface.

"Can I see your northern quadrant data?" Reed asked the engineer.

The engineer looked at him for two seconds. Measured him. Then hopped to the thinking moss and pressed one forelimb against its surface in a sequence practiced and specific, a password on a familiar keyboard. The moss responded. Not visually. The vibrations beneath him changed, sharpened, and the moss surface shifted, patterns forming in its fibrous texture that meant nothing to Reed but that Krrik and the engineer bent toward simultaneously with the focused hunger of two people who spoke the same language.

Krrik produced a sharp orange flash across his skin. "This is wrong."

"It is not wrong," the engineer said. "It is what we measured."

"I do not mean your data is wrong. I mean the growth is wrong. It should not be doing this. The voss should not be at the surface. The voss has never been at the surface. Something in the radiation pattern is different, or something in the substrate response has changed, or both, and I do not know which and I do not have enough computation capacity to model all three variables simultaneously."

"We brought additional arrays. They are being cultivated in the transit pools. They will be operational in two days."

"Two days is adequate. Two days ago would have been better."

"We were traveling. We do not enjoy the process and do not hurry."

The political argument had reached a temporary lull. Noll was proposing something procedural. Reed could hear his voice, calm and structured, threading a path between the Siberian bluntness and the Amazon frogs' chromatic indignation. And then, below the procedural language, below the political performance, Reed saw it happen.

The room changed color.

Not a ripple. Not a wave of chromatic reaction from one faction to another. A change. Sixty-odd frogs, Amazon and Siberian, their colors moving toward the same shade at the same time. A pale, washed green that Reed had never seen in the settlement, a color that no individual frog seemed to choose but that the room arrived at collectively, a field of grass bending in the same wind. The Siberians, whose grey surfaces rarely moved, showed the faintest flush of the same green at their throats and along their forelimbs. For three seconds the entire chamber was the same color, and the hum that ran through the water and the stone and Reed's teeth resolved into a single low frequency, and sixty frogs were feeling the same thing at the same time.

Fear.

Reed had never seen collective fear as a visible phenomenon. He had seen it in herds, in flocks, in primates freezing before a predator. But this was different. This was sixty individual minds arriving at the same conclusion simultaneously and broadcasting it through their skin, and the conclusion was that the thing they had been managing for two hundred million years was, this time, larger than the management.

The color held for three seconds. Four. Then it broke. Individual patterns returned. The Amazon frogs shifted back toward their conversational greens and ambers. The Siberians' grey reasserted. Voices resumed. The political argument picked up where it had paused. But the room had said something together that no individual had said alone, and everyone in the chamber had heard it, and Reed sat on his wet stone ledge and understood that the data he had been carrying in his notebook, the root density measurements and soil chemistry readings he had collected while watching monkeys, the boring surface observations that nobody on his team had thought were relevant, had just become part of something planetary and old and frightened.

Quill, at the top of the chamber, was watching Vesh and Talla. From across the tiered pools, his attention was fixed not on the political argument or the scientific data but on the two frogs who sat three rows apart and did not look at each other. The tension between them was visible in what they did not do. Talla had spoken against the merging protocol, and Vesh had defended it, and now they occupied the same chamber with the practiced distance of two people whose disagreement was older and more personal than any policy debate.

Reed filed it. He filed everything. It was how his mind worked: data first, interpretation later, the accumulation of observations that would eventually arrange themselves into a pattern he could not yet see.

The chamber was emptying. Frogs climbed from the pools in small groups, Amazon and Siberian mixed, still arguing, still producing overlapping vibrations that filled the corridors with the residual hum of unfinished business. Noll left with the Siberian lead beside him, their conversation private, Noll's blue controlled and his damaged forelimb tucked carefully inward. Vale followed them at a distance that was precisely calibrated to look casual. Hart and Marsh descended the eastern steps together, Marsh writing in her notebook as she walked, Hart's field kit bumping against her hip.

Quill sat where he was. He was watching something Reed could not see. He had been doing that more often.

At the chamber's edge, Krrik and the Siberian engineer were still at the thinking moss. The chamber was nearly empty. The water in the pools rippled faintly with the residual vibrations of a meeting that had not resolved anything and had clarified everything. The two engineers were arguing. Krrik's orange flashed in rapid bursts. The Siberian's grey showed its dark pulses. Their forelimbs moved across the moss surface, pulling data, comparing patterns, adjusting models that were already obsolete by the time they ran.

They were not agreeing. They were doing something better than agreeing. They were working.

Reed pushed his glasses down from his hair onto his nose and walked toward them. The moss hummed beneath his boots. The water channels carried their sound through the stone. The civilizational machinery of survival was engaging, and Reed, with his transcribed surface data and his borrowed moss sheet and his primitive human instruments, was going to stand next to it and be useful.

It was the least, and the most, he could do.

---

# Chapter 17: After Hours

Hart could not sleep.

She lay in the shared quarters listening to the others breathe. Marsh slept on her side, field notebook on the stone beside her like a second pillow. Reed slept restlessly, glasses folded on the ledge, legs tangled in the thin woven blanket the frogs had provided, which was damp because everything was damp. Vale slept on his back with his arms crossed, a posture so controlled it looked rehearsed. Quill's corner was empty. He had been coming back later and later, and Hart had stopped asking where he went because the answer would involve pools and breathing and something she did not want to have an opinion about.

The fungal light pulsed on the walls. The water channels ran their constant low murmur through the stone. The ambient hum of the settlement carried through every surface, a frequency she could feel in her teeth if she pressed her jaw together, which she did now, testing, the way she tested water samples and skin rashes and food stores. Hart tested things. That was what she did. She tested things until she found the contamination, and then she pointed at it until someone listened.

Tonight the thing she could not identify was inside the testing apparatus.

She got up.

The corridors at night were different. During the day: crowded, noisy, frogs moving in clusters, croaking layered so thick it became ambient. At night the crowds were gone. The bioluminescence, which during the day was scattered and absorbed by hundreds of small moving bodies, now had nothing to diffuse it. The light was brighter. The pale blue-green glow clung to every surface with an intensity the daytime concealed, and the walls looked, for the first time since Hart had arrived, like what they were: a living thing, breathing, pulsing, older than anything she had a framework for.

The water channels were louder in the relative quiet. A sound with texture she had not noticed when it was competing with six hundred conversations about fermentation.

Hart walked.

She did not have a destination. She was not conducting a survey or cataloguing a biohazard or following Talla into the parts of the settlement that proved her right. She was walking because the alternative was lying in the dark testing her own teeth against a vibration she could not classify.

The practice pools were three corridors from the residential quarter, down a passage that sloped and opened into a wide, shallow cavern with a low ceiling. She stood at the entrance and watched.

Twelve displacement operators sat in the warm water, arranged in a loose arc, their bodies half-submerged. They were drilling. Hart could tell it was a drill because the movements had the deliberate, repetitive quality of rehearsal, the same pattern cycled and cycled until the body forgot it was being told what to do. Their colors flickered in synchronized patterns, rapid, precise, each frog shifting through the same sequence at the same instant, their skin throwing light across the wet stone walls in moving bands of green and gold and pale violet. The reflections rippled on the ceiling. A light show without an audience, performed in the small hours for no one, because the machinery of planetary defense did not care what time it was.

Hart watched from the corridor entrance. The operators did not notice her or did not care.

The synchronization was tight. Eleven of the twelve held the pattern cleanly, their colors clicking through the sequence with the precision of something mechanical. The twelfth was slower. Half a beat behind, her colors dragging, the greens arriving a fraction late and the violets muddy. She was young and she was tired and her body was broadcasting both. The frog beside her adjusted slightly, compensating, pulling the rhythm back into alignment the way a musician covers for a missed beat. Nobody spoke. Nobody stopped. The drill continued.

She left before anyone looked up.

The fermentation argument was audible from twenty meters away.

Hart followed the sound down a side passage and found four frogs in a shallow alcove lit by a single streak of bioluminescence on the far wall. They were arranged in a rough square, each occupying a wet stone ledge, each leaning forward with the aggressive posture of someone who had been making the same point for a very long time and was not going to stop now.

"Eighteen degrees," said the largest frog, a heavy olive female whose throat patch was flaring orange with conviction. "Eighteen degrees for the first four days, then a gradual reduction to fourteen over the following six. This is the method. This has always been the method."

"This has been your method," said the frog across from her, smaller, darker, his skin cycling through irritated yellows. "Your method produces a larval culture that tastes like mineral sediment. I have been telling you this for nine years."

"You have been wrong for nine years."

"The optimal temperature," said a third frog, this one pale and young and apparently serving as the argument's moderator, "is sixteen point five. I published this in the fermentation collective's seasonal review. The data is clear."

"Your data is from a single batch," said the fourth frog, who had not spoken until now and who delivered his opinion with the weary authority of someone who had outlived several rounds of this dispute. "A single batch in a controlled alcove with artificial humidity. You cannot generalize from a single batch. I have been fermenting beetle larvae for fourteen of my twenty-one years and I can tell you that the optimal temperature depends on the larvae source, the water mineral content, and whether the moon is visible through the canopy vents, which nobody believes but which I have documented."

"The moon," said the olive female.

"The moon."

"You are blaming the moon for your bad fermentation."

Hart had stopped walking. The four frogs noticed her simultaneously. Their colors shifted, a brief collective ripple of surprise that resolved into curiosity.

"Human," said the olive female. "You are the one who studies contamination."

"I am."

"You have a scientific mind. Settle this. Eighteen degrees or sixteen point five?"

"I study parasites and toxins, not fermentation."

"Fermentation is controlled rot," said the dark frog with the irritated yellows. "That is your area."

Hart opened her mouth to decline and found herself considering the question. Controlled rot was, in fact, her area. The four frogs watched her with the focused attention of people who had been waiting years for an impartial judge and were prepared to reject the verdict immediately.

"I would need to see the larvae, the water chemistry, and the temperature controls before forming an opinion."

The four frogs erupted. Not at her. At each other. The olive female declared this proved the need for systematic testing. The dark frog said systematic testing was what he had proposed nine years ago. The young moderator insisted his published data already constituted systematic testing. The fourth frog said the moon was a variable and he would die on that ledge.

Hart left them to it. Behind her, the argument continued at full intensity, four voices overlapping in the dim alcove, the settlement's two-hundred-million-year-old architecture absorbing the sound of four frogs fighting about beetle larvae in the small hours of a night nobody else was using.

The corridor that led toward the settlement's outer edge was quieter. Narrower. The bioluminescence thinned to scattered patches, and the stone underfoot was older, smoother, worn by generations of small feet traveling the same route. Hart's boots were loud here. She was aware of them. She was always aware of them in this place, the heaviness of her steps in a civilization scaled for something lighter.

A sound from ahead. Scraping. Methodical, rhythmic, the sound of a tool against wet stone.

The cleaner was working in a section where the corridor bent and widened into a junction. She was older, her green faded to something between sage and grey, her movements deliberate and unhurried. She held a flat tool, stone-edged, and was scraping mineral buildup from the base of the wall where the water channel met the floor. The scrapings fell into a shallow dish at her feet. The smell was sharp, mineral and astringent, like vinegar cut with wet chalk.

She saw Hart and did not stop working.

"You are the human who walks at night," the cleaner said. "I have heard about you."

"You have heard about me."

"I hear about everyone. I have cleaned this corridor for twelve years. Twelve of my twenty. More than half my life on this stretch. I know who walks here, when they walk, and what their walking means. The families in the eastern alcoves argue on a fourteen-day cycle, which is also their mating cycle, which nobody admits. The engineer from the computation banks comes through at the same hour every night to check the nutrient flow, and he thinks nobody notices, but I notice because he tracks moss residue and I am the one who cleans it. The young frog from the displacement training pools, the one who keeps falling behind in the drills, she comes through here crying three nights out of five, and I leave a clean ledge for her to sit on because that is what I can do."

Hart leaned against the corridor wall. The stone was warm. The cleaner's voice had the steady, unperformative quality of someone who was not trying to impress, instruct, or convert. She was simply talking. Hart had not heard someone simply talk in weeks.

"The archive," the cleaner said, and she scraped a long, curling strip of mineral deposit from the stone and deposited it in her dish with practiced accuracy, "keeps the memories. The songs and the tissue and the spores. Pem tends the dead. That is important. Everyone agrees that is important." She worked another section of the wall, her movements economical, decades of practice compressed into the angle of her wrist. "But who tends the living corridor? Who scrapes the buildup so the water runs clean? Who notices that the young operator cries? Who knows that the family in the third alcove has a sick juvenile they have not brought to the practitioners because they are afraid the practitioners will report the illness to the coordinators, who might reassign them again?" She looked at Hart. "We are the real archive. Pem keeps the dead. I keep the living."

Hart did not respond immediately. The cleaner did not require a response. She returned to her wall, her flat tool finding the next ridge of mineral deposit with the practiced certainty of decades.

"Why do humans not clean communally?" the cleaner asked.

Hart thought about how to explain it. "We hire people to clean. We pay them. Many people consider the work beneath them."

The cleaner stopped scraping. She turned to Hart. Her faded green skin shifted through a slow display of colors that Hart had not seen before, a rippling sequence that moved from her throat to her forelimbs and back, cycling through greens and ambers and finally settling on a pale, bewildered grey.

"Beneath them," the cleaner repeated.

"Yes."

"The work that keeps the corridor alive. The work that keeps the water clean and the walls from growing closed and the mineral buildup from blocking the channels that carry the sound that carries the decisions that keep the settlement functioning. That work."

"That work."

The cleaner stared at her for a long moment. Then she returned to scraping. She did not say anything else about it. Her silence was louder than the fermentation argument.

Hart stayed. She stood in the corridor and watched the cleaner work and listened to the water channels carry their sound through the stone, and the cleaner told her about the family with the sick juvenile, and the engineer who tracked moss, and the displacement trainee who cried, and a maintenance dispute from six years ago that the cleaner considered more important than any Speaking Chamber session, because the Speaking Chamber did not have to live with the consequences of its own drainage policy.

It was the most grounded conversation Hart had had in weeks.

She moved on. The outer edge of the settlement was where the corridors narrowed to single-body width and the bioluminescence dimmed to a faint pulse. The walls here were warm and rough and still growing, root structures pushing outward into the surrounding earth. Hart touched the surface. Warm through her palm. The settlement did not end. It tapered, the architecture softening from civic infrastructure into something more like the edge of a forest, where cultivation faded and wildness resumed.

The water channels were thinner here, carrying fragments of voices from deeper in the settlement, conversations stripped of their words and reduced to rhythm. Hart stood in the oldest, quietest part of a civilization that predated flowering plants and she did not think about what any of it meant because she was not interested in what things meant. She was interested in whether they worked, and whether they leaked, and what grew in the cracks.

The walls were warm. The settlement breathed around her in its sleep.

She found Vesh by accident.

The meditation pool was small, set into an alcove off a corridor Hart had not walked before. The water was still. The bioluminescence was reduced to a faint blue-green glow at the pool's edges, barely enough to distinguish the water from the stone. Hart saw the pool first and the frog second.

Vesh sat at the pool's edge. He was not meditating. He was not performing anything. He was sitting with the stillness of a creature that had run out of motion, his small body hunched forward, his forelimbs resting on the wet stone. Five centimeters of frog in dim light. His colors were wrong. The vivid emerald was gone, the gold flecks dark. His skin had settled to a flat grey-green that made him look like any frog on any stone in any forest, unremarkable, small, old.

He looked old. Hart had not thought of him as old before, because the performance was so large it filled every room he entered, the grandiosity and the vulgar comedy and the gold-flecked brightness swallowing his actual size. Without it he was a small frog in the dark, his skin dull, his posture compressed, his one slightly larger eye catching the faint light and holding it with an expression that was not serene, not philosophical, not cosmic. Tired. Just tired. And beneath the tiredness, something Hart recognized because she had spent her career studying organisms under stress.

He was afraid.

She did not approach. She did not move. She stood in the corridor and looked at the mystic without the mysticism, a creature who would be dead in a few years, who had spent his life preaching that death was a scheduling formality and dissolution was beautiful, and who sat alone in the dark at whatever hour this was with his colors stripped to the grey-green of a frog who was not ready.

Then he noticed her.

The change happened in stages. First the posture. His spine straightened, his forelimbs repositioning on the stone, the hunch dissolving into something more composed. Then the colors. The grey-green warmed. Green deepened. The gold flecks surfaced, slowly at first, then with increasing confidence, the emerald reasserting, the stained-glass brightness returning to his skin like theater lights coming up. He caught an insect from the air beside his head, a motion so quick and practiced it looked casual, and swallowed it without breaking the emerging performance. Then the voice.

"Dr. Hart." The serene rumble was back, rolling through the corridor with the full weight of a frog who had apparently been sitting here contemplating the cosmos rather than whatever Hart had actually seen. "Insomnia is the self refusing to practice dissolution. I recommend the pools. Or the beetle."

Hart looked at him. The performance was complete. The grey-green frog from thirty seconds ago was gone, replaced by Brother Vesh, Ritual Guide, gold-flecked and certain.

"The pools are contaminated and the beetle is dead," she said.

"Two excellent reasons to try both."

She could have walked away. She almost did. But something in the exchange had a weight to it that was not the usual Hart-Vesh register. They were not natural conversation partners. She spoke in systems failure and he spoke in cosmic dissolution and the two languages shared almost no vocabulary. Almost.

"The Pruning," she said. "Can they do it?"

Vesh regarded her. The gold flecks held steady but his larger eye, the one that gave him his permanent expression of lopsided wonder, was still and focused in a way the performance did not quite cover.

"The operators are training. The Siberians have brought their arrays. Krrik is doing what Krrik does, which is to make the impossible merely very difficult. The question is not a simple one."

"Simple questions are the only kind worth asking."

"Then yes. They can do it. They have done it before. They will do it again."

Hart leaned against the corridor wall. "The question is not whether it succeeds but what it costs."

Vesh was quiet for a moment. The pool reflected his colors on its still surface, the emerald and the gold, and the reflection was steady and bright and not quite the same frog she had seen sitting alone in the dark.

"You sound like Talla," he said.

Hart could not tell if this was a compliment. She suspected Vesh could not either.

"I sound like someone who has watched systems survive at the expense of the people inside them. Your operators come home with their minds half-dissolved. Your dissenters get logged by a clerk in an alcove. And the cleaner in Corridor Nine has been scraping mineral deposits for twelve years and knows more about who lives and who suffers in this settlement than Noll will ever hear in the Speaking Chambers."

Vesh ate another insect. He did it slowly, with the deliberate relish of a frog reasserting the physical after too much of the abstract.

"The cleaner in Corridor Nine," he said, "is named Revvi. She has cleaned that section since before I became a ritual guide. She once told me that my breathing circles were too long and that I talked too much about the cosmos and not enough about drainage. I found this observation spiritually clarifying."

Hart almost smiled. She held it. "Good night, Vesh."

"Good night, Dr. Hart. The dawn here is worth seeing, if you can tolerate beauty on an empty stomach."

She walked back through corridors that were beginning to shift. The bioluminescence was changing, the blue-green warming toward amber at the edges. The water channels quickened, carrying the first vibrations of the settlement stirring. Bodies entering the water. The chemical and chromatic exchanges that constituted morning for a species that started each day by absorbing the world through their skin.

The first croaking rose from the warrens. Low, scattered, individual voices joining and overlapping until the sound became collective, a ragged chorus that was not coordinated and not random and not beautiful in any way Hart could defend with data.

She reached the shared quarters. Marsh had turned over. Reed's blanket was on the floor. Vale breathed with his arms still crossed. Quill's corner was still empty.

Hart sat on her moss pad. The bioluminescence was shifting through its dawn sequence, and the settlement was waking around her, and somewhere four frogs were still arguing about beetle-larva temperature because some arguments outlived the frogs who started them.

She did not sleep.

She did not say what she thought.

---

# Chapter 18: Voluntary Participation

The Speaking Chambers were full in a way Vale had not seen before.

Not the curated fullness of the Siberian meeting, where delegations had arranged themselves in deliberate formations. This was different. Frogs packed the tiered pools from the lowest mineral basin to the highest root-carved ledge, bodies pressed close, the water between them dark with the chemical signals of a hundred and forty animals broadcasting anxiety through their skin. The smell hit Vale first. Sweet and metallic, like copper pennies dissolved in warm honey, the concentrated output of a species that communicated partly through what it secreted. His eyes watered. Beside him on the upper ledge, Hart counted heads and stopped at a hundred and twelve because the lower tiers were too densely packed to distinguish individual bodies.

"More than the Siberian session," she said. "Significantly more."

Marsh, on Hart's far side, was watching the audience rather than the stage. Her pencil moved in her field notebook. She was reading the room's colors, and the room was afraid. The collective skin tone of the packed chamber had shifted toward a pale, washed green that Vale remembered from the Siberian meeting: the color of shared fear, the involuntary broadcast of a species whose bodies could not keep secrets from each other. Individual frogs flickered with personal colors, ambers and darker greens, but the baseline had moved. The water absorbed the mood, carrying vibrations through the stone that Vale registered as a low, persistent hum in his sternum.

Noll sat at the chamber's center, half-submerged in the primary pool, his muted green-grey holding steady. His damaged forelimb was tucked inward. His skin did not flicker. It never flickered. That control was itself a signal: the leader who remained calm so that others could see calm and be calmed by it. Vale had spent twenty years studying the technique. He had seen it in prime ministers and tribal elders and NGO directors who held crumbling organizations together by projecting composure they did not feel. He had never seen it done through skin color before, but the mechanism was identical.

The meeting began with procedure. Noll's diplomatic register, which Vale had learned to parse like a second language, delivered the familiar framework: coordination updates, Pruning preparation timelines, resource allocation for displacement staging. The frogs in the pools produced low vibrations of acknowledgment, a physical murmur that traveled through the water and up through the stone. Vale watched the language. He was always watching the language. Language was how power moved when it did not want to be seen.

The shift came eleven minutes in.

"The coordination office has consulted with technical and spiritual advisors regarding the preparation schedule," Noll said. His blue was perfect. Controlled. Serene. "Given the severity of the current cycle, enhanced community participation during the coordination period has been recommended. The breathing circles will expand to daily gatherings, with morning and evening sessions available at four locations throughout the settlement."

Enhanced community participation. Vale turned the phrase over. He had heard its equivalent in a dozen political systems on three continents. It meant mandatory. It always meant mandatory. The sentence structure was the giveaway: passive voice, attributed to unnamed advisors, framed as a recommendation that happened to carry the full weight of the coordination office behind it. Nobody had ordered anything. A recommendation had simply emerged, the way Noll's outcomes always simply emerged, and the distinction between recommendation and order was one that only mattered to people who had the option of refusing.

The chamber's vibration shifted. A pulse of darker green ran through the lower tiers. Some frogs adjusted their positions in the water, a collective physical restlessness that was the closest thing to murmuring Vale had seen in frog assemblies. But nobody spoke against it. The vibration settled. The recommendation was absorbed.

Marsh's pencil stopped. She looked at Vale across Hart's shoulder. He looked back. They did not need to say it.

Talla spoke from the middle tiers.

She was standing on a stone ledge above the waterline, which in itself was a statement. The frogs around her had given her a small clearing, the practiced adjustment of neighbors who knew what was coming. Her dark skin was nearly black under the bioluminescence, and her pale spots were visible across her back, which meant she was not controlling them, which meant she was too angry to care.

"The Siberians coordinate displacement operations without mandatory breathing circles," she said. Her voice was flat, directed at the chamber, at nobody specifically and everybody collectively. "They have done this for sixty million years. Their accuracy is documented. The operational need does not require this expansion. The coordination office is using the crisis to normalize increased participation in the merging."

The chamber did not erupt. The chamber absorbed. Vale watched it happen with the focused attention of a man watching a system he had studied his entire career operate in a medium he had never imagined. A frog two tiers below Talla shifted to a concerned amber that was precisely calibrated to signal moderate disagreement without appearing hostile. Three frogs near Noll's pool produced a synchronized color that read as measured thoughtfulness. The room was managing Talla, and the management was happening through skin.

Then the unnamed coordinator spoke.

She was positioned at the second tier, an older frog with muted olive coloring and the unhurried posture of someone who had attended several thousand of these sessions and intended to attend several thousand more. Her voice carried the particular gentleness of institutional authority at its most practiced.

"Talla-of-the-Third-Pool, the coordination record values your consistent engagement with procedural review." A pause. "Have you considered that your objections might be better raised after the emergency has passed? The current period requires focus on preparation, and the coordination office welcomes input during the standard review cycle."

The chamber murmured agreement. Not a word. A vibration, low and collective, traveling through the water and the stone and into Vale's chest. A hundred and forty frogs expressing the institutional consensus through their bodies: the reasonable position was to defer. The reasonable position was always to defer. The emergency was real and the objection was noted and the review cycle was available and the breathing circles were voluntary and the recommendation was a recommendation and the distinction between all of these carefully separated categories was so thin that only someone determined to cause difficulty would insist on examining it.

Vale looked at Hart. She was watching the coordinator with the narrow, still attention she gave to organisms she was classifying. She had seen it too. They both knew the technique. Schedule dissent for later. Later never arrives. The emergency is always present, or the review cycle is always next quarter, or the recommendation has already been implemented, and the person who raised the objection has been heard, valued, and administratively dissolved.

Talla's spots flared bright against her dark skin. She did not reply. She stepped down from the ledge and back into the water, and the clearing around her closed, and the session moved on.

The breathing circle was smaller. Thirty frogs in a pool that was warmer than the Speaking Chambers, circular, with low walls of living root. The bioluminescence here was dimmer, concentrated at the pool's edges in soft blue-green strips that cast the water in shadow and the frogs' bodies in half-light. The air was thick. Not with humidity, which was constant in the settlement, but with something else. The chemical output of thirty frogs breathing in proximity, their skin releasing whatever compounds synchronized breathing produced, a sweet metallic heaviness that coated the back of Vale's throat and made his sinuses tighten.

Noll had invited him. The word "invited" doing the work it always did in Noll's vocabulary, which was to describe something that was not quite a command and not quite optional and not quite either.

Vale entered the water.

It was warm. Body temperature or slightly above, mineral-rich, with a faint slickness that he assumed was biological. The pool's floor was smooth stone, and he sat on it with the water at his chest, his back against the curved wall, his clothes soaking through. Around him, thirty frogs were arranged in a loose formation, some floating, some perched on submerged ledges, some half-submerged with their heads above the surface. Their eyes were closed. Their bodies rose and fell with a synchronized rhythm, a collective breathing that produced a faint, audible pulse in the enclosed space.

Quill was there.

He was across the pool, eyes closed, his pale face tilted slightly upward, his hands resting on the surface of the water. He was breathing with them. Not observing. Not analyzing. Breathing. His chest rose and fell in the same rhythm as the frogs around him, and his expression was not the searching, troubled look Vale associated with Quill's philosophical processing. It was still. Settled. A man who had stopped asking questions about what he was doing and was simply doing it.

Vale saw him and did not say anything and did not think a thesis statement about it. He just saw him. The fact sat in his chest alongside the hum from the Speaking Chambers and the metallic sweetness of the air.

He closed his eyes because the frogs around him had closed theirs and because keeping them open felt like the wrong kind of resistance.

The breathing was easy to follow. A slow rhythm, in and out, the collective pulse of the pool setting a tempo that his lungs matched without instruction. The water was warm. His body softened in it. His jaw unclenched. His shoulders, which he carried like scaffolding around his neck, dropped a centimeter. The chemical air entered him through his nose and coated his throat and he breathed it out and the pool's small waves carried it back and the cycle continued. It was pleasant. He had not expected pleasant. He had expected theater.

Then the edges of himself thinned.

It lasted seconds. Less than ten. But in those seconds the pool was not thirty-one separate bodies in warm water. The frog to his left was a warmth that extended into a pressure that became an awareness. The frog three positions away was a chemical signature that Vale's skin read before his mind could process it, a presence that was not a thought but a sensation, like standing in sunlight and knowing the sun was there without looking. The water carried something between them. Not language. Not telepathy. Something older and simpler and less organized. A blurring. The point where Thomas Vale ended and the warm water began became briefly unclear, and the warm water contained other things that were also unclear at their edges, and for a span of time he could not accurately measure, the room was not a room full of individuals but a single warm medium through which separate intentions moved like currents.

His eyes opened. The room was a room again. Thirty frogs and one biologist in a warm pool. His hands were shaking in the water, a fine tremor visible in the disturbed surface. His heart was fast. His skin felt raw, oversensitized, registering the water's temperature and chemistry with an attention that was not normal.

He stood up.

Around the pool, frogs were surfacing at different speeds. Some stayed in the water, their colors slow and calm. Others climbed the edges with the efficient movements of people finishing a routine. A few lingered at the boundary between water and stone, their eyes half-focused, the look of someone returning from somewhere they had not entirely left.

Quill remained in the water. His eyes were still closed. His face was still.

Vale walked out of the pool and through the low root-arch that served as an exit. His clothes dripped on the stone. The corridor air was cooler and his skin contracted with the change and he stood there, wet and trembling slightly, and tried to reconstruct what had just happened using the vocabulary of a man who had spent his career studying power, hierarchy, and the structures through which societies controlled their members.

Noll was waiting outside.

He stood in the corridor with the unhurried patience of a frog who had known Vale would come out first and would come out shaking. His blue was steady. His damaged forelimb was tucked inward. He regarded Vale with an expression that was warm, which was worse than if it had been triumphant, because warmth suggested Noll genuinely believed Vale had just experienced something good.

"You felt it," Noll said.

Vale looked at the small, steady frog. He said nothing. His hands were still trembling. The water dripped from his shirt onto the stone.

Noll held his gaze for a moment, and the warmth in his expression did not waver, and then he walked past Vale toward the pool with the measured gait of a coordinator attending to the next item on an agenda that had been running smoothly since before Vale's species learned to stand upright.

Hart went to the Third Pool district.

She had not told Vale or Marsh. Talla's meetings were not announced, not scheduled, not official. You heard about them the way you heard about everything in the settlement: someone mentioned it while passing through a corridor, and you either went or you did not.

The Third Pool district was smaller than the central settlement, less maintained, the bioluminescence patchier, casting uneven shadows across root walls that had not been cleaned in weeks. Revvi's territory this was not. The meeting space was an open alcove beside a shallow warm pool, rough-walled, lit by a single fungal streak.

Forty frogs. Maybe forty-five. Hart stood at the back, the only human, her arms folded, her field kit at her feet. Nobody asked her to leave.

Talla spoke from the pool's edge. She was not performing outrage. She was listing. Food distribution unchanged for relocated families. Maintenance crews cut from six to four. Breathing circle attendance now tracked and reported. Breathing circle frequency increased. She listed these things the way Hart would have listed contaminants in a water sample: clinically, precisely, letting the numbers do the work.

The frogs listened. Their colors were muted, damped, careful. These were not revolutionaries. These were frightened people who wanted someone to say, in a room where saying it was permitted, that the system they depended on was also the system that was squeezing them.

Hart recognized two faces. Both had been in the Speaking Chambers that morning, in the upper tiers, their colors bright with public support for Noll's announcement. Here, their colors were flat. Controlled. They stood near the back, apart from each other, attending quietly.

Hart filed this. It was the most important political intelligence she had gathered since arriving.

She left before the meeting ended. The corridors back to the central settlement were dim and damp and smelled of the mineral-water-and-growth smell she had stopped noticing and now noticed again.

The biologists' quarters were dim when she arrived. Vale was already there, sitting on the ledge, still damp from the breathing circle. Marsh was at the stone table, her notebook out, writing rapidly. They both looked up.

"I need to say something and I need you both to hear it before we discuss it." Vale sat on the ledge. He was still damp. He did not care. "Every dictator in human history dreamed of this. A mechanism that makes obedience feel like enlightenment."

Hart stopped sorting. Marsh's pencil lifted from the notebook.

"It works," Vale said. "It is not suggestion. It is not group psychology. It is not the warm water and the dim lights creating a mood. Something in that pool gets inside your biochemistry and loosens the place where you end and the next person begins. For about five seconds I was not entirely myself. I did not consent to that. My body did not ask permission. And when I came out, the administrator was waiting to tell me, with complete sincerity, that the experience was a gift."

Marsh set her pencil down. "It also saves the planet."

"That," Vale said, "is exactly the problem."

The room held the silence. Through the stone walls, the water channels carried the low vibration of the settlement's breathing, the constant sound that was either infrastructure or prayer and which Vale was increasingly certain was both.

Hart spoke. "I went to Talla's meeting. Third Pool district. Forty-five frogs, maybe more. Scared, not radical." She looked at Vale. "Two of Noll's public supporters from this morning were there. At the back. Colors damped. Listening."

Vale processed this. "The gap between the official consensus and the actual mood."

"Wider than Noll thinks. Or exactly as wide as he thinks and he considers it manageable." Hart pulled something from her field kit and put it back. "I do not know which is worse."

The root-arch that served as a doorway darkened, and Reed came through it with the slightly manic energy of a man who had been running calculations and needed to tell someone. His glasses were in his hair. His hands were full of a dried moss sheet covered in markings.

"Krrik's modulation compounds are not holding," he said. "The eastern growth sectors blew through the latest application in three days. Three days. The projection was ten. He has reformulated twice and the voss adapts faster than the formula can change." He looked around the room, reading the mood, not reading it well. "What happened?"

"Politics," Hart said.

"The breathing circles are mandatory now," Vale said. "In everything but name."

Reed processed this. He pushed his glasses down from his hair, found they were already halfway to his nose, pushed them the rest of the way. "Right. That is. That is a separate problem. My problem is that the growth rate in the eastern sectors has exceeded the Ch. sixteen projections by another eleven percent, and if the modulation keeps failing at this rate, Krrik is going to need the synchronized operators working at full capacity within days, not weeks, and the operators are already exhausted from the drills, and the whole thing feeds into the same." He stopped. Looked at Vale. "You went to a breathing circle."

"I did."

"And?"

Vale looked at Reed. At Hart. At Marsh. Through the stone, the settlement hummed its constant, ambiguous hum.

"The mechanism works," he said. "That is the only thing I am prepared to say about it."

Reed stood in the doorway with his moss sheet and his glasses and his urgent data, and the room held the two problems side by side: the political problem and the operational problem, the mechanism that dissolved the self and the mechanism that saved the planet, and the fact that they were the same mechanism, and nobody in the room could resolve the contradiction because the contradiction was the point.

Marsh picked up her pencil. Hart closed her field kit. Reed unrolled his moss sheet on the stone table and began explaining the growth data, his voice falling into the rapid, clause-heavy rhythm that meant the numbers were telling him something and he needed help hearing what it was.

Vale sat on the ledge and listened. His clothes were still damp. His hands had stopped trembling. The hum from the pools traveled through the walls and through the stone and through his wet shirt and into his chest, where it sat beside the memory of five seconds when the edges of Thomas Vale had thinned and something else had been there in the water with him, and the worst part, the part he would not say to anyone in this room or any room, was that it had not been unpleasant.

---

# Chapter 19: The Growth

The lab had acquired a population.

Reed stood in the corridor entrance and counted. Krrik. Two junior engineers. Three frogs he did not recognize from Krrik's regular team, who were crouched over a secondary moss bed that had not been active the last time Reed visited. The Siberian engineer from the coordination meeting, compact and grey, occupying a stone ledge near the largest computation array rigid and still, clearly considering the room's chaos a character flaw. And, at the back, near the water channel, a pair of frogs who appeared to be arguing about whether the nutrient drip on the secondary array needed adjusting or whether adjusting it would compromise the recalibration Krrik had started an hour ago.

The moss beds were glowing brighter than Reed had ever seen them. Not the gentle bioluminescence he had come to associate with their resting state. This was active, strained, the pale green shifted toward something almost white at the edges. The computation arrays were overworked, running simultaneous models across every bed in the room, and the beds were responding the way any living system responded to sustained overload: they were getting hot and complaining about it in light.

The smell was worse than usual. Ozone and fermentation and the sweet metallic scent that frogs produced when stressed, concentrated by too many bodies in an enclosed space. Reed's eyes watered. He wiped his glasses on his shirt, pushed them back up, and stepped into the room.

Krrik was at the largest bed. His forelimb rested flat against the moss surface. The display beneath his touch was dense, branching networks of light pulsing through the green in patterns Reed had learned to read over the past weeks. Voss growth models. But the model he was seeing now bore almost no resemblance to the one Krrik had shown him in the early days. That model had been organized, bounded. An expanding network with clear edges. This one had no edges. The light pushed to the borders of the moss bed and continued, the display folding back on itself to show layers of growth overlapping in three dimensions, and the bright center, which had been the primary substrate mass beneath the settlement, had expanded until it occupied a third of the entire projection.

"Krrik."

"I see you." Krrik did not look up. His mottled brown-green skin was cycling through its unconscious processing patterns faster than Reed had ever seen, a churn of color that made him look like a stone being rotated under shifting light. "Shut the door."

"There is no door."

"Then stand in it and pretend."

One of the junior engineers, a small dark-green frog with anxious yellow at the edges of her coloring, looked up from the secondary bed. "The modulation compounds are degrading faster than we can reformulate them. The eastern sectors absorbed the latest application in, in." She looked at the bed for confirmation. "Forty-one hours."

"Forty-one," Reed said. He did the arithmetic in his head against the timeline he had been tracking. The previous application had lasted three days. Before that, ten. Before that, the projections had called for ten, and reality had shredded them. The compounds were not just failing. They were losing ground in a curve that bent the wrong way. "That is almost half the duration of the last round."

"The voss adapts," Krrik said. His voice was flat. The same operational flatness Reed had heard when the models first began failing weeks ago, but deeper now, stripped further. "Each application selects for resistant growth. The resistant growth propagates. The next application is less effective. The next is less effective than that. This is not complicated biology. This is evolutionary pressure on an accelerated timescale."

"Can you reformulate faster than it adapts?"

"I have been reformulating faster than it adapts. The gap is closing."

The Siberian engineer spoke from her ledge. She had not moved since Reed entered. Her flat grey skin absorbed the moss beds' glow without reflecting any of it, which made her look like a gap cut into the light.

"The gap closed in the northern quadrant two days ago." Her voice was clipped, toneless, stripped of the diplomatic layering that the Amazon frogs applied to every sentence. "Our modulation team exhausted their reformulation options for the deep-root sectors. We have shifted to physical suppression. Cutting. Burning. Your methods from the early cycles."

Reed looked at her. "You're cutting voss. Physically."

"We are cutting voss. Physically. With tools. The irony is not lost."

"Your northern growth was seventeen percent above projections a week ago," Reed said. "Where is it now?"

"Thirty-one."

Krrik's colors stopped cycling. For a moment his entire body went a flat, solid brown that Reed had never seen on him. Then the churn resumed.

"Amazon eastern sectors?" Reed asked.

"Thirty-eight percent above projections as of this morning's reading," the junior engineer said. She said it the way someone reports test results they have checked three times and hope are wrong. "Global growth rate across all monitored sectors is approaching ninety percent above the resting boundary."

Reed's glasses needed pushing up. He pushed them. Ninety percent. Weeks ago, when Krrik had first shown him the growth models, the number had been sixty-seven, and that had made the room go quiet. Ninety was not a room-going-quiet number. Ninety was a number that changed the nature of the questions you were asking.

"Show me the compound degradation curve alongside the growth curve," Reed said.

"You cannot read the arrays."

"I can read a curve. I have been reading curves for twelve years. Show me."

Krrik looked at him. Both eyes, which was rare. Then he touched the moss bed and the display shifted, and two branching patterns of light overlaid each other in the green depths of the living computation medium. One was the growth model, the pulsing expansion Reed had been watching. The other was something new: a fading trail of pale yellow that retreated as the growth advanced. The modulation compounds. Losing ground. The two curves were not parallel. They were converging. And the point where they met, where the yellow trail vanished into the advancing white, was not far.

"Weeks," Reed said.

"Less."

"How much less?"

Krrik removed his forelimb from the moss. "Come with me."

They moved to the back of the lab, past the arguing nutrient-drip frogs, past a table covered in stone dishes containing chemical preparations in various states of development. Krrik stopped at a basin Reed had not seen before. Small, shallow, filled with a thick amber liquid that caught the moss light and held it. Around the basin, seven sealed stone containers sat in a row. Each one was labeled in markings Reed could not read, but the progression was visible. The first container was nearly empty. The seventh was full.

"These are the reformulated compounds," Krrik said. He pointed to the first. "This was the formula active during your first visit to the lab. It held the growth for twelve days." He pointed to the fourth. "This is the formula that failed three days ago. It held for three days." He pointed to the seventh. "This is the latest. Applied this morning. I do not yet know how long it will hold." He looked at the basin. "I am running out of chemical space. The voss is alive. It learns. Not the way you learn. The way an immune system learns. Each compound teaches it what to resist."

Reed stood over the basin. The amber liquid looked warm. It looked like something that should work, that had worked, that was being outpaced by a substrate two hundred million years old that had been adapting to chemical intervention for longer than flowering plants had existed.

"You need my surface data," Reed said.

"I need your surface data."

The junior engineer had followed them. She stood at a respectful distance, her colors grey and uncertain. The Siberian engineer had not followed. She sat on her ledge, studying her own data on a small computation pad she had brought from the northern settlement, her grey skin unchanged, her posture a monument to the Siberian conviction that emotion was a waste of processing capacity.

Krrik spoke without ceremony. "The modulators are insufficient. I am modifying them. I need your surface data. You see from above. We see from within. Neither view is complete."

"What specifically?"

"Growth density at the surface boundaries. Your canopy stress readings correlated with root expansion. Your soil temperature gradients. I can model from inside the substrate but the surface behavior is a variable I cannot observe from down here, and the monitoring stations have not been calibrated for this kind of emergence because surface emergence was not supposed to happen." A flash of orange across his skin. "I am asking for help. Note this. I do not intend to do it again."

Reed was already pulling the dried moss sheets from the satchel he carried everywhere. Six months of surface data, transcribed by hand because human electronics did not survive the settlement's humidity. Crude instruments, primitive methodology, pattern recognition and patience. The broken window that tells you about the wind.

"I need the latest canopy readings correlated against the eastern sector growth data," Reed said. "If the surface emergence is following the root corridors, there should be a stress gradient in the canopy that maps to the underground expansion. I can show you what that looks like from above."

Krrik took the moss sheets without acknowledgment. He carried them to the largest computation array and began feeding the data in, pressing patterns into the moss surface with his forelimbs. The display shifted. New light threaded through the existing models, pale and different, the surface perspective overlaying the underground topology.

The Siberian engineer finally moved. She hopped down from her ledge and crossed the lab to the array, which was the most physical urgency Reed had seen from any Siberian. She studied the combined display for twelve seconds.

"The surface gradient confirms the subsurface expansion rate," she said. "Your underground model is conservative. The actual growth front is further advanced than your projections show."

"I know," Krrik said.

"Then your projections are useless."

"My projections are being revised. That is why I am standing here instead of attending the coordination meeting I was summoned to. Would you like to be helpful or would you like to continue identifying the problem?"

"I would like you to admit that the modulation approach has a failure horizon, and that failure horizon is closer than you have communicated to the coordination office."

Krrik did not respond to this. His silence, which was the loudest sound in the room, lasted four seconds. The junior engineer's skin went three shades paler.

"I have communicated what the data supports," Krrik said. "The data changes hourly. I will update the office when I have something stable enough to update them with."

The Siberian engineer returned to her ledge. The verdict she did not deliver hung in the air alongside the ozone and the stress-sweat: Krrik was not sharing how bad it was. Whether this was scientific caution or political calculation, she did not say. Her grey skin said nothing at all.

Reed left the lab twenty minutes later. He carried three moss sheets covered in Krrik's notations, data requests for surface readings he would need to compile from his field records. His head was full of growth curves and compound degradation rates and a number, ninety percent, that sat in his thoughts like a stone in a shoe.

The residential corridor he walked through on his way back to quarters told the rest of the story.

The eastern section of the warrens was flooded.

Not with water. With voss. The living substrate had pushed through the root wall of a residential corridor, a slow intrusion that had probably been happening for days, but what had been a faint warmth in the stone and a slight luminescence at the seams was now a rupture. The wall had split where a root joint met the carved stone, and through the gap, pale luminous growth spilled into the corridor. Warm, faintly pulsing, it spread across the floor in tendrils that followed the water channels with the patience of something that had been growing for two hundred million years and was not in a hurry but was also not stopping.

A frog family stood in the corridor outside their chambers. Two adults and three juveniles, the juveniles small enough to fit in Reed's palm. Their possessions were in woven baskets beside them: sleeping moss, food containers, a small stone dish that might have been decorative or functional or both. The female's throat patch cycled between amber and grey, a rapid oscillation of worry. The male's colors were flat, controlled, the deliberate stillness of a parent performing calm for children who were not fooled.

A neighbor had emerged from the adjacent chamber and was speaking rapidly, gesturing toward her own space. An offer of room. The family's eldest juvenile, barely large enough to have completed metamorphosis, was staring at the voss growth pushing through the wall. The glow reflected in his eyes.

Two maintenance frogs worked at the rupture. One was applying a thick paste to the edges of the breach, a chemical retardant that Reed recognized from Krrik's lab. The other was physically cutting back the tendrils where they had spread across the floor, using a curved stone tool with a motion that was precise and futile, because behind each cut tendril, the growth continued, slower now where the paste held, but continuing.

Oona arrived from the far end of the corridor with four other frogs, each carrying supplies. Cleaning materials. Food containers. Blankets woven from dried moss. She moved through the small crowd with the unhurried competence of someone who had organized things before and did not need a title to do it. Her orange throat patch was dim, which was unusual. Oona's throat patch was her broadcasting system, brightening with gossip, dimming with boredom. Dim in a crisis meant the crisis had moved past the point where gossip could process it.

"This never used to happen until the third week," Oona said. She was not addressing anyone specifically. She was addressing the corridor, the situation, the voss itself. "The third week. We are in the second week. Someone should tell the voss it is early."

A frog behind her, older, carrying a stack of woven mats, made a sound that was either a laugh or the vocal equivalent of shaking her head. "The Croaking is loud today," the older frog said, which was frog shorthand for everything feels connected, everything feels alive, and in this context carried the additional weight of everything feels connected and the connected thing is eating our homes.

Reed helped. He did not plan to help. He was carrying moss sheets covered in growth data and heading back to quarters to compile surface readings, and then the juvenile frog dropped one of the family's food containers and it rolled into the voss tendrils on the floor, and Reed bent down and picked it up. The container was warm. The tendril it had touched was warm. Everything was warm, the walls and the floor and the air, the settlement running a low fever it could not break.

Marsh appeared at the far end of the corridor with Oona's supplies team, carrying a stone pot that steamed faintly. Food for the displaced. She saw Reed and raised her eyebrows, a question and an acknowledgment, and continued past him toward the family. Behind her, a frog Reed did not know was explaining to the maintenance crew that her sleeping chamber, three doors down, also had warmth coming through the wall, and was that normal, and the maintenance frogs exchanged a look that was not normal, not at all, but what they said was: "We will assess it after the current repair."

The female from the displaced family accepted food from Marsh. She held the container in both forelimbs and looked at the growth pushing through her wall and said, to Oona: "Where do we go?"

"West," Oona said. "The western warrens have space. The families from section four moved there two days ago. It is crowded but the walls are holding."

"The walls are holding," the female repeated. The repetition was not reassurance. It was a question wearing the grammatical clothes of a statement.

"The walls are holding," Oona said again, and her throat patch did not brighten and her voice did not lift and she looked, for a moment, like a frog who was managing a situation she did not believe was manageable.

Reed left the residential section carrying the image of the juvenile staring at the glowing wall and the sound of "the walls are holding" spoken twice without conviction.

The staging grounds were louder.

The open area near the settlement's western perimeter, which during the vibration games had been a place of competition and fermented-fly contests, was now an operational space. Displacement operators sat in the coordination pools, twenty of them in two concentric arcs, their bodies half-submerged, running synchronization drills. Their colors moved in unison, patterns clicking through the green-gold-violet sequence that meant they were thinking together, aligning their cognitive outputs for the displacement operations that would push the voss back when the time came.

Three operators had been pulled from the pool and were sitting on the stone edge, attended by medical frogs. One of them was the young operator Reed remembered from Reed's nocturnal walk through the corridors. No. That was Hart's walk. Hart had told him about the operator who cried three nights out of five. This frog, small and green her coloring washed out, given more than she had, sat on the stone with her forelimbs resting on her knees and her eyes unfocused, looking at something that was not in the room.

Hart was there. She stood near the medical frogs with a water sample in one hand and a questioning expression that Reed recognized. The expression meant she had found something in the data and needed someone to argue with her about it.

"Three operators pulled from the last session," Hart said when she saw him. "Three. Carried out. One of them lost color coherence for six minutes, which their medical team is calling 'transient dissonance,' which is a polite way of saying her brain forgot how to run her body." She held up the water sample. "The pool chemistry after a synchronization session has twice the chemical load of a normal pool. Their skin is absorbing whatever the group produces during merging, and it accumulates. They are not draining between sessions."

"The schedule does not allow draining between sessions," Reed said. "Noll expanded to four sessions per day."

"At what point does preparation become damage?"

Reed looked at the operators in the pool. Twenty frogs, synchronized, their colors moving together, the precision beautiful if you forgot what it cost. He looked at the three on the edge. At the young operator who had not regained focus. At the medical frogs checking chemical levels in her skin with instruments that looked like twigs dipped in moss.

"At the point where the alternative is the voss eating the settlement."

"That is what they said about the last three sessions too."

"Because it is true about the last three sessions too. Hart, the eastern residential section has voss growing through the walls. I just walked through it. Families displaced. Maintenance cutting it back with hand tools. Oona organizing refugees. The growth is at ninety percent above the resting boundary and the modulation compounds are failing in under two days."

Hart set down the water sample. She looked at him. The narrow, still look she reserved for information that confirmed her worst assessment.

"Ninety."

"Ninety. The Siberians' northern quadrant is at thirty-one. The Amazon eastern sectors, thirty-eight. The compounds Krrik is brewing to slow it down are losing effectiveness faster than he can reformulate them. The voss adapts. Each application teaches it what to resist."

Hart looked at the operators. At the medical frogs. At the young frog on the stone edge whose eyes still were not tracking.

"Then the operators are the only thing standing between the settlement and an expanding planetary substrate that has been alive longer than most of the life on this planet," she said. "And the operators are being ground down by daily synchronization drills that their bodies are not recovering from."

"Yes."

"Then we are in trouble."

"We have been in trouble. The trouble got worse while we were arguing about breathing circles."

Hart picked up her water sample. She did not argue further. She did not need to. They stood at the edge of the staging grounds, two human biologists in a frog civilization preparing for a planetary operation that had been conducted roughly every generation for two hundred million years, and watched the operators drill, their colors moving in patterns of green and gold and violet, the beautiful synchronized motion of a species that had learned to think together because thinking alone was not enough to survive what came for them every thirty years.

Reed went back to the lab.

The population had thinned. The arguing nutrient-drip frogs had gone. Two of the unfamiliar engineers had left. The Siberian engineer remained on her ledge, still studying her computation pad with the focused patience of someone for whom data was a form of emotional regulation. Krrik was at the largest moss bed. The junior engineer stood beside him, holding a fresh stone container of modulation compound. The amber liquid caught the light.

The lab was quieter. The hum of the overworked computation arrays filled the space, the living sound of moss thinking harder than it was designed to, and beneath it the constant background murmur of the settlement's water channels, carrying vibrations from everywhere, the sound of twelve hundred frogs going about the business of existing in a civilization that might, for the first time in recorded memory, not be enough.

"Krrik," Reed said.

Krrik touched the moss bed. The display shifted. Reed's surface data, now integrated, showed the combined picture. The growth from below, the stress from above. The modulation compounds retreating. The operators' capacity, represented as a thin line of blue light that was supposed to be the last defense, the synchronized displacement that would push the voss back to its resting boundary.

The blue line was thin.

"We have done this before," Krrik said. He said it quietly. Not to Reed. Not to the Siberian engineer. To the room, maybe. To the moss bed. To himself. "We have always done this."

The junior engineer stood very still. Her stone container of fresh compound rested in her forelimbs. Her colors, which had been cycling through anxious greens and greys all day, went flat. All color drained from her skin in a single, silent wave, leaving her a pale, translucent grey that showed the faint shadow of her internal structures beneath the surface. An involuntary display of fear so total that her body abandoned every other signal to broadcast only this: I am afraid.

Nobody commented on it. Krrik did not look at her. The Siberian engineer did not look at her. Reed did not look at her. They all saw it. They all saw each other not looking. The lab held the silence for three seconds, and the moss beds pulsed their overworked light, and the water channels carried the sound of a settlement that did not know the numbers Reed had spent the day learning.

"But 'always' is a comfort," Krrik said. "Not a guarantee."

---

# Chapter 20: The Merging

The corridors narrowed as they descended.

Quill followed Vesh through a passage that had not been shaped for human proportions. The root walls pressed closer with each turn, the bioluminescence dimmer, blue-green fading to a deep aquatic blue that made the stone look wet even where it was dry. The air thickened. Warm, heavy, saturated with moisture and the copper-and-herb smell of concentrated frog chemistry. Marsh was behind him, her notebook in her back pocket, her pencil behind her ear. Reed was behind Marsh, his glasses already fogging in the humidity, wiping them on his shirt every few steps and putting them back on to fog again.

The vibration reached Quill before the sound did. A low pulse that entered through the soles of his boots and climbed his skeleton and settled in the hinge of his jaw. Something was already underway below them. It hummed in his teeth.

Vesh moved ahead without looking back. His small emerald body was vivid against the dim stone, gold flecks bright, his lopsided gaze fixed forward. He had not spoken since they left the main settlement level. The silence was unlike him. Vesh filled silences the way water filled containers, naturally and completely. That he was not filling this one said something about where they were going.

The last stretch of corridor was carved narrower than the rest, the root walls close enough that Quill's shoulders brushed both sides and he had to duck where a root arch crossed overhead. Marsh's breathing was steady behind him. Reed's was not. The vibration in the stone was stronger here, a sustained pulse that entered through the walls and the floor and the warm thick air, so that by the time the passage turned a final time and opened, Quill's body was already humming with something that had started before he arrived.

The ceremonial pool occupied a natural chamber deep beneath the settlement's lowest level. It was large, roughly oval, the water dark and still and warm enough that steam drifted across its surface in thin currents. Tiered stone ledges rose on three sides, carved or grown from the root walls, their surfaces worn smooth by generations of use. The bioluminescence here came from below, from fungal networks beneath the water's surface, so the light moved with the water's small currents and the frogs in the pool appeared to float in a field of slow-moving stars.

Forty frogs. Maybe fifty. Quill stopped counting because the bodies in the water were close together and some were partially submerged and their colors were already shifting in patterns that made it difficult to tell where one ended and the next began. He recognized faces. The brown-green female from the fermentation cellars who had argued with Oona about bark ratios three days ago. Two maintenance frogs from the eastern section, their hands still stained with the chemical retardant they used on the voss. A displacement operator Quill had seen at the staging grounds, young, her coloring the deep focused green of someone preparing for something she had trained for and was not certain she was ready for. Near the pool's far edge, half-submerged, a frog whose face Quill did not know but whose posture he recognized: the rigid, deliberate stillness of someone who had done this enough times that the preparation was physical memory, not thought.

On the stone ledges, the observers. Fifteen, maybe twenty, arranged on the tiers at different heights. Some sat close to the water, their feet dangling over the edge. Others had climbed to the higher ledges, keeping distance. Two frogs near the back were speaking softly to each other, their colors low. Medical frogs waited at the water's far edge, still, their instruments beside them on the stone. They were part of the arrangement. They were always part of the arrangement.

Vesh took his position at the pool's edge, a flat stone shelf that jutted over the water. He settled onto it with the careful posture of a frog who had done this many times. His gold flecks were fully expanded, bright against his emerald skin. He did not address the chamber. He looked at the water and the frogs in it, and his silence was the opening.

Quill climbed to the upper ledge with Marsh and Reed. The stone was warm beneath him. From here, the pool was a dark oval below, the floating bioluminescent light turning the water into something that looked like a clear night seen from above. The frogs in the pool were shapes among the moving stars.

An elderly female frog was already seated on the ledge beside where Marsh settled. Small, faded green, her coloring the translucent pallor of advanced age. She regarded the three humans with the mild interest of someone who had seen stranger things arrive at these ledges. She did not greet them. She shifted slightly to make room and returned her attention to the pool.
