Across the chamber, on the lower tiers, a young male frog sat rigid. His skin flickered between green and a nervous, washed-out yellow, the rapid cycling of someone who did not yet know how to control what his body broadcast. First time. Quill could tell because the frogs on either side of him were not flickering. They had seen this before. One of them, an older female, rested a forelimb on the young one's back. A steadying gesture. The young frog's flickering slowed but did not stop.

Several frogs on the near ledge turned to look at the humans. Three seats. Three humans. Their colors shifted, a murmur in skin: they had expected more. Hart's absence registered across the observers in small ripples of amber. A frog two tiers down turned to the one beside her and said something Quill did not catch. The reply was a color: flat, brief, dismissive. The frogs returned their attention to the pool.

"A group hallucination with operational consequences," Reed said quietly. "Her words."

Marsh said nothing. She had her notebook out but was not writing.

Below, Vesh spoke.

His voice was soft. It carried through the chamber's acoustics, through water and stone, arriving in Quill's chest as much as his ears. "The chord was here before us. The chord will be here after us. We are the temporary voices that join it."

The frogs in the pool began.

It started with breathing. Individual rhythms, unsynchronized, the ordinary rise and fall of forty separate bodies in warm water. Then the rhythms converged. Not all at once. A cluster near the pool's center aligned first, their chests rising and falling together, and the alignment spread outward in a slow wave, pulling in the frogs at the edges, until the entire pool breathed as one body. The fermentation-cellars frog submerged into the shared rhythm. Her individual breathing disappeared into the collective pulse. The maintenance frogs joined. The young operator joined. They were still there. They were also somewhere else.

The colors followed. Individual markings faded. The greens and browns and olives and the occasional flash of personal amber smoothed into a single shade that moved across all of them at once, a slow tide of color that rose and receded and rose again, each wave pulling the individual patterns further into the shared one. Quill watched the last holdouts: a frog near the pool's edge whose personal markings resisted for a few seconds longer, a dark mottling that flickered and flickered and then smoothed into the collective color like a stone sinking into water. Their skin became a single surface. The pool held one color that breathed.

Then the croaking began.

Individual voices at first. Low, sustained, each frog producing its own pitch, the sound rising from the water in a dense, overlapping texture that filled the chamber's lower registers. The voices found each other. Two frogs on the same pitch merged into one sound. Three more joined. Five. The pitches converged, not into unison but into a chord, a sound made of separate voices that were approaching the boundary of not being separate. It was not music exactly. It was lower than music and older than music and it was felt in the chest and teeth and the bones of the skull, a vibration that bypassed the ears and entered the body directly through the stone and the warm air and the water vapor that coated every surface. The water's surface trembled with it. The bioluminescent light below shivered and the slow-moving stars blurred and the stone under Quill's hands vibrated with a frequency his body knew before his mind could name it.

The elderly frog beside Marsh leaned closer. Her voice was barely audible above the chord.

"Thirty of these." She paused. Her faded eyes were on the pool. "They never get easier to watch. The first one you think it is beautiful. By the tenth you know it is. That does not help."

Marsh looked at her. The elderly frog did not look back. She was watching the pool with the stillness of someone who had learned that watching was all you could do.

Reed was whispering. He had been whispering since the breathing aligned, a low rapid murmur directed at nobody, his voice doing what Reed's voice always did when confronted with something his training had not prepared him for: narrating, measuring, trying to hold the impossible inside a framework. Quill caught fragments. "The synchronization rates are." A pause. Reed's pencil tapped against his knee. "I have never seen biological systems achieve this kind of." Another pause, longer. His pencil stopped. Reed adjusted his glasses and they fogged immediately and he did not wipe them. His voice changed. "Wait. Wait, something is wrong with."

Then Reed stopped whispering.

In the pool, the chord deepened. The individual frogs were barely distinguishable now. Their colors had merged into a single shifting pattern that moved across all of them like weather across a landscape. Their breathing was one breath. Their croaking was one voice made of forty voices that had stopped pretending they were forty. The water between them carried their chemistry, the copper-and-herb output of forty bodies dissolving their signals into a shared medium, and the medium was not just water anymore. It was the frogs and the water they sat in and the stone beneath the water and the vibration that traveled through all of it, approaching the boundary of being a single thing.

The Almost.

Quill knew the word. Vesh had explained it weeks ago, in the warm pools upstairs, between mouthfuls of fermented grub. Two hundred million years of trying. The chord nearly resolving. Never fully resolving. The puddles almost remembering they were water.

This was what it looked like.

The chord climbed. The single color across forty bodies brightened, pale and luminous, and the water's reflected stars blurred into a continuous glow, and the sound filled the chamber, not louder but deeper, pressing into Quill's chest and jaw and the spaces behind his eyes. The frogs in the pool were not frogs. They were one thing. Almost. The boundary between them was still there, thin, a membrane that light passed through, and the membrane was thinning, and for a span of time Quill could not measure, the chamber went still and the chord hung at a pitch that was not a pitch but a threshold, and everything in the room leaned toward it.

Then the chord receded.

Not all at once. The single color broke first, individual markings surfacing like objects emerging from fog. The breathing separated, rhythms drifting apart. The croaking dissolved from one voice back into many, the pitches scattering, each frog's voice returning to its own frequency. The membrane thickened. The frogs became frogs again.

Some were dazed. They floated in the warm water with half-focused eyes, their colors cycling slowly through patterns that looked involuntary, the skin processing something the mind had not yet caught up to. Some were euphoric, their colors bright and fast, the look of someone who had been somewhere extraordinary and had not entirely returned. Some were simply exhausted. They climbed the pool's edges with slow, heavy movements and sat on the stone and breathed their own breath and did not speak.

One did not move.

A young operator near the pool's center. She floated in the water, her body still, her colors flat. Not the flat of exhaustion. The flat of absence. Her eyes were open and they were not tracking anything.

The frogs on either side of her separated, making space, and two medical frogs at the water's edge slid into the pool with efficient, practiced movements. They reached her in seconds. One supported her body. The other checked her skin, her eyes, the chemical output from her throat. They lifted her from the water and carried her to the stone ledge at the far side of the pool, water streaming from her limp body back into the pool where it mixed with the water that still carried everyone else's chemistry. Their movements were smooth. Unhurried. They had done this before.

Vesh did not turn to look. His gold flecks had dimmed to a faint trace against his emerald skin. He sat on his stone shelf at the pool's edge and watched the frogs who were returning to themselves and did not look at the one who was not returning.

The crowd on the ledges did not react. The elderly frog beside Marsh watched the medical frogs carry the operator away and her expression did not change. Her faded skin held steady. Thirty of these. The young first-timer across the pool had gone blank, all color drained, the translucent pale of total fear that Quill had seen once before, in the Speaking Chambers during the Siberian meeting, when sixty frogs had processed the same dread at the same moment and every other color had abandoned them. Frogs on either side of the young one did not look at him. The older female who had steadied him earlier kept her forelimb on his back but did not turn. The observers on the upper tiers continued watching the pool. The lower tiers were quiet. Nobody spoke about the operator. Nobody adjusted their posture to acknowledge what had happened. 
Marsh's hand closed on Quill's forearm. Her grip was tight. The pencil callus on her middle finger pressed into his skin.

Reed had stopped whispering. His glasses were fogged and he did not wipe them. He sat very still, his hands flat on the stone ledge, looking at the place where the operator had been floating. His pencil lay where it had rolled from his knee to the stone. He did not pick it up.

The three of them sat on the upper ledge. Below, the pool settled. The remaining frogs continued surfacing, returning to themselves, and the process was ordinary. That was the thing. The pool's routine absorbed the loss. The water cycled. The bioluminescent stars resumed their slow drift. The medical frogs reached the far exit with the operator's body limp between them, her eyes still open, and the stone passage took them and they were gone, and the chamber continued as if the number had always been thirty-nine.

Vesh spoke into the chamber's quiet. His voice was steady. His gold flecks were beginning to brighten again, slowly, color by color, like a room where someone was turning up the lights one at a time.

"They are not losing themselves." He was looking at the pool, at the frogs still surfacing, still separating, still becoming individuals again. "They are remembering that the self was always temporary. The chord was always there. We just stop pretending we are solos."

Nobody answered him. The pool's water settled. The bioluminescent stars below steadied and resumed their slow drift. The chamber held the silence for a long time, and in the silence Quill could hear the water channels in the walls carrying vibration from the settlement above, the constant murmur of twelve hundred frogs going about the work of being alive, and beneath it, the fading hum of the chord, still present in the stone, still present in his teeth, still present in a place behind his sternum that he had not known existed until it started vibrating.

Marsh's grip on his arm loosened. She did not let go.

The frogs filed out slowly. The observers first, descending the stone tiers in small groups, their colors private again, muted, the performance of normalcy resuming. Two frogs on the lower tier exchanged a glance that lasted longer than necessary and then one of them looked away and hopped down to the next ledge. The elderly frog beside Marsh rose without a word and made her way down with the careful movements of old joints. She did not look back at the pool. She had seen thirty of these and she would see more and the getting-easier part was not coming and she had stopped expecting it. The participants followed from the pool, slower, some still dazed, leaning on each other. The young first-timer was helped down the tiers by the older female who had steadied him earlier, her forelimb on his back, guiding him toward the exit without speaking. His colors were beginning to return in small, uncertain patches. He would be all right. Probably. The word "probably" was the thing the community had learned to live with, and the living-with-it was the thing that nobody mentioned and nobody could avoid.

Vesh remained at the pool's edge until the chamber was nearly empty. Then he stood, shook himself once, water scattering from his skin, and hopped down from the shelf. He passed Quill on the ledge without stopping. His gold flecks were bright. His lopsided expression was serene. He smelled of the pool's mineral water and the concentrated chemistry of forty frogs who had, for a few minutes, almost stopped being forty.

He did not mention the operator. He walked into the stone passage and the dim light took him.

Quill sat in his chamber.

The room was dark. Small. The walls were root and stone and the bioluminescence had been turned low, barely enough to see by. The sounds from the settlement filtered through the walls, distant and constant, the murmur of water channels and the low vibration that was either infrastructure or prayer. Marsh had said something to him in the corridor on the way up. He could not remember what. Reed had taken off his glasses and cleaned them and put them back on and they had stayed clean, and Quill had watched him do this without thinking anything about it.

He sat on the sleeping ledge and tried to find his edges. The boundary where Martin Quill ended and the rest of the world began. It had been clear once. He was certain of that. There had been a line, definite and familiar, and he had lived inside it for forty-four years without questioning its location.

The edges were less distinct than he remembered. He was not sure when they had moved.

---

# Chapter 21: The Archivist's Silence

The eastern nursery was running thin.

Marsh counted as she entered. She had been counting things for weeks now, a habit that had migrated from her field notebook into her body, so that she tallied absences the way other people noticed weather. The nursery's morning shift had run four caretakers when she first visited, rotating through a larger pool of twelve. Then three after the staging grounds expanded. Now two. Oona, and an older caretaker whose name Marsh had never caught.

The pools were warm and dim. Bioluminescent light from the walls threw soft blue across the water's surface, and the tadpoles moved in the shallow sections in patterns Marsh had learned to read. Usually they drifted, clustered loosely, their small dark bodies following the gentle current that the caretakers maintained by adjusting the water channels. This morning they were not drifting. They moved in tight circles near the edges of the pools, crowding toward the stone walls, their tails flicking in agitated bursts that sent small ripples across the surface. Marsh had seen stressed fish behave this way. She had never thought she would see it in a nursery.

Oona was sorting. She stood at the edge of the largest pool with her forelimbs submerged, lifting tadpoles one at a time and relocating them between temperature zones. Her orange throat patch was dim. She worked with the steady, unthinking rhythm of someone who had done this so many times that her hands knew the task better than her mind did.

"It always thins before a Pruning," Oona said. She did not look up. "They reassign caretakers to support work. Logistics. Food preparation for the staging teams. Things that are, apparently, more urgent than tadpoles." She lifted a tadpole and held it briefly, examining it with a practiced tilt of her head, then placed it in a warmer section. "The tadpoles do not mind. Tadpoles do not mind anything, which is the best thing about tadpoles."

The older caretaker straightened from the far pool. She was larger than Oona, her coloring a deep mottled brown that had faded with age to something closer to bark. She had been working the nurseries for most of her adult life. Marsh knew this because the older caretaker mentioned it in nearly every conversation, not from vanity but from the authority of someone whose body of evidence was measured in generations of tadpoles raised and released.

"Tadpoles do not mind?" The older caretaker's voice carried across the shallow water. "This batch has not settled in three days. They taste the stress in the water. We taste it. Everyone tastes it. The archive calls it 'elevated ambient chemistry.' I call it fear in the water."

"You call everything fear in the water," Oona said. "Last season you said the same thing about the fermentation runoff."

"The fermentation runoff was also a problem. I was right about the fermentation runoff."

The younger caretaker at the shallow end did not speak. Her skin flickered, a brief shift from green to a muted grey-brown that matched the older caretaker's coloring. Agreement, expressed in the involuntary language of a body that had opinions even when its owner chose silence.

Marsh wrote in her notebook: *Tadpole distress, 3rd consecutive day. Water chemistry? Community stress absorbed through skin. Caretaker staffing 50% of baseline.*

She had started a list in the back pages of the notebook. It was not a list anyone had asked her to compile. It had begun as a set of observations, the kind she always made in the field, small behavioral notes that might or might not mean anything. The list had grown.

Four mourning rituals in the past six days. She had attended two and heard the low vibration of the other two through the settlement walls, the communal hum that meant someone had died and the community was gathering to absorb their final chemical signal. Before the Pruning preparations intensified, the interval had been longer. One every four or five days, which was the normal rate for an aging population in a settlement of twelve hundred. The current rate was nearly double.

She had been watching the frogs who died. Not the displacement operators, whose risks were visible and acknowledged. The elderly. A caretaker from the western nursery who had worked her shift the morning before and was dead by evening. Two frogs from the residential warrens that had been evacuated when voss pushed through the walls, both old, both relocated to temporary quarters in the crowded western section. An engineer's assistant, elderly, who had collapsed at a staging ground and been carried to the treatment chambers and had not come back.

Each death was absorbed by the community in the standard way. The body placed in water. The living frogs gathering to receive the final chemical broadcast. The archive receiving the biological imprint. The phrase spoken: wet in, wet out. The community dispersing within the hour. Life continuing.

None of the deaths were unusual on their own. Old frogs died. It was the nature of short lives that the old were always close to the end, and any disruption could shorten the distance. Marsh knew this. She had documented aging in animal populations for fifteen years. She knew what accelerated mortality looked like.

It looked like this.

"Oona." Marsh kept her voice level. A question, not an accusation. "The mourning rituals. I have been counting them."

Oona's hands paused over the tadpole she was holding. Her throat patch flickered, a brief orange that went out again immediately. She placed the tadpole in the warm section and wiped her forelimbs on the pool's stone edge.

"You count things," Oona said. "You count everything. This is your most human quality. Not the height or the dry skin. The counting."

"Four in six days. That is more than usual."

"It is more than usual."

"The ones I have seen are elderly. Not operators. Not Pruning casualties. Older frogs."

Oona continued sorting. She picked up a tadpole, examined it, placed it. Picked up another. Her movements were slower now, the automatic rhythm broken by something she was deciding whether to say.

"The Pruning costs lives before it begins," Oona said. Her voice was flat and factual in the way Marsh had come to recognize as Oona's version of honesty stripped of its usual warmth. "It always has. The breathing sessions change the water chemistry. More sessions, more chemicals. The stress of preparation, the displacement of families, the disruption of routine. The old ones feel it first. Their skin absorbs more than it should. Their bodies do not filter the way younger bodies do." She placed a tadpole with particular care, as if this one, at least, she could put in exactly the right place. "The archive does not emphasize this."

Two caretakers at the far end of the nursery exchanged a look. Neither spoke. They did not need to.

The older caretaker crossed the pools to where Oona stood. Her brown skin was dark and still, no flickering, no shifts. The coloring of someone who had decided what she thought a long time ago.

"My mother died during the last Pruning," the older caretaker said. "Not from an operation. Not from the merging. From the water. From the stress in the water that nobody measures because measuring it would mean admitting the preparation kills people who are not part of the preparation." She looked at Marsh. "You are writing this down."

Marsh looked at her notebook. She was, in fact, writing it down.

"Good," the older caretaker said, and returned to her pool.

---

The Archive of Pem was busier than Marsh had seen it.

The corridors leading to the central chamber held traffic: frogs carrying spore samples in stone dishes, a pair of younger archivists moving quickly past her with bundles of crystallized material that caught the dim light and held it in milky suspension. The smell was stronger than usual, old mushrooms and mineral water and the faint electrical scent that Marsh had learned meant the archive's living tissue stores were active, processing, receiving. A civilization's memory bank operating at capacity during a crisis.

Inside the main chamber, a junior archivist sat at a workstation cataloguing spore patterns. Small stone trays held rows of dried spore samples, each labeled in markings Marsh could not read, arranged in sequences the junior archivist was sorting with the focused patience of someone filing documents in a library that happened to be alive. The samples looked like dried flower petals, pale and flat, but when the archivist held them up to the bioluminescent light they showed internal structures, faint branching patterns that encoded information in a form Marsh could not decipher and probably never would.

Pem was at the far end of the chamber, attending to a tissue archive. The old archivist's faded grey-green skin was nearly translucent in the low light, thin enough that Marsh could see the faint shadow of bone beneath. Pem moved with the economy of someone rationing their remaining energy. Clear, bright eyes in a face that had stopped bothering with the vanity of vivid coloring decades ago.

Pem looked up when Marsh entered. A slow turn. A steady regard. Pem had always been welcoming to the humans, in the archivist's own way, which was to say: not warm but available, present in the manner of an institution that had existed long enough to accommodate all visitors without being changed by any of them.

"Dr. Marsh." Pem's voice was deliberate, each word placed. "You have come with questions."

"I have."

Pem waited. An editor scanning a manuscript before deciding what to cut.

"I have been tracking mortality in the settlement," Marsh said. "Since the Pruning preparations began. I am seeing an increase in deaths among the elderly that does not correlate with the operational casualties. It correlates with the intensified breathing sessions and the general disruption."

Pem's expression did not change. The junior archivist at the workstation, however, flickered. A quick, involuntary shift across her skin, green to pale to green again, the coloring of a frog who had heard something she was not supposed to react to and whose body had reacted before she could stop it.

"The operational casualties are documented," Pem said. "The displacement teams have lost three operators to coherence failure since the preparations began. One in the secondary staging pool this week. The medical reports are available to the coordination office. The archive records these as operational costs, which they are."

"I am not asking about the operators."

"You are asking about the others."

"Yes."

Pem was still for a long time. The tissue archive behind the old archivist pulsed faintly, its living surface catching the dim light, a wall of preserved memory that breathed and grew and forgot nothing. Or forgot selectively, which was perhaps the same thing.

"The others die," Pem said. "This is observed. It is recorded. Old frogs die during Pruning cycles at a higher rate than during resting periods. This has been true for as long as the archive has maintained mortality records, which is a very long time."

"How much higher?"

"The archive does not publish aggregate mortality figures during active Pruning cycles. The coordination office receives operational reports. The general population receives general reassurance."

"You have the numbers."

"The archive has everything."

"And what does the archive do with everything?"

The junior archivist's skin flickered again. She kept her eyes on the spore trays. Her hands had stopped sorting.

Pem looked at Marsh with the bright, clear eyes that seemed to belong to a much younger frog housed in a body that was running out of time. The gaze was not hostile. It was not apologetic. It was the gaze of someone who had been asked this question before, in different forms, across many generations, and had developed an answer that was true without being complete.

"The archive records what the civilization needs to remember." Pem spoke slowly. "What it needs to forget, it also records. In different places. Accessed differently."

Marsh held the words in her mind and turned them. The junior archivist had gone very still at her workstation. Her skin was flat green, the color of someone working hard to broadcast nothing at all.

"You are telling me there are two archives," Marsh said.

"I am telling you there is one archive with different rooms. Some rooms are open. Some rooms require knowing that they exist." Pem paused. The old archivist's faded coloring shifted, a slow ripple of something that might have been blue, the residual signature of concentration. "This is not deception. A civilization that remembers everything equally remembers nothing. Emphasis is a form of survival. What we emphasize, we strengthen. What we do not emphasize, we permit to recede. The deaths of old frogs during Pruning cycles are real and they are recorded and they are not emphasized, because emphasizing them would change nothing about the Pruning and would damage the community's willingness to participate in what the Pruning requires."

"So the archive decides what the community can bear to know."

"The archive preserves. The archive also curates. These have never been separate functions." Pem's bright eyes held steady. "You are a scientist, Dr. Marsh. You understand selection. In memory, as in nature, what is selected for persists. What is not selected for recedes. The archive selects."

Marsh closed her notebook. She did not write down what Pem had said. She did not need to. It would stay.

---

The biologists' quarters smelled of damp root walls and the faint sweetness of the dried moss that served as bedding. Vale was sitting on the stone ledge that functioned as a bench, his long legs folded at an angle the frog-scaled room had never been designed to accommodate. Hart leaned against the far wall with her arms crossed. Reed was on the floor with his back against the wall, three of Krrik's moss sheets spread across his lap, his pencil tapping against one of them in a rhythm that had nothing to do with the conversation.

Marsh told them what she had found. The thinned nursery. The accelerating deaths. Oona's explanation. The archive.

She told it the way she told everything: in sequence, grounded in what she had seen, without commentary. She described the tadpoles circling the pool edges. The older caretaker's voice when she mentioned her mother. The junior archivist's involuntary flicker. Pem's careful, deliberate words. She did not interpret. She laid down the evidence and let it sit.

Vale spoke first. "Different rooms. Accessed differently." He repeated Pem's words with the precision of someone cataloguing ammunition. His jaw was set, the tendons in his neck visible, the posture he adopted when anger had moved past the arguing stage into the architectural stage, the stage where he was building a case. "So the most ancient civilization on this planet maintains a two-tier information system. Public memory for morale. Private memory for continuity. And the keeper of both is a single institution with no external oversight."

"Right," Hart said. "So the most advanced civilization on Earth manages public health data the same way every government on ours does. Shocking."

"This is not a joke, Simone."

"I am not joking. I am recognizing a pattern. Suppressing inconvenient mortality data during a crisis to maintain public cooperation. The NHS does it. The CDC does it. The Threnn do it with spore records instead of spreadsheets. The delivery system changes. The logic does not."

Vale's hands were flat on his knees, fingers spread. His version of restraint. "The logic is that an institution with total control over a civilization's memory supply is choosing which deaths count and which deaths are filed in a room nobody knows exists. That is not public health management. That is narrative control."

"It is both," Hart said. "It is always both."

Reed looked up from his moss sheets. He had been half-listening, his pencil still, his glasses low on his nose. "How much higher?" he said. "The mortality rate. Pem said higher. Did Pem give you a number?"

"No. Pem was very clear about not giving me a number."

Reed pushed his glasses up and returned to the moss sheets. His pencil resumed tapping. Krrik's latest growth data, Marsh guessed. The numbers that consumed him now. He was still inside the Pruning's operational crisis, and the archive's political management was a problem he did not have the bandwidth to carry. She did not blame him. They were all carrying different pieces and none of them could hold the full weight.

The chamber entrance darkened. Quill stood in the corridor, one hand on the root wall, his pale face catching the dim bioluminescence. He looked tired. He had been looking tired for days, but this was different. The fatigue had settled deeper, into the muscles around his eyes, into his posture, into the way he entered the room and sat down on the stone floor without asking what they were discussing.

"Pem," he said. Not a question.

"You heard?"

"I heard enough from the corridor." He leaned his head back against the wall. "The archive manages what the civilization remembers. It has probably always managed what the civilization remembers. This is not new information, Ellie. This is the information Pem did not give me when I asked about the previous humans. The silences were the same shape."

"The shape being institutional," Vale said.

"The shape being necessary." Quill closed his eyes. "They believe it is necessary. That is what makes it work."

Vale looked at Quill with the narrow, watchful expression he reserved for moments when Quill's sympathies alarmed him. "You are defending them."

"I am describing them. There is a difference."

"The difference keeps getting smaller."

The room was quiet. Water moved through the channels in the walls, the constant murmur of a settlement built on flow. Reed's pencil had stopped tapping. He was reading the same line of Krrik's notation for the third time, his eyes unfocused. Hart stood against the wall with her arms still crossed, looking at the ceiling, or at the roots that served as a ceiling, or at nothing.

Marsh sat with her notebook closed in her lap. She had spent the day compiling evidence of something the frogs already knew and had decided not to discuss. The elder caretaker knew. Oona knew. The junior archivist knew. Pem knew. The knowledge was there, distributed through the community in private conversations and involuntary skin-flickers, available to anyone who counted the mourning rituals and noticed who was dying. It was not a secret. It was something worse than a secret: a fact that everyone possessed and no institution acknowledged.

She opened her mouth to say something about this, and a sound from the corridor stopped her.

Oona appeared in the entrance carrying a stone bowl. The bowl steamed faintly, and the smell that preceded her into the room was warm and earthy, fermented protein, the unmistakable scent of Oona's grubs, which Marsh had learned to tolerate and Hart had learned to enjoy and Reed still approached with visible reluctance.

Oona looked at the five of them. She took in the room. The postures. The silence. The way Quill's eyes were closed and Vale's jaw was tight and Hart's arms were crossed and Reed's pencil was still and Marsh's notebook was shut. She read all of it. She was a frog who had spent her life reading rooms, not through education or special talent but through the ordinary skill of someone who had raised tadpoles and managed neighbors and noticed things because noticing things was what you did when you lived in a community and cared about the people in it.

She set the bowl on the stone ledge beside Vale.

"The grubs are good this batch," Oona said. "Eat."

She did not ask what they had been discussing. She did not offer comfort or explanation or philosophy. She stood in the entrance for a moment, her orange throat patch neither bright nor dim, holding at a middle frequency that carried no gossip and no judgment, only the steady signal of someone who was there.

Then she turned and left.

The grubs steamed in their bowl. The water moved in the walls. Vale looked at the bowl and then at the corridor where Oona had gone and his jaw loosened by one degree. Hart uncrossed her arms and reached for the bowl. Reed set down his pencil.

Marsh opened her notebook and wrote nothing.

---

# Chapter 22: Talla's Wager

The Third Pool was not a secret. That was the point.

Hart sat on a stone ledge above the water, her boots pulled up beneath her because the ledge was scaled for frogs and her feet would otherwise dangle in the pool where a dozen agitated bodies were producing enough color to light the alcove without bioluminescence. The pool was communal, open to the corridor, and anyone walking past could see exactly who was in it. Three frogs had walked past in the ten minutes since Hart arrived, and each had looked in with the particular focus of someone cataloguing faces. Two had continued walking. One had entered and taken a position near the back, where the pool shallowed against the root wall, and had not spoken.

Twelve frogs. Hart counted because counting was what she did when she could not run a diagnostic. Twelve frogs in warm water, their skins throwing rapid shifts across the pool's surface, greens and olives and agitated ambers reflecting off the stone ceiling in overlapping bands. The water rippled with their chemistry. The air smelled of copper and crushed herbs and something sharper underneath, the acrid note Hart had come to associate with frog adrenaline, if frogs had adrenaline, which Reed would correct her about and which she did not care about because the smell meant stress and stress was stress regardless of the molecule.

Talla stood at the pool's center. The water reached her chest. Her dark skin was nearly black in the low light, her small body rigid, held upright with the compensating posture Hart recognized from weeks of watching her refuse to be overlooked. She was not making a speech. She was listing things.

"The coordination office reports seventeen operator casualties across two displacement waves. This is the number released to the general population. The archive records additional deaths among the elderly since preparations began. Oona Reedmoss, who tends the eastern nursery, reports seven mourning rituals in twelve days against a baseline of roughly one per week. The coordination office has not commented on this discrepancy. I am commenting on it now."

A frog near the pool's edge shifted position. Male, older, his skin a deep brown mottled with age spots that did not flicker because age spots did not flicker, a body past the point of involuntary display. He had the look of someone who had attended many meetings and considered most of them a waste of water.

"I want the aggregate mortality data released," Talla said. "Not the operator count. The full cost. Every death that correlates with Pruning preparations. I want the breathing circle mandate examined by practitioners who are not appointed by the coordination office. And I want the Siberian method tested. Technical coordination without the merging. They do it. It works. We could do it."

"The Siberians lose more operators than we do," said a frog to Hart's left, her voice carrying the brisk, certain tone of someone stating a number she had looked up.

"The Siberians lose operators to displacement errors, not to consciousness dissolution. There is a difference between a technical failure and feeding your people into a ritual that sometimes does not give them back."

The pool surface was alive. Colors moved across it in competing patterns, the reflected skins of twelve frogs processing twelve reactions at once. Some of the patterns aligned briefly, clusters of agreement rippling from one body to the next, and some clashed, amber cutting through green. Hart watched the surface the way she watched cultures in a petri dish: looking for what was growing, and which way it was spreading.

An older female near the back, grey-green and faded, spoke without raising her body from the water. "You ask for alternatives during the crisis itself." Her voice was level and tired. "Talla, this is not courage. This is timing."

The pool shifted. Several frogs turned toward the speaker. Colors moved.

"If not during the crisis, when?" The response came from a younger frog near Talla, his olive skin bright with a conviction his voice had not caught up to. "After the crisis, we are told to be grateful. Before the crisis, we are told to wait. There is never a correct time to ask whether the system that saves us is also the system that kills us, because the system is always either saving us or preparing to save us."

The older female did not reply. Her colors held steady. She had heard this argument before. She found it correct and inconvenient.

Two frogs near the pool's edge started speaking at once. Their voices collided, overlapped, separated. A brown male with fading spots on his throat was arguing that the Siberian method had never been tested at the Amazon settlement's scale. A smaller female beside him insisted scale was precisely the argument the coordination office used to prevent any testing at all. A third frog, who had been silent until now, asked whether Talla had actually spoken to the Siberian engineers or was citing secondhand reports. Talla said she had spoken to them. The third frog's colors shifted, processing this.

The pool was loud. Twelve voices finding and losing each other, the water's surface turbulent with their chemistry, colors flashing in combinations Hart could not parse but could read by temperature. Agreement was warm. Disagreement was sharp. The older female at the back watched it all with visible patience. The water would eventually settle. That was the problem Talla was trying to name: settling was what the system relied on.

Talla's pale spots were visible. She controlled them the way Hart controlled her own jaw when she was angry, through effort that was itself a tell. The spots ran across her back in a pattern that broadcast what her voice refused to: that this was costing her, that standing in a public pool calling out the coordination office was not comfortable dissent performed for an audience but the act of someone who had decided the cost was cheaper than the silence.

Hart sat on her ledge and watched a political organism under stress. The supporters, the skeptics, the one frog at the back who had entered without speaking and whose colors were flat and controlled and whose presence would be reported to someone by the end of the day. The young frog whose conviction outpaced his argument. The older female whose objection was not disagreement but exhaustion. Hart had seen this room in Manaus during a dengue response. She had seen it in a hospital boardroom in Montreal. She had seen it in every system she had ever studied, the moment when the people maintaining the machinery started asking whether the machinery was maintaining them.

The pool hummed. A low vibration passed through the water, the bodies processing something collectively, and Hart was not sure whether it was a vote or a feeling or both.

---

Noll's response arrived through intermediaries the next morning.

Hart heard about it from a frog she did not know, a small pale female who stopped her in the corridor outside the fermentation cellars and delivered the message with the cheerful neutrality of someone reading a weather report. Talla's concerns were valued. Her proposals were being considered by the relevant coordinating bodies. In the meantime, the coordination office wished to remind all residents that the breathing circles were a community resource available to everyone, and that attendance during this difficult period was encouraged. Talla specifically was invited to attend the evening session as a gesture of communal solidarity.

The frog delivered this without irony. She believed it. That was the thing. She believed the message she was carrying because the message was constructed to be believed, every word shaped to sound like care, every suggestion framed as invitation, the entire response a sentence made of open doors that led to the same room.

Hart thanked her and went to find Talla.

She found her at the communal meal.

The warren's eating space was large, low-ceilinged, warm. Dozens of frogs occupied the shallow pools and stone ledges that served as tables, and the smell was fermented protein and fresh insects and the mineral tang of standing water. Food passed from group to group in stone dishes. Frogs entered from three corridors, ate, argued, left. The domestic normalcy of shared eating in a civilization running at crisis capacity.

Hart took a position at the ledge where Talla sat with two frogs from the Third Pool, both younger, both eating with the concentrated speed of people who had somewhere to be. Hart picked up a grub. She had learned to eat the grubs. They were not good. They were not bad. They were protein, and Hart respected protein.

Talla ate without speaking. Her spots were controlled. Her jaw worked on something that was probably a beetle larva. The two younger frogs beside her were talking about displacement schedules, routine logistics, their voices low and ordinary.

Then Talla stopped eating.

She did not stand. She did not raise her voice beyond what Hart would have called conversational, though in a space with frog acoustics that meant half the room could hear her.

"The breathing circles are scheduled for this evening. I will not be attending." She spoke toward the pool in front of her, not addressing any particular frog, not performing for the room, simply stating a fact loudly enough that the fact became public. "If my absence endangers the Pruning, then the Pruning has a design problem, not a participation problem."

The meal rippled.

It was not a sound. It was a visual event. Colors shifted across the room in a wave that started near Talla and spread outward through the bodies of forty or fifty frogs, greens darkening or brightening, ambers flaring, throats pulsing with signals Hart could read by feel if not by grammar. Conversations stopped and restarted in clusters. Two frogs at a near table stood and moved to another section without looking at Talla. Three frogs from the corridor entrance moved closer, their colors curious, interested, the skin of people who had heard something they wanted to hear said out loud.

A young frog at the table behind Talla set down her food and left without finishing. An older pair at the far wall did not look up. They continued eating with the deliberate steadiness of people who had lived through previous Prunings and previous dissidents and did not intend to interrupt their meal for either. Near the corridor entrance, a group of three frogs spoke rapidly to each other, their voices too low for Hart to catch, their colors quick and private.

Hart watched the room rearrange itself around Talla's refusal and thought: this is how it works. Not the refusal. The absorption. The system was already processing the disruption, sorting the population into positions, each frog calculating where they stood relative to the line Talla had drawn in the warm water.

Vale was across the room. Hart could see him on a raised ledge near the eastern wall, his long legs folded beneath him, eating nothing, watching with the narrow focus he used when he was cataloguing a power structure in real time. He caught Hart's eye and gave her the smallest nod.

Marsh was closer, two pools away, seated beside Oona. Hart looked at Oona. She looked at Oona because Marsh was looking at Oona, and Marsh looked at things worth looking at.

Oona ate.

Her orange throat patch held at a steady middle frequency. Not bright with gossip, not dim with distress. Controlled. She did not move away from Talla's table, though she easily could have. She did not move closer. She continued eating her grubs with steady, domestic rhythm, and for the first time in Hart's observation, she was choosing to display no opinion at all. Her colors were flat, deliberate, the chromatic equivalent of a woman eating her lunch with great concentration while the table next to her caught fire.

Marsh's pencil was in her hand. She was not writing. She was holding it and watching Oona the way a field naturalist watches a subject doing something new, and Hart knew that the notebook entry, when it came, would be precise and devastating and would describe the most politically sophisticated act in the room: the ordinary frog's refusal to take a position.

---

Evening.

The biologists' quarters were lit by the wall's dim fungal glow, which made five human faces look slightly green. Hart leaned against the root wall with her arms crossed. Vale sat on the stone ledge, legs folded at an angle the room punished. Reed was on the floor with his back against the wall, Krrik's moss sheets in his lap. Marsh stood near the entrance. Quill sat in the far corner, knees drawn up, his back against the stone.

The corridor darkened.

Noll entered their quarters for the first time. He moved through the entrance with careful, deliberate steps, rehearsal and nature indistinguishable. His muted green-grey coloring was steady, calm, controlled. His skin had been a political instrument for longer than most frogs had been alive. His damaged forelimb was tucked inward. He did not duck through the entrance because he did not need to. The room had been built for his species. The humans were the ones who did not fit.

Pem followed. Slow, translucent, bright-eyed. The old archivist moved to the corner opposite Quill and settled there. Quiet. Missing nothing.

"I apologize for the hour," Noll said. His voice filled the small room without effort, the measured cadence of a coordinator addressing a gathering he had not called but somehow chaired. "The day's events have been, I think, distressing for all of us. Talla's concerns are genuine. I wish to be clear about that. Her frustration is heard and her questions are not unwelcome."

Vale's jaw tightened. Hart saw it. Nobody else did. Reed glanced up from his moss sheets and then back down.

"The community is under strain," Noll continued. "The Pruning proceeds. The operators do their work. The breathing circles continue. But cohesion requires more than procedure. It requires trust. And trust, at this moment, is thinner than I would like."

He let this settle. His colors did not flicker. The room's fungal light painted his muted green-grey skin the same shade as the walls, so that he looked, for a moment, like part of the architecture. A feature of the room rather than a visitor to it.

"I come with a request," Noll said. "Not an instruction. Not a requirement. A request."

He paused. The pause was the length of a breath and the weight of a decision being offered as a gift.

"Your perspective as outside observers carries weight. The community knows you have seen our preparations, our rituals, our collective effort. If you were to speak about what you have witnessed, the cooperation, the sacrifice, the beauty of the response, it might help the community find cohesion in a difficult moment."

The silence in the room was the kind Hart associated with the moment between a diagnosis and a reaction. She looked at Noll. He meant it. That was the obscene part. He was not scheming. He was not manipulating. He was a coordinator who had spent his entire adult life aligning collective effort, and he was doing it now, in this room, with these five people, because he genuinely believed their voices could help. He could not see why they would refuse. The request was reasonable. The cause was real. The crisis was real. The community needed cohesion. Outside perspectives carried weight. Every word was true and the sum of the words was a trap, and Noll could not see the trap because to Noll the trap and the truth were the same thing.

"No." Vale. Immediate, clipped, the single syllable carrying the weight of a door closing. He did not elaborate. He did not need to. His jaw was set and his eyes were on Noll and the word was complete.

Marsh spoke next, her voice quiet and firm. "I am not willing to be used as a political instrument. What I have witnessed is real, and it is also not mine to narrate for the coordination office. I am sorry, Noll. No."

Reed looked at Vale. He looked at Marsh. He looked at his hands on Krrik's moss sheets, his pencil still, his glasses low on his nose. His mouth opened, closed. He was calculating something, Hart could see it, the instinct to be helpful warring with a recognition that had arrived too late for comfort and just in time for use. He shook his head. A small motion. He did not trust his voice to say the right thing, so he let his body say no and went back to the moss sheets.

Hart laughed.

It was short, hard, and not amused. The sound of a diagnostic completing. She had been waiting for this since the first pool tour, since Noll's first careful sentence, since the word "entanglement" had been offered as philosophy and accepted as hospitality. The request was the system doing what systems did. She had seen it in every institution she had ever studied, the moment when the outside observer was asked to validate the inside. The laugh said all of it. She did not add words because the laugh was not a response to Noll. It was a response to the fact that she had predicted this in her field notes three weeks ago and had not shown anyone the entry.

Noll received the laugh without flinching. His colors held steady. His damaged forelimb did not move.

Quill said nothing.

The silence expanded. Noll waited. The room waited. Hart watched Quill from her position against the wall. He sat in his corner with his knees drawn up and his pale face lit by fungal glow and his hands still and his eyes open and looking at Noll or through Noll or at something behind Noll that the rest of them could not see. He did not speak. He did not shake his head. He did not nod.

Noll regarded Quill's silence for a long moment, and then he took it. Gently. Completely. Without confirming what it meant.

"I understand," Noll said. He said it to all of them and to none of them, and the phrase covered five different refusals with a single courtesy. "The offer remains, should any of you reconsider. I do not press. I ask. The difference matters to us."

He turned toward the entrance. Pem did not move immediately. The old archivist's bright eyes traveled across the five humans in the small room, resting on each face with unhurried attention. Hart met Pem's gaze and held it. The archivist's faded skin was still, translucent in the dim light. Pem looked at Vale's set jaw and Marsh's steady hands and Reed's downturned face and Hart's crossed arms and Quill's silence, and then rose and followed Noll out of the room with slow, certain steps, having already filed what needed filing.

The corridor took them and the room held the five of them in the fungal light.

Nobody spoke for a while. The water channels murmured in the walls. Reed's pencil did not move. Vale uncrossed his legs, recrossed them, and stared at the entrance where Noll had stood.

Hart looked at the entrance and then at the wall and then at her own hands, crossed over her chest, the hands she had used to catalogue fungal growth and test retardant compounds and take samples of a civilization she had entered as an epidemiologist and was now living inside as a subject.

The walls had always been there. They were just noticing them.

---

# Chapter 23: The Displacement

The staging grounds had been an open space the last time Reed saw them. A gathering area, half arena, half town square, where frogs played vibration games and argued about fermentation technique and lounged on wet stone in the dim light of a civilization that had been doing this for longer than mammals had been alive.

It was not an open space now.

Reed stood at the edge and counted. He could not help counting. It was what he did when the scale of something exceeded his ability to process it emotionally, which was often, and which was happening now with a thoroughness that made his fingers twitch for a pencil. Hundreds of frogs. Not dozens. Not the forty or fifty he had seen at the mourning ritual or the sixty-odd at the Siberian meeting. Hundreds. Arranged in concentric formations around six displacement platforms, each platform a ring of wet stones threaded with fungal networks that looked, as they had always looked, like something a child might build at the edge of a creek. The most powerful technology on Earth, operated by hundreds of small amphibians sitting in warm water, looked like a very organized picnic.

The sound was the first thing that had stopped him.

He had heard the chord before. In the breathing circles, in fragments carried through the settlement's water channels, in the ceremonial pool where the merging ritual had turned individual frogs into something else. But he had never heard it at this scale. Hundreds of voices producing a single sustained note that was not, technically, a single note at all but a layered harmonic so dense that Reed's ears could not separate the components. It entered through his chest. It entered through his feet, carried by the wet stone. It entered through his skin, which was not supposed to be possible for a species that did not breathe through its skin, but the vibration did not care about species boundaries. His teeth hummed.

Colors moved through the operator groups in waves. Not the individual flickers and shifts Reed had learned to read over the past weeks. These were storms. Cascading patterns that swept across dozens of bodies at once, green to gold to deep blue to a color Reed did not have a name for, something between amber and violet that the human visual system was not built to process cleanly. The operators were thinking together, and their skins were broadcasting the thinking. A living screen. A collective mind solving a planetary equation, rendered in color across hundreds of small bodies.

Reed pushed his glasses up his nose and walked into it.

The modulation station was a cluster of computation arrays that Krrik had relocated from the laboratory two days ago. Four moss beds, each the size of a dining table, arranged on raised stone platforms with water channels feeding them from below. The moss was active, its surface patterns shifting in rapid spirals that meant the arrays were running at capacity. A faint smell of ozone hung over the station, mixed with the fermentation-and-copper scent of hundreds of frogs in close proximity.

Krrik was at the central array. He was smaller than the frogs around him, mottled brown-green, perpetually dusty, his asymmetric hop distinctive even in a crowd. He was issuing instructions to two junior engineers, his voice rapid and clipped, social niceties abandoned several days ago.

"Modulation at seven-point-three. No. Seven-point-one. The eastern array is compensating incorrectly. Tell them to reset baseline and recalibrate from the revised growth data. Not the old data. Reed's data. Now."

The junior engineers moved. They did not argue. Nobody argued with Krrik during the Pruning. Reed had watched the transformation over the past week, the grumpy engineer becoming something else, something focused and tireless and slightly frightening, a frog whose entire existence had compressed into the operational problem in front of him.

Reed took his position at the secondary array. His job was small: feed the surface canopy observations into the modulation calculations. The frogs' subsurface sensors tracked the voss growth from below with extraordinary precision, but they had no equivalent data from above. Reed's weeks of canopy stress measurements, his painstaking documentation of root density anomalies and soil temperature gradients and the slow visual evidence of a forest being consumed from underneath, provided the view from outside. A single variable in a system with thousands. A puzzle piece.

He fed the latest readings into the array. The moss absorbed the chemical-encoded data through its surface and integrated it into patterns Reed could watch but not fully understand. His data, his clumsy human observations made with smudged instruments and expired reagents, became part of a computation that had been running, in one form or another, for two hundred million years.

"Your modulation window is too narrow." The Siberian engineer was on the platform's far side, her mottled grey skin dark with concentration, blue flecks visible at her joints. She addressed Krrik with the blunt directness that Reed had come to associate with the Siberian temperament, which was to say, no diplomacy at all. "The Siberian arrays use a broader harmonic range. We lose precision but we do not lose operators."

"You also do not fully displace." Krrik did not look up from the array. "You scatter. We remove."

"Scattering is sufficient for containment."

"Containment is not removal. The growth returns. You cut it back and it regrows and you cut it back again and you call this a method."

"We call it keeping our people alive."

Krrik's skin flashed orange. Brief, involuntary, the warning color Reed had learned meant frustrated beyond words. "The growth is at ninety percent above resting. Your northern quadrant shifted to physical suppression because your modulation failed entirely. Do not lecture me about methods."

The Siberian engineer's colors did not change. She held her position on the platform edge, arms folded, and regarded Krrik the way a surgeon regards a colleague whose technique she considers reckless and whose results she cannot dismiss.

Reed fed another batch of readings into the array and looked up.

From the modulation station, he could see the full staging grounds. The six displacement platforms, each surrounded by rings of synchronized operators, their bodies arranged in precise configurations that Krrik had spent days calculating. The coordination pools between the platforms, where relay frogs maintained the links between operator groups, their colors flickering in patterns that carried information Reed could not decode. Medical stations at the edges, staffed and ready, the frogs there still and alert.

Vale was at the staging ground's eastern edge. He stood on a raised ledge, his tall frame unmistakable among the frogs, his arms folded, watching Noll. Noll was twenty meters away, moving between coordination groups at a pace that was itself a form of governance — measured, unhurried, projecting control. His muted green-grey was steady. His damaged forelimb was tucked against his body. He spoke to a relay coordinator, listened, moved to the next group, spoke again. Even now, in the middle of a planetary operation, Noll was coordinating. Not commanding. Coordinating. Vale's jaw was set, visible even from here.

Marsh was with the medical teams on the western perimeter. She had a stone basin of water and was helping a medical frog clean instruments. She had not been asked to help. She had arrived and started helping, because that was what Marsh did, and the medical frogs had absorbed her presence with the same efficiency they applied to everything else. Her field notebook was tucked into her back pocket. She was not writing in it.

Hart moved between stations. She crossed Reed's field of vision twice in ten minutes, walking from the medical post to the modulation station and back, carrying data that Krrik needed and that the medical frogs generated. The operator health readings. Heart rates, or the frog equivalent. Synchronization depth. Coherence levels. She delivered each report with clipped precision and did not stay to discuss.

Quill was at the perimeter. Reed found him by looking for the only human who was not doing anything. He stood near the support crowd, among the families and logistics crews and off-duty personnel who had gathered to watch their civilization defend itself. He was still. His hands were at his sides. He was watching the operators with the same attention he had brought to the merging ritual, something that was not scientific and not quite spiritual and not quite anything Reed had a word for.

Talla was there.

Reed had not expected to see her. After the communal meal, after her public refusal, he had assumed she would stay away. She stood near the southern platform, her small dark body rigid, her posture the compensating uprightness Reed recognized. Her skin was blank. Not flickering, not shifting, not broadcasting. Controlled to the point of silence, which was itself a statement, because a frog whose colors said nothing was a frog making a choice about what not to say. She watched the operators. She did not participate. She was present because the Pruning was real, and even the dissident who questioned the system came to watch the system work, because the alternative to the system was a planet consumed by its own energy source.

Oona was in the support crowd, carrying food. She moved between groups of off-duty operators with a stone bowl in each hand, distributing fermented grubs, because people needed to eat whether or not the world was ending and Oona intended to make sure they did. She had found time, even now, to express an opinion about the grubs: "Not my best batch. The humidity in the secondary cellar is wrong. I have told the logistics committee." A young operator accepted a grub with visible gratitude. Oona told him to eat slowly because the acidity was intentional and he would understand later. Her orange throat patch was dim. She was not gossiping. This, more than anything else Reed had seen that morning, told him how serious the situation was.

Beyond Oona, the support crowd stretched back into the corridors. Families. Children too young for the nurseries held in parental arms. Logistics frogs carrying supplies. A group of elderly frogs sat on raised stone near the western wall, watching with the stillness of people who had seen this before and survived it and were not certain they would see it again. Two food runners threaded through the crowd carrying stone basins of water. A young frog near the perimeter was crying, her colors flashing in involuntary distress, and the frog beside her put a forelimb on her back and left it there. Nobody told her to stop. Nobody looked away. A civilization was watching itself survive.

The displacement began.

The chord shifted. The operators at the central platform adjusted their synchronization, and the harmonic deepened, dropping below the range of Reed's hearing into something he could only feel. A pressure in his chest. A thickness in the air. The wet stones of the displacement platform began to glow, a faint luminescence that spread through the fungal threading like light through capillaries.

Then the voss vanished.

Not all of it. Not even most of it. But in the root systems directly beneath the central platform, the living material that had been growing beyond all projections, the warm breathing substrate that powered everything and threatened everything, folded. Reed had no better word. The arrays showed it: a section of voss, two hundred meters of root-threaded growth, was simply no longer there. Displaced to a deep ocean trench off the Pacific shelf, where it would settle into cold dark water and slow and eventually stop growing, because voss needed warmth and proximity to living root systems and the deep ocean provided neither.

The second platform fired. The third. The fourth. Across the staging grounds, displacement events rippled outward in a coordinated sequence that Krrik had choreographed with the precision of someone who understood that imprecision meant death. Each event removed a section of voss. Each event required dozens of operators thinking as one, holding the fold in their collective mind, pushing the material across thousands of kilometers of curved space to a destination that existed in their synchronized consciousness as a shared sensory image. Each event cost them something. The operators closest to his station dimmed after each displacement. Their breathing slowed. One frog, a young male near the edge of the nearest ring, swayed and was steadied by the frog beside him without the ring breaking its synchronization.

The arrays showed the growth slowing. The modulation compounds, the seventh reformulation, held. The displacement was working. For a moment, Reed let his hands rest on the stone edge of the computation array and watched the data scrolling across the moss surface and allowed himself to think: it is going to work.

The eastern sectors pushed back.

The array pattern changed. Reed saw it before anyone announced it, because he had been staring at the data for three weeks and knew what the growth curves looked like when they accelerated. The eastern sectors, already thirty-eight percent above projections, surged. The displacement events had triggered a response. The voss, which was alive in ways that neither human nor frog science fully understood, reacted to the removal of material by accelerating growth in adjacent areas, filling the gaps with new substrate faster than the operators could fold it away.

"Eastern sectors accelerating," Reed said. "Growth rate exceeding the revised model. The displacement is triggering compensatory expansion."

Krrik's head turned. His skin cycled through three shades of brown in rapid succession, the unconscious processing display that meant he was thinking faster than he could speak. "Rate?"

"The array shows twelve percent increase in the last four minutes. If the curve holds, it will exceed the operators' displacement capacity in."

"I can read the curve." Krrik's voice was flat. "Tell the eastern relay to increase synchronization depth by two intervals. They will resist. Tell them the alternative is losing the sector."

Reed relayed the instruction. The junior engineer sprinted toward the eastern platforms, her small bright body disappearing into the crowd.

The operators strained. Across the staging grounds, the collective effort was visible in the way the color storms intensified, the patterns becoming more erratic, the chord fracturing at the edges into individual voices that strained to hold their harmony. Two operators at the eastern platform broke synchronization. Their colors scattered, and they were pulled from the pool by the frogs beside them, limp, their eyes unfocused. A medical team moved in. Efficient. Practiced.

A third operator collapsed at the southern platform. She was carried to the medical station, where Marsh was waiting with clean water and the stone instruments the medical frogs used. Hart appeared at Reed's station thirty seconds later.

"Three down. One not breathing on her own. If you push the next wave at the same intensity, you'll lose more."

Krrik did not look up from the array. "Noted. Push the next wave."

Hart's jaw tightened. She looked at Reed. Reed looked at the data. The growth curve was still climbing. The eastern sectors were consuming the displacement gains faster than the operators could produce them. If they stopped, the voss would push through the remaining barriers within days. If they continued, they would lose operators.

Hart left. She walked back toward the medical station, her stride hard, her jaw set. She had delivered a clinical assessment. She had received the answer she expected.

Vesh moved.

Reed had not tracked Vesh's position in the staging grounds. He had been focused on the data, on Krrik's instructions, on the arrays. But now Vesh was among the eastern operators, the ones who were faltering, the ones whose synchronization was breaking apart as the voss pushed back harder than anyone had predicted. Vesh was small, smaller than most of the operators, his emerald green vivid against their fading colors, his gold flecks burning at full intensity. He moved between them in the water, not touching, not speaking in any language Reed could hear. He was singing.

The sound was in Threnn-song. Reed could not understand the words. He could feel the vibration. It passed through the water of the coordination pools, through the stone of the platforms, through the air, and it settled into the chord that the operators were losing, not replacing it but supporting it, the way a hand under an elbow supports a stumbling walker. Vesh's voice was not loud. It did not need to be. It operated on the channel the frogs used for emotional and relational context, the sub-vocal vibration that humans could feel in their chests and their teeth and their bones. Reed felt it. Quill, at the perimeter, stood very still, and Reed knew he felt it too.

The eastern operators steadied. Their colors did not recover fully, but the chaotic scattering slowed, the individual voices finding each other again, the chord reforming around Vesh's voice like water around a stone. The synchronization held. Barely. The displacement events resumed.

One operator did not recover.

The medical frogs found her at the edge of the eastern pool. She had been part of the second ring, a support operator whose job was to maintain the synchronization boundary while the primary operators handled the displacement fold. She had given more than her boundary required. Her colors had gone flat, not the controlled blankness of Talla's silence but the absence of signal that meant the system generating the signal had stopped. The medical frogs carried her out of the pool with the practiced movements of people who had done this before and would do it again. They laid her on wet stone near the medical station. They worked on her for several minutes. They stopped.

The operation did not pause.

The chord continued. The displacement events continued. The crowd at the perimeter did not react with shock or grief or ceremony. A few frogs near the medical station turned their heads. A support crew member carrying water to the eastern platform stopped for three seconds, looked at the still body on the stone, and continued walking. Somewhere in the support crowd, a voice said something in Threnn-song that Reed did not understand, and several frogs near the voice shifted their colors briefly, a shared flicker of something that was not mourning, because mourning would come later, in the pool, with the ritual. Now was not the time. Now was the Pruning.

Krrik recalibrated.

"The second wave goes out with the revised parameters," he said. He was speaking to Reed and the Siberian engineer and the junior engineers and the relay frogs and possibly the computation arrays themselves, because Krrik did not distinguish between audiences when the work was happening. "The growth compensation in the eastern sectors follows Reed's surface gradient. The canopy stress data shows the expansion front is ninety meters ahead of our subsurface projections. We recalibrate from the surface data, not the underground model. The underground model is wrong. Reed's data is less wrong. Use it."

The Siberian engineer, who had been watching the eastern collapse with her arms folded and her colors dark, said nothing. She moved to the secondary array and began adjusting the parameters herself, working alongside Reed with a competence that did not require agreement or friendship, only the shared understanding that the planet was doing something the models could not predict and the only available data that captured it was the clumsy canopy measurements of a thirty-four-year-old human with smudged glasses.

The second wave went out.

Reed fed his latest canopy readings into the array, his fingers clumsy on the stone input surface that Krrik's engineers had configured for human hands three days ago. The configuration was imperfect. The stone was wet. His glasses kept sliding. He pushed them up and kept working, because the alternative was to stand here and be useless, and Reed had never been good at useless.

The arrays processed. Reed watched them. The displacement events at the six platforms resumed in staggered sequence, each fold calibrated against the revised growth projections, each operator group working at the edge of their synchronization capacity. The voss growth in the eastern sectors did not stop. It slowed. The compensation rate dropped from twelve percent to seven, then to four. The modulation compounds, reformulated overnight, held in the newly displaced zones for longer than the previous batch, buying time that was measured in hours, not days, but hours were what they had.

The chord held. The operators held. The colors sweeping through the staging grounds settled into a rhythm that was not triumph and not failure but endurance, the sustained effort of a system that had been built for exactly this and was performing exactly as designed, at exactly the cost it had always required.

Reed stood at the modulation station and watched a civilization do what it had done for two hundred million years. The frogs did not need him. They had needed one variable his science happened to capture, a set of surface observations made by a human whose entire species' understanding of biology was, by the frogs' standards, a promising first draft. His data was in the arrays now, integrated, useful, and already being superseded by the frogs' own measurements as the operation continued. He had contributed a piece. The puzzle was planetary, and the frogs were solving it as they had always solved it: collectively, at cost, with the resigned competence of a species that had buried its dead after every Pruning since before the dinosaurs and would bury this one too and then return to arguing about fermented flies.

The operator's body lay on wet stone near the medical station. Nobody had covered her. Nobody had moved her. She would be moved when the operation permitted. She would be mourned when the mourning could begin. She would dissolve into the pool and her final chemical signal would pass through the living and Pem would receive the imprint and the archive would preserve what the archive chose to preserve, and the community would say wet in, wet out, and the breathing circles would continue, and the next Pruning would come in another generation, and there would be another operator on the stone, and the chord would hold because it had always held, because holding was what the chord was for.

Reed pushed his glasses up his nose. His hands were shaking. He could not tell if it was fear or exhaustion or something closer to reverence, the involuntary response of a body confronted with a thing too large and too old and too costly to process through any channel except awe.

The arrays hummed. The chord held. The operation ground forward.

---

# Chapter 24: The Cost

The recovery pools smelled of copper and crushed herbs and something underneath both, a sweetness Quill could not name, thick and biological, the smell of bodies mending in warm water. The pools were shallow, wider than the regular bathing pools, lined with stone that had been worn smooth by use that predated human agriculture. Dozens of frogs occupied them. Some floated with their eyes closed, their colors dim, their breathing visible in the slow rise and fall of their sides. Others sat at the edges with their legs in the water, their skins flickering in low, irregular patterns that carried no information Quill could read. Partners sat with operators. Friends sat with friends. Medical frogs moved between them carrying stone vessels of something that smelled sharp and herbal, pouring it into the water at intervals that seemed practiced.

Vesh was in the nearest pool.

He moved between the operators with the unhurried attention of someone doing a job he had done before. He sat with each one. He sang, low, in Threnn-song, the sound more vibration than melody, something Quill caught in his chest and the backs of his teeth. He placed his hands on their skin in a gesture Quill could not quite follow and that Vesh did not explain. His emerald green was steady, his gold flecks subdued, the gaudy brightness of his spiritual performances replaced by something quieter and more functional. He was not interpreting the Wet Way. He was doing it. The way a doctor does medicine. The way a plumber does pipes. The body, the hands, the repetition.

A young male near the pool's eastern edge opened his eyes when Vesh reached him. His colors flickered twice, a green so pale it was nearly white, and his mouth moved. Vesh leaned closer. The young male said something Quill could not hear. Vesh said something back. The young male's colors steadied, deepened by a fraction, and his eyes closed again. Vesh moved to the next one.

Marsh was there.

She knelt at the far end of the pool with a stone basin of treated water, helping a medical frog rinse instruments. A disoriented operator had been brought to the pool's edge minutes before Quill arrived, a small brown female whose colors scattered randomly, not communication but static, the visual equivalent of a radio between stations. The operator did not recognize Marsh. She flinched from the medical frog's touch but settled when Marsh put a hand on the wet stone beside her, not touching, just present, a large warm shape that the operator's damaged senses could orient against. Marsh's field notebook was in her back pocket. She was not reaching for it.

Quill watched Marsh steady the operator and thought: she has crossed. The line between studying and belonging. She had crossed it without announcement, without decision, the way water crosses a membrane. He recognized it because he had been watching it happen to himself, in slower motion, through a different door.

Three more operators were brought in while Quill stood at the pool's edge. Their partners followed. One partner, a compact olive female, took position at the waterline and did not move. Her colors were controlled to the point of blankness, the determined stillness of someone holding a shape against pressure.
